Dialectic -- Jadal

The Principle of Not Requesting Proof for the Absolutely Known in Dialectical Debate
Author states the principle that one cannot request proof for the absolutely known, with a nuance on commitment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a request for proof (dalil) is permissible in a jadal exchange, and explain how the commitment to correctness (iltizam al-sihha) changes the status of a transmitted statement.

Core Idea
What is absolutely known cannot be challenged; only when seeking another path of knowledge may proof be requested.

In dialectical debate (jadal), not every request for proof is legitimate. The scholars of mantiq established a key principle: if a matter is known with absolute certainty, you cannot demand correction, reminder, or proof of it—unless your aim is to know it through a different avenue. This prevents futile objections and keeps debates focused on genuine points of contention. However, the passage also introduces a nuance: when a transmitter commits to the correctness of a transmitted statement, he becomes responsible, and objection (man') can then be directed at him. This redefines man' as a request for proof from the one committed to correctness, not from the premise itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Find the sentence that states it is not permissible to request correction, reminder, or proof for the absolutely known. This is the default rule.
2
Note the exception
The passage says 'unless seeking another way of knowing.' When is it allowed to request proof for an absolutely known matter? Only when the goal is to know it through a different approach.
3
Recognize the shift with commitment
The author discusses that if a transmitter is committed to the correctness of a statement (iltizam al-sihha), then he is no longer a mere narrator; his speech becomes a commitment. In that case, objection (man') can be directed at him.
4
Understand the redefinition of man'
The footnote explains that some scholars redefine man' as a request for proof from the person who has committed to the correctness of the statement, not a request for proof for a specific premise. This allows objection to be directed at the claim itself.
5
Apply the principle to a real debate scenario
Consider a debate where one party asserts 'The world is temporally originated.' If this is taken as absolutely known by the opponent, no proof can be requested. But if the opponent commits to its correctness, then man' can be used to demand proof from him.
This example is from kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جَرَتْ كَلِمَتُهُم عَلَى أَنَّهُ لا يجُوزُ طَلَبُ التَّصْحيح والتنبيه والدليل عَلَى المَعْلُوم مُطلقا ، وأنَّ ذلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنْ المَقْصُودُ معلُومِيَّتُه بطريق آخر . وأما إذا التزم صحته فمن حيث الالتزام ليس بناقل، وكلامه ليس بنقل بهذا الاعتبار ، فيتوجه عليه المنع . قال قدس سره - فيما نقل عنه - : وأنت خبير بأن هذا القول منه يدل على أن تفسير المقدمة بما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل غير مسلم عنده . تم كلامه ، وجه الدلالة أن المنقول - بعد كونه ملتزم الصحة – ليس مما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، مع أنه يجوز ورود المنع عليه ، ولا يخفى عليك أنه إنما يدل على ذلك إذا فسر المنع بطلب الدليل على المقدمة، وأما إذا فسر بطلب الدليل على ملتزم الصحة فلا ، نعم يرد عليه - حينئذ - أن يمنع المدعى أيضا حقيقة، ولا بعد في التزامه (۱) ( وقد جرت كلمتهم ) أي النظار على أنه ) أي الشأن و لا يجوز طلب التصحيح ) عند النقل ( والتنبيه ) عند دعوى الأمر البديهي الغير الأولى ( والدليل ) عند دعوى الأمر النظري على المعلوم مطلقا ، من غير تقييد بما إذا لم يكن المقصود معلوميته بوجه آخر (٢) . (۲) (۱) لقد ذكر المصنف فيما تقدم أن المنع لا يرد على المنقول ولا على الدعوى . وإذا وجه عليهما كان ذلك على ضرب من التأويل والدعوى . ولم يكن المنع واردا عليهما حقيقة بل مجازا. وهنا أراد المصنف أن يقول . إن بعض أهل فن الأدب والمناظرة أجاز توجه المنع على المنقول الذي التزم الناقل صحته . وحينئذ يكون معنى المنع كما قال الشارح . هو طلب الدليل من ملتزم الصحة . لا طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة وبناء على هذا يمكن أن يرد المنع كذلك على الدعوى . لأن صاحبها ملتزم صحتها لأن المنع هو طلب الدليل من ملتزم الصحة . لا طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة . (۲) متى يجوز طلب التصحيح عند النقل ، والتنبيه عند الخفاء، والدليل عند الجهل بالمدعي ؟ يجوز كل هذا إذا لم يكن المنقول أو المدعى الخفي أو المدعى المجهول معلوما مطلقا . أما إذا كان معلوما مطلقا فلا يصح طلب شيء إلا إذا أريد معرفته من طريق آخر فإنه يجوز حينئذ . وهذا معنى قوله . على المعلوم مطلقا أي من غير تقييد بمعرفته من طريق آخر.
Their custom has proceeded that it is not permissible to request correction, reminder, or proof for the absolutely known, and that that is not the intended knowledge of it by another path. And if he is committed to its correctness, then from the perspective of commitment he is not a transmitter, and his speech is not a transmission in this respect, so objection (man') is directed at it. He (may his secret be sanctified) said - in what was transmitted from him: And you are aware that this statement of his indicates that the interpretation of the premise as that upon which the correctness of the proof depends is not accepted by him. His words ended. The indication is that the transmitted statement - after being committed to correctness - is not something that the correctness of the proof depends on, even though it is permissible for objection to occur regarding it. And it is not hidden from you that it only indicates that if objection is interpreted as a request for proof for the premise, and if it is interpreted as a request for proof from the one committed to correctness, then not. Yes, it is then objected to it that he also truly objects to the claim, and there is no impossibility in his commitment. (1) (And their custom has proceeded) i.e., the scholars (that it) i.e., the matter (and it is not permissible to request correction) in transmission (and reminder) in claiming an obvious matter that is not primary (and proof) in claiming a theoretical matter, for the absolutely known, without restriction to when the intended knowledge of it is not by another way. (2) (1) The author previously mentioned that objection is not directed at the transmitted statement nor at the claim. And if it is directed at them, that is by way of interpretation and claim, and the objection is not truly directed at them but metaphorically. Here the author intends to say that some of the people of the art of etiquette and debate allowed the direction of objection to the transmitted statement whose transmitter is committed to its correctness. And then the meaning of objection, as the commentator said, is a request for proof from the one committed to correctness, not a request for proof for a specific premise. And based on this, objection can also be directed at the claim, because its proponent is committed to its correctness; because objection is a request for proof from the one committed to correctness, not a request for proof for a specific premise. (2) When is it permissible to request correction in transmission, and reminder in concealment, and proof in ignorance of the claimant? All this is permissible if the transmitted statement, the hidden claim, or the unknown claim is not absolutely known. But if it is absolutely known, then no request is valid unless one intends to know it from another way; then it is permissible. And this is the meaning of his saying: 'for the absolutely known,' i.e., without restriction to knowing it from another way.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage states a key principle in dialectical debate: one cannot request correction, reminder, or proof for something that is absolutely known (ma'lum mutlaq). The principle is that if a matter is already known with certainty, asking for proof or clarification is impermissible unless the goal is to attain knowledge through another path. The author then discusses a nuance: if a transmitter is committed to the correctness of a transmitted statement, then he is no longer a mere narrator; he becomes responsible, and objection can be legitimately directed at him. A quote from a previous authority (Quds Sirah) is cited to argue that such a transmitted statement, after commitment to correctness, is not something the proof depends on, yet objection is still permissible. This leads to a reinterpretation of 'objection' (man') as a request for proof from the one who has committed to correctness, not from the premise itself. The footnotes elaborate: The author had earlier said that objection is not truly directed at transmission or claim, but here he notes that some dialecticians allow it for statements whose transmitter is committed. This shifts the meaning of man' from 'request for proof for a premise' to 'request for proof from the one committed to correctness'. Thus, it becomes possible to object to a claim itself, because the claimant is committed to its truth. The second footnote explains when such requests are permissible: they are allowed if the matter is not absolutely known; if it is absolutely known, they are invalid unless seeking another avenue of knowledge.

Key Terms
المعلوم مطلقا
منع
التزام الصحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in the passage, when is it permissible to request proof (dalil) for a claim?
A) Always, because every claim needs proof.
B) Only if the claim is about a theoretical matter and is not absolutely known.
C) Only if the opponent is committed to the correctness of the claim.
D) Never, because proof is only for premises.

Key Takeaway
The absolutely known cannot be challenged in debate; only when seeking a different path of knowledge may proof be requested, and commitment to correctness shifts the meaning of objection from a challenge to the premise to a challenge to the person's commitment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a jadal session between two scholars on the ruling of riba, one scholar asserts that 'Riba is forbidden' as an absolutely known matter (from the Qur'an and consensus). The opponent wants to request proof for this claim. According to the principle from this lesson, is the request permissible? Justify your answer.
?
Consider a transmitter who says 'I heard from the Prophet that prayer is obligatory.' He then declares his commitment to its correctness. How does the redefinition of man' apply to this situation? What can the opponent now request?

When Can You Request Proof in a Debate?
Commentary on the permissibility of requesting proof for known matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a dialectical request for correction, alert, or proof is permissible and identify the two interpretations of man' (objection) in jadal.

Core Idea
You cannot demand proof for the obvious, unless you want to know it through another route.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), there are specific rules about what a debater may request from an opponent. One cannot simply demand a proof or correction for a known matter without a valid reason. This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary explains the standard position and its exceptions, as well as the nuanced interpretation of the objection (man') that applies to a committed statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of requests
Find the terms tasbih (correction), tanbih (alert), and dalil (proof) in the Arabic. Understand what each corresponds to in a debate: correction for transmitted statements, alert for self-evident matters, and proof for theoretical claims.
2
Understand the general rule
The rule states that these requests are not permissible 'upon the known absolutely' (ala al-ma'lum mutlaqan). Why? Because requesting proof for something already known implies ignorance or frivolity, which disrupts productive debate.
The exception is when the seeker intends to know the matter from another path.
3
Examine the objection (man')
The passage discusses two interpretations of man': (1) requesting proof for the premise (muqaddima), and (2) requesting proof from the one committed to correctness (multazim al-sihha). Analyze how each interpretation affects the permissibility of the objection.
The commentator argues that only the first interpretation aligns with the rule.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical debate
Take a claim that is known (e.g., 'The Quran is the word of God'). Can the opponent request a proof? Only if he wants to know it through a different argument (e.g., from historical transmission swerve). This illustrates the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جرت كلمتهم على أنه لا يجوز طلب التصحيح والتنبيه والدليل على المعلوم مطلقا، وأن ذلك لم يكن المقصود معلوميته بطريق آخر. وأما إذا التزم صحته فمن حيث الالتزام ليس بناقل، وكلامه ليس بنقل بهذا الاعتبار، فيتوجه عليه المنع. قال قدس سره - فيما نقل عنه - : وأنت خبير بأن هذا القول منه يدل على أن تفسير المقدمة بما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل غير مسلم عنده. تم كلامه، وجه الدلالة أن المنقول - بعد كونه ملتزم الصحة – ليس مما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، مع أنه يجوز ورود المنع عليه، ولا يخفى عليك أنه إنما يدل على ذلك إذا فسر المنع بطلب الدليل على المقدمة، وأما إذا فسر بطلب الدليل على ملتزم الصحة فلا، نعم يرد عليه - حينئذ - أن يمنع المدعى أيضا حقيقة، ولا بعد في التزامه. (وقد جرت كلمتهم) أي النظار على أنه أي الشأن ولا يجوز طلب التصحيح عند النقل والتنبيه عند دعوى الأمر البديهي الغير الأولى والدليل عند دعوى الأمر النظري على المعلوم مطلقا، من غير تقييد بما إذا لم يكن المقصود معلوميته بوجه آخر. ولا يلزم من بطلان الدليل بطلان المدلول. الجواز أن يكون المدلول واحدا لدلال شتى، فبطلان واحد منها لا يبطله، فإذا بطل الدليل فلا منصب للمعلل سوى التغيير والتبديل.
Their statement runs that it is not permissible to request correction, alerting, and proof upon the known absolutely, and that that was not intended for its being known by another path. But if one commits to its correctness, then from the perspective of commitment it is not a transmission, and his speech is not a transmission with this consideration, so objection applies to it. He (may his soul be sanctified) said - in what was transmitted from him: And you are aware that this statement of his indicates that interpreting the premise as that upon which the correctness of the proof depends is not accepted by him. End of his speech. The indication is that the transmitted - after being committed to correctness - is not among that upon which the correctness of the proof depends, while it is permissible for objection to be directed at it. And it is not hidden from you that it indicates that only if objection is interpreted as requesting proof for the premise; but if it is interpreted as requesting proof from the one committed to correctness, then no. Yes, it is objected to him - then - that he also denies the claim in reality, and there is no impossibility in his commitment. (And their statement runs) i.e., the debaters, that it (i.e., the matter) and it is not permissible to request correction upon transmission, and alerting upon claiming a non-primary self-evident matter, and proof upon claiming a theoretical matter upon the known absolutely, without restriction to when the intended is not its being known in another way. And the falsehood of the proof does not necessitate the falsehood of the proven. The permissibility is that the proven may be one for multiple proofs, so the falsehood of one of them does not falsify it. So if the proof becomes false, the only role for the proponent is to change and replace.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the Rashidiyya deals with two interrelated rules of dialectical argumentation (jadal). First, it discusses the permissibility of requesting correction (tasbih), alerting (tanbih), or proof (dalil) regarding a matter that is already known (ma‘lum). The standard position (expressed as 'their statement runs') is that such requests are not allowed absolutely. However, an exception is made when the seeker intends to know the matter through another path. The commentator explains that if a person commits to the correctness of a transmitted statement, then from the perspective of that commitment it is not considered a mere transmission, and thus an objection (man‘) may be directed at it. The commentator then cites the original author (quddisa sirruh) to clarify that interpreting the premise as that upon which the correctness of the proof depends is not accepted, because the transmitted statement, even if committed to, is not a premise that the proof depends on. The objection, if interpreted as requesting proof for the premise, is allowed; but if interpreted as requesting proof from the one committed to correctness, it is not. Second, the passage asserts a well-known logical principle: the falsehood of a proof (d alil) does not entail the falsehood of the proven (madlul). This is because a single conclusion may have multiple proofs; if one proof is invalidated, the conclusion may still be supported by others. Thus, when a proof is refuted, the proponent's duty is simply to replace it with another. The commentator emphasizes that this principle allows for the possibility of multiple independent arguments for the same claim, a common feature in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Key Terms
منع
تصحيح
تنبيه
دليل
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is it permissible to request a proof (dalil) for a known matter?
A) Always, because every claim needs proof.
B) Never, because the matter is already known.
C) Only when the seeker wants to know it through another path.
D) Only if the opponent is known to be lying.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, you cannot demand proof for the obvious; but if you seek a different perspective, you may.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a known proposition in fiqh: 'Alcohol is forbidden.' Is it permissible in a debate to request a proof (dalil) for this? Explain why or why not, citing the exception if applicable.
?
In a jadal session, your opponent transmits a hadith and commits to its correctness. You object (man') by saying 'Prove that the hadith is authentic'. Is this a valid objection according to the two interpretations of man'? Discuss.

One Conclusion, Many Proofs: False Proof Does Not Falsify the Proven
Commentary on the principle that false proof does not falsify the proven

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that the falsity of a proof does not entail the falsity of the conclusion, and explain why the proponent must replace a refuted proof with another.

Core Idea
A robust conclusion can survive a thousand invalid proofs.

In theological and legal debates, it is common for opponents to attack a proof. A beginner might think that if the proof falls, the conclusion falls. This passage from the Rashidiyya decisively refutes that notion. It establishes that a single conclusion may have multiple proofs, so refuting one does not refute the conclusion. The proponent's only duty is to change to another proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Locate the statement 'Wa la yalzamu min butlan al-dalil butlan al-madlul' in the Arabic. Write it down and note its literal meaning: 'And the falsehood of the proof does not necessitate the falsehood of the proven.'
2
Understand the rationale
The text says 'jawaz an yakun al-madlul wahid li dalalat shatta' – it is possible for the proven to be one for multiple proofs. Explain why this implies that falsehood of one proof does not invalidate the conclusion.
If the conclusion has other valid proofs, it remains true.
3
Apply to a concrete case
Consider the theological claim 'The Quran is the word of God'. One proof is its linguistic inimitability; another is the prophethood of Muhammad. If someone refutes the inimitability argument, does the claim become false? According to the principle, no.
The proponent can then rely on the other proof.
4
Know the proponent's duty
When his proof is refuted, what should the proponent do? The text says 'taghyir wa tabdil' – change and replace. He must bring a different proof for the same claim.
He does not need to concede the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جرت كلمتهم على أنه لا يجوز طلب التصحيح والتنبيه والدليل على المعلوم مطلقا، وأن ذلك لم يكن المقصود معلوميته بطريق آخر. وأما إذا التزم صحته فمن حيث الالتزام ليس بناقل، وكلامه ليس بنقل بهذا الاعتبار، فيتوجه عليه المنع. قال قدس سره - فيما نقل عنه - : وأنت خبير بأن هذا القول منه يدل على أن تفسير المقدمة بما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل غير مسلم عنده. تم كلامه، وجه الدلالة أن المنقول - بعد كونه ملتزم الصحة – ليس مما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، مع أنه يجوز ورود المنع عليه، ولا يخفى عليك أنه إنما يدل على ذلك إذا فسر المنع بطلب الدليل على المقدمة، وأما إذا فسر بطلب الدليل على ملتزم الصحة فلا، نعم يرد عليه - حينئذ - أن يمنع المدعى أيضا حقيقة، ولا بعد في التزامه. (وقد جرت كلمتهم) أي النظار على أنه أي الشأن ولا يجوز طلب التصحيح عند النقل والتنبيه عند دعوى الأمر البديهي الغير الأولى والدليل عند دعوى الأمر النظري على المعلوم مطلقا، من غير تقييد بما إذا لم يكن المقصود معلوميته بوجه آخر. ولا يلزم من بطلان الدليل بطلان المدلول. الجواز أن يكون المدلول واحدا لدلال شتى، فبطلان واحد منها لا يبطله، فإذا بطل الدليل فلا منصب للمعلل سوى التغيير والتبديل.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the Rashidiyya deals with two interrelated rules of dialectical argumentation (jadal). First, it discusses the permissibility of requesting correction (tasbih), alerting (tanbih), or proof (dalil) regarding a matter that is already known (ma‘lum). The standard position (expressed as 'their statement runs') is that such requests are not allowed absolutely. However, an exception is made when the seeker intends to know the matter through another path. The commentator explains that if a person commits to the correctness of a transmitted statement, then from the perspective of that commitment it is not considered a mere transmission, and thus an objection (man‘) may be directed at it. The commentator then cites the original author (quddisa sirruh) to clarify that interpreting the premise as that upon which the correctness of the proof depends is not accepted, because the transmitted statement, even if committed to, is not a premise that the proof depends on. The objection, if interpreted as requesting proof for the premise, is allowed; but if interpreted as requesting proof from the one committed to correctness, it is not. Second, the passage asserts a well-known logical principle: the falsehood of a proof (d alil) does not entail the falsehood of the proven (madlul). This is because a single conclusion may have multiple proofs; if one proof is invalidated, the conclusion may still be supported by others. Thus, when a proof is refuted, the proponent's duty is simply to replace it with another. The commentator emphasizes that this principle allows for the possibility of multiple independent arguments for the same claim, a common feature in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only role (man sab) of the proponent when his proof is proven false?
A) Accept that his claim is false and concede.
B) Change and replace the proof.
C) Deny the opponent's objection without justification.
D) Request correction from the opponent.

Key Takeaway
The falsehood of one proof does not falsify the proven; multiple independent proofs can support the same conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fiqh ruling that 'riba is haram'. One proof is the explicit Quranic verse; another is the Sunna. If someone argues that the hadith is weak, what should the proponent do according to the principle? Does the ruling become invalid?
?
Identify a real-world objection to a theological proof (e.g., the kalam cosmological argument). How would you apply this principle to defend the conclusion even if that proof is challenged?

intermediate
8 min
The Principle: Invalid Proof Does Not Invalidate Conclusion
Author states principle with commentary on multiple proofs and objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a false proof does not refute a conclusion and apply this principle in dialectical exchanges.

Core Idea
Invalidity of a proof does not imply invalidity of the conclusion; truth may be supported by multiple proofs.

In dialectical debates (munāẓara), it is a common mistake to think that defeating a proof defeats the conclusion. The author of this text clarifies that a conclusion (madlūl) may be supported by several proofs, so the failure of one leaves the conclusion intact. This lesson examines the logical and strategic implications of this principle for both the challenger and the defender.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish proof from conclusion
Identify the two key terms: dalīl (proof) and madlūl (conclusion). Understand that they are logically independent: a true conclusion can have a bad proof, and a false proof does not make the conclusion false.
This independence is the basis of the principle.
2
Understand multiple proofs
The commentator explains that a single conclusion may have multiple indications (adilla). Thus, if one proof fails, the conclusion still stands and can be supported by another proof.
This is a common situation in Islamic sciences where many rulings have multiple evidences.
3
Apply in a dialectical scenario
If a challenger (sā'il) invalidates the defender's (mu'allil) proof, the defender should not concede the conclusion. Instead, he may substitute the invalid proof with a different valid one that yields the same conclusion.
This is the practical takeaway for munāẓara.
4
Analyze the objection about final cause
An objector argues that allowing multiple proofs for the same known thing would imply multiple final causes (ghāya) for debate, which is invalid. The commentator responds that increasing certainty (ziyādat al-īqān) does not depart from the true purpose of debate: manifesting truth (iẓhār al-ṣawāb).
Thus, seeking alternative proofs is legitimate even if the conclusion is already known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم من بطلان الدليل بطلان المدلول . ثم عطف على قوله يستبان قوله : ولا يلزم من بطلان الدليل بطلان المدلول الجواز أن يكون المدلول واحدا لدلال شتى ، فبطلان واحد منها لا يبطله ، فإذا بطل الدليل فلا منصب للمعلل سوى التغيير والتبديل . أورد على جواز توجه طلب التصحيح والتنبيه والدليل على المعلوم مطلقا إذا أريد معلوميته بطريق آخر أن على تعدد العلة الغائية للمناظرة لكن تعدد العلة الغائية للمناظرة باطل فبطل ما أدى عليه ، وإذن يصح طلب التصحيح والتنبيه والدليل على المعلوم مطلقا ولو أريد معلوميته من طريق آخر. ولقد أجاب الشارح عن هذا بأن زيادة الإيقان والعلم لا يخرج عن إظهار الصواب. والمناظرة تكون لإظهار الصواب . وعلى هذا يجوز طلب التصحيح والتنبيه والدليل على المعلوم مطلقا إذا أريد معلوميته من طريق آخر . يريد أن يقول المصنف إذا بطل السائل دليل المعلل فإنه لا لزم من هذا إبطال مدلوله الجواز أن يكون للمدلول الواحد أدلة متعددة. وعلى ذلك يكون منصب المعلل عند إبطال الدليل أن يغير دليله ويبدله بدليل آخر ينتج المطلوب
And it is not necessary that the invalidity of the proof necessitates the invalidity of the proven. Then he conjoined his saying 'it becomes clear' with his saying: 'And it is not necessary that the invalidity of the proof necessitates the invalidity of the proven' because it is possible that the proven is one with multiple indications, so the invalidity of one of them does not invalidate it. So if the proof is invalid, there is no position for the one being argued against except change and substitution. An objection was raised against the permissibility of directing the request for correction, notification, and proof of the known absolutely if its being known is intended through another path: that it is based on the multiplicity of the final cause of the debate, but the multiplicity of the final cause of the debate is invalid, so what it led to is invalidated. Therefore, the request for correction, notification, and proof of the known is valid absolutely even if its being known is intended from another path. And the commentator answered this by saying that increasing certainty and knowledge does not exit from manifesting what is correct. And debate is for manifesting what is correct. And on this, it is permissible to request correction, notification, and proof of the known absolutely if its being known is intended from another path. He (the author) intends to say that if the challenger invalidates the proof of the one being argued against, it does not necessitate the invalidation of its proven because it is possible that the single proven has multiple proofs. And on that, the position of the one being argued against upon invalidation of the proof is to change his proof and replace it with another proof that produces the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage establishes a key dialectical principle: the falsity of a particular proof does not entail the falsity of the conclusion it attempts to prove. The commentator expands on the author's statement by noting that a single proposition (المدلول) may be supported by multiple proofs (أدلة). Thus, if one proof fails, the conclusion is not thereby refuted, and the defender may simply replace the invalid proof with another. The passage then addresses an objection: if a thing is already known, why allow seeking proof for it? The objection claims this would imply multiple final causes for debate, which is invalid. The commentator answers that increasing certainty (زيادة الإيقان) does not contradict the aim of debate, which is to manifest truth (إظهار الصواب). Thus, seeking proof for known things is permissible when one intends to know them through a different path. The underlying lesson is that the purpose of dialectic (مناظرة) is not only to establish new knowledge but also to strengthen certainty and clarify truth through alternative proofs.

When is Objecting to a Premise Permissible in Dialectic?
Author discusses conditions for permissibility of manʿ in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of premises can be validly objected to (manʿ) in a dialectical exchange and distinguish legitimate objections from obstinacy (mukābara).

Core Idea
Objecting to the obvious is obstinacy; objecting to the hidden or the unstated is a request for clarification.

In dialectic (jadal), the move called manʿ (objection to a premise) is a powerful tool, but it has limits. A common misconception is that you can only object to premises that are explicit parts of the proof. This passage clarifies that you may also object to implicit premises upon which the discourse relies. However, objecting to something that is absolutely known is mere stubbornness (mukābara) and not permitted. The exception is when the premise is 'hidden' (khafī) or is a premise of notification (tanbīh), where the objection is actually a request for clarification or proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule being established
Find the opening statement: 'منع مقدمة معينة أو أكثر صريحة أو ضمنية يكون بناء الكلام عليه جائز'. This is the core rule.
2
Recognize the misconception being addressed
The author says 'إيراد هذا الكلام لدفع توهم أنه لا يجوز لأن تلك المقدمة ليست بجزء الدليل'. The misconception is that only premises that are formal parts of the proof can be objected to.
The author refutes this by expanding the definition of 'premise'.
3
Distinguish explicit vs. implicit premises
The passage explicitly mentions both صريحة (explicit) and ضمنية (implicit) premises. The latter are not stated but are necessary for the discourse to be coherent.
Think of implicit premises as unstated assumptions.
4
Contrast valid objection and obstinacy
Objecting to the absolutely known (معلوم مطلقا) is مكابرة (obstinacy) and is not to be entertained. The two exceptions are الخفي (the hidden) and مقدمة التنبيه (premise of notification).
The hidden is a self-evident matter with some obscurity; objection here is a request for clarification.
5
Understand the real meaning of manʿ
Manʿ in reality is a request for proof (طلب الدليل) of a specific premise. The exceptions are permissible figuratively because they are particular instances of this general request.
This unifying principle explains why the exceptions are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منع مقدمة معينة أو أكثر صريحة أو ضمنية يكون بناء الكلام عليه جائز . إيراد هذا الكلام لدفع توهم أنه لا يجوز لأن تلك المقدمة ليست بجزء الدليل، والمشهور أن المقدمة جزء الدليل، وإنما يجوز، لأن المقدمة على ما مر تفسيره أعم من جزء الدليل. ومنع المعلوم مطلقا أي من كل وجه مكابرة لا تسمع دون منع الخفي أي البديهي الذي فيه خفاء ودون منع مقدمة التنبيه فإنه أي كل واحد من منع البديهي بمعنى طلب التنبيه عليه، ومنع مقدمة التنبيه بمعنى طلب الدليل، أو التنبيه عليها ويجوز تجوزا لما عرفت من أن المنع حقيقة طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة من الدليل، والعلاقة كون كل جزئيا لمطلق الطلب.
Objecting to a specific premise or more, explicit or implicit, upon which the discourse is based, is permissible. The mention of this statement is to repel the misconception that it is not permissible because that premise is not part of the proof, and the well-known is that the premise is part of the proof. Rather it is permissible because the premise, according to its earlier interpretation, is more general than part of the proof. And objecting to the known absolutely, i.e., from every aspect, is obstinacy (mukābara) and is not heard. Except objecting to the hidden (al-khafī), i.e., the self-evident that has some obscurity, and except objecting to a premise of notification (tanbīh). For each of objecting to the self-evident in the sense of requesting notification of it, and objecting to a premise of notification in the sense of requesting a proof or notification thereof, is permissible figuratively, because you know that objection in reality is a request for proof of a specific premise of the proof, and the relationship is that each is a particular instance of the absolute request.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage constitutes the fourth investigation (البحث الرابع) and defines the scope of the dialectical move called *man'* (objecting to a premise). The author states that it is permissible to object to a specific premise (or more), whether explicit (صريحة) or implicit (ضمنية), as long as the discourse is built upon it. This is to counter the assumption that *man'* is only allowed against premises that are formal parts of the proof (جزء الدليل). The commentator clarifies that the term 'premise' (مقدمة) here is broader, covering any proposition on which the argument relies, even if not explicitly listed. The passage then distinguishes between types of premises: objecting to something that is absolutely known (معلوم مطلقا) is mere obstinacy (مكابرة) and not to be entertained. However, objecting is permissible in two cases: (1) the hidden (الخفي) – a self-evident matter that has some obscurity, where the objection is actually a request for clarification (طلب التنبيه); and (2) a premise of notification (مقدمة التنبيه) – where the objection is either a request for proof or for notification. These are permissible figuratively, because the essential nature of *man'* is a request for proof of a specific premise, and these cases are particular instances of that general request.

Key Takeaway
Manʿ is permissible for any premise upon which the discourse relies, but objecting to the absolutely obvious is obstinacy; the only exceptions are premises that are 'hidden' or serve as 'notification'.

The Scope of Permissible Objection
Author defines permissibility of objecting to premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premises (explicit or implicit) can be legitimately objected to in a jadal argument, based on the broader definition of muqaddima.

Core Idea
Objection is not limited to syllogistic premises—it applies to any statement the discourse relies upon.

In dialectical debates, the objector often targets the premises of a proof. But what counts as a premise? This lesson unpacks the author's claim that any statement upon which the discourse depends—whether explicitly stated or implicitly assumed—can be objected to. You will learn to distinguish between premises that are part of a formal syllogism and those that are more foundational, such as background assumptions or common knowledge claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The author states that objecting to a specific premise—whether explicit or implicit—upon which the discourse is built is permissible. Find the Arabic phrase that expresses this.
Look for 'مَنْعُ مُقدِّمَةٍ مُعيَّنَةٍ أو أكثر صريحة أو ضمنيَّةٍ يَكُونُ بِناءُ الكَلامِ عَليْهِ جائز'.
2
Understand the broader definition of 'premise'
The author clarifies that 'premise' here is more general than just a part of a formal proof. It includes any statement the correctness of the argument depends on. Why is this distinction important?
This expansion allows objection to hidden assumptions, not just the two premises of a syllogism.
3
Apply to a sample argument
Take a kalam argument: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' Identify which premises are explicit and which might be implicit (e.g., the principle that change implies temporality).
The explicit premises are the first two; the implicit premise might be 'Change is evidence of temporality'.
4
Test the rule
Suppose an opponent objects to the implicit premise 'Change implies temporality'. According to the author, is this objection permissible? Why?
Yes, because it is a premise upon which the proof depends, even if not stated.

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of legitimate objection (manʿ) in dialectical argumentation. The author begins by stating that objecting to a specific premise—whether explicit or implicit—upon which the discourse is built is permissible. This is significant because the common understanding is that objection only applies to premises that are parts of a formal proof. The author clarifies that the term 'premise' here is broader: it includes any statement on which the reasoning depends, even if it is not part of a strict syllogism. For example, in a kalam argument like 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated,' objecting to either premise is standard. But the author extends permissibility to implicit premises, such as an unstated assumption that 'change implies temporality.' He then addresses objecting to known matters (maʿlūm). If something is absolutely known (e.g., 'the whole is greater than its part'), objecting to it is obstinacy (mukābara) and is not entertained. However, two exceptions are allowed: first, objecting to a hidden self-evident (al-khafī) that is not immediately obvious—for instance, a subtle implication in a ḥadīth text that requires reflection; second, objecting to a premise that serves as a reminder (tanbīh), which is permissible metaphorically, since the true intent is to request a proof or clarification, not to deny the obvious. Thus, the author refines the rules of objections, balancing between preventing frivolous denial and allowing challenges to genuinely unclear points.

Key Takeaway
Objection is permissible for any premise—explicit or implicit—upon which the argument's correctness depends.

When Objection is Not Allowed: Known, Hidden, and Hint Premises
Author defines permissibility of objecting to premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between obstinate denial (mukābara) of absolutely known premises and legitimate objection to hidden or reminder premises.

Core Idea
Objecting to self-evident truths is obstinacy, but requesting clarification on hidden or reminder premises is acceptable.

While objecting to premises is generally permissible, the author introduces crucial exceptions: premises that are absolutely known (e.g., mathematical or logical axioms) cannot be challenged without falling into obstinacy. However, there are two allowances: 'hidden' self-evident premises that require reflection, and 'reminder' premises that serve as prompts for self-evident truths. This lesson sharpens your ability to recognize when an objection is frivolous and when it is a valid request for clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for absolutely known premises
The author states: 'وَمَنْعُ الْمَعْلُومِ مُطْلَقًا مُكَابَرَةٌ' — objecting to what is known from every aspect is obstinacy and not heard. Find this phrase and understand its implication.
This refers to axioms like 'the whole is greater than its part' or 'existence cannot be both affirmed and denied simultaneously'.
2
Understand the exception for 'hidden' self-evident premises
The author allows objection to 'al-khafī'—the self-evident that has some hiddenness. What does this mean? Give an example from Islamic theology.
Example: The statement 'the Creator must be one' might be self-evident to a Muslim but not immediately obvious to a polytheist; thus, requesting a proof is not obstinacy.
3
Understand the exception for 'reminder' premises (tanbīh)
Objecting to a premise that serves as a reminder is permissible metaphorically. Why metaphorical? Because the true intent is to request a proof or a reminder, not to deny the premise itself.
For instance, objecting to 'the Prophet is the seal of the prophets' by asking for a proof is a request for a reminder, not a denial.
4
Apply to a scenario
In a debate, one debater says: 'Opposing the consensus of the companions is impermissible.' The opponent objects: 'I need a proof that consensus is a source of law.' Is this objection obstinate or permissible? Categorize the premise.
This premise may be known to some but not all; it could be considered hidden or a reminder, depending on context.

مَنْعُ مُقدِّمَةٍ مُعيَّنَةٍ أو أكثر صريحة أو ضمنيَّةٍ يَكُونُ بِناءُ الكَلامِ عَليْهِ جائز .
منع) البحث الرابع
مقدمة معينة ) من الدليل ( أو أكثر، وحينئذ يكون أكثر من منع واحد صريحة ) صفة مقدمة ، أو خبر كان المحذوف أو ضمنية يكون بناء الكلام عليه ) صفة مقدمة أو أكثر، وتذكير الضمير إما باعتبار لفظ أكثر ، أو بتأويل كل واحد منهما ، أو بالنظر إلى أن المقدمة عبارة عما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل ( جائز ) خبر قوله - منع - وإيراد هذا الكلام لدفع توهم أنه لا يجوز لأن تلك المقدمة ليست بجزء الدليل، والمشهور أن المقدمة جزء الدليل، وإنما يجوز، لأن المقدمة - على ما مر تفسيره - أعم من جزء الدليل (۱) ( ومنع المعلوم مطلقا أي من كل ، وجه ( مكابرة ) لا تسمع ( دون ) منع ( الخفي ) أي البديهي الذي فيه خفاء ( و ) دون منع مقدمة التنبيه فإنه أي كل واحد من منع البديهي بمعنى طلب التنبيه عليه ، ومنع مقدمة التنبيه بمعنى طلب الدليل، أو التنبيه عليها و يجوز تجوزا » لما عرفت من أن المنع حقيقة طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة من الدليل، والعلاقة كون كل جزئيا لمطلق الطلب (٢) .
ومَنْعُ المَعْلومِ مُطلقًا مُكابَرَةٌ ، دُونَ الخَفيّ ، ومَنْعُ مُقَدِّمَةِ التَّنبيه فإِنَّهُ يَجُوزُ تَجوزا
The fourth research: Objecting to a specific premise or more, explicit or implicit, upon which the discourse is based, is permissible. (Objecting) The fourth research: A specific premise from the proof, or more, and then it is more than one objection, explicit as a description of the premise, or the predicate of the omitted kāna, or implicit, upon which the discourse is based as a description of the premise or more, and the masculine pronoun is either by considering the wording of 'more' or by interpreting each one, or by considering that the premise is what the correctness of the proof depends on. (Permissible) is the predicate of His statement - objection - and the mention of this statement is to repel the misconception that it is not permissible because that premise is not part of the proof, while the well-known view is that the premise is part of the proof. Rather, it is permissible because the premise, according to the earlier interpretation, is more general than a part of the proof. (1) And objecting to what is known absolutely, i.e., from every aspect, is obstinacy, not to be heard. Except for objecting to the hidden, i.e., the self-evident that has some hiddenness, and except for objecting to a premise of reminder, for each of objecting to the self-evident in the sense of requesting a reminder of it, and objecting to a premise of reminder in the sense of requesting a proof or a reminder of it, is permissible metaphorically because you know that objection in reality is requesting a proof for a specific premise of the proof, and the relationship is that each is a particular case of absolute request. (2) And objecting to what is known absolutely is obstinacy, except for the hidden, and objecting to a premise of reminder is permissible metaphorically.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of legitimate objection (manʿ) in dialectical argumentation. The author begins by stating that objecting to a specific premise—whether explicit or implicit—upon which the discourse is built is permissible. This is significant because the common understanding is that objection only applies to premises that are parts of a formal proof. The author clarifies that the term 'premise' here is broader: it includes any statement on which the reasoning depends, even if it is not part of a strict syllogism. For example, in a kalam argument like 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated,' objecting to either premise is standard. But the author extends permissibility to implicit premises, such as an unstated assumption that 'change implies temporality.' He then addresses objecting to known matters (maʿlūm). If something is absolutely known (e.g., 'the whole is greater than its part'), objecting to it is obstinacy (mukābara) and is not entertained. However, two exceptions are allowed: first, objecting to a hidden self-evident (al-khafī) that is not immediately obvious—for instance, a subtle implication in a ḥadīth text that requires reflection; second, objecting to a premise that serves as a reminder (tanbīh), which is permissible metaphorically, since the true intent is to request a proof or clarification, not to deny the obvious. Thus, the author refines the rules of objections, balancing between preventing frivolous denial and allowing challenges to genuinely unclear points.

مقدمة
مكابرة

Key Takeaway

Conditional Objection in Dialectic
Conditional objection in dialectic: objecting to a premise after accepting another premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid conditional objection in a dialectical exchange and identify when such objections are permissible.

Core Idea
Reject one premise only after conceding another — a dialectical tactic for precision.

In dialectic, the respondent does not have to reject every premise outright. Sometimes the most effective objection comes after accepting part of the argument. The Rashidiyya teaches how to object to a premise on the condition that another premise is accepted, both in disjunctive and non-disjunctive reasoning. This refines your ability to challenge arguments without conceding the entire case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's argument structure
Read the proponent's claim and identify the premises and conclusion. Is it a simple categorical syllogism or a disjunctive argument?
2
Determine if the argument is disjunctive or not
Disjunctive arguments use 'either...or' (tarīdāt) and often have conditional branches. Non-disjunctive arguments are straightforward syllogisms.
This determines the style of conditional objection.
3
Construct a conditional objection
Accept the premise you find strongest, then object to the other premise(s) while noting that your objection is conditional on the acceptance of the first.
For disjunctives: accept the disjunction and one conditional, then reject the other conditional. For non-disjunctives: accept the first premise, then reject the second.
4
Verify the objection is valid in dialectic
Ensure the objection targets a distinct part of the argument and does not contradict earlier concessions. The objection must be permissible under the rules of munazara.
A conditional objection is not a contradiction; it is a strategic move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَنْعُ المُقدِّمَةِ عَلَى مَنْعِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أُخْرَى عَلَى تقدير التسليم سواء كانَ المَنْعُ في الترديدات ، كما إذا قال المعلل : لا يخلو إما أن يكون هذا أو ذلك، فإن كان هذا فكذا ، وإن كان ذلك فكذلك ، فيقول السائل : لا نسلم أنه إن كان هذا فكذا ، وإن سلمناه فلا نسلم أنه إن كان ذلك فكذلك ، أو يقول بالعكس، بأن يقول : لا نسلم أنه إن كان ذلك فكذلك ، وإن سلم فلا نسلم أنه إن كان هذا فكذا (أولا) يكون فيها، كما قيل . العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث، فيقول . لا نسلم أن العالم متغير، وإن سلمنا ذلك ، لكن لا نسلم أن كل متغير حادث ، أو يقول بالعكس (۱) ولكن كون ذلك المنع على تقدير التسليم قد يكون بطريق الوجوب، كما إذا كان المنع الثاني مبنيا على تقدير التسليم، كما إذا قال التغير في العالم موجود ، فلابد من حدوثه ، فيقول . لا نسلم أن التغير في العالم موجود، وإن سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم كونه ضروري
And objecting to a premise upon objecting to another premise on the condition of acceptance, whether the objection is in disjunctives, as when the proponent says: 'It cannot be but that this is either this or that; if it is this, then thus; and if it is that, then thus.' Then the respondent says: 'We do not accept that if it is this, then thus; and if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is that, then thus.' Or he says the opposite, by saying: 'We do not accept that if it is that, then thus; and if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is this, then thus.' Or it is not in disjunctives, as it is said: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' So he says: 'We do not accept that the world is changing; and if we accept that, still we do not accept that everything changing is originated.' Or he says the opposite. (1) But that objection on the condition of acceptance may be by way of obligation, as when the second objection is based on the condition of acceptance, as when he says: 'Change in the world exists, so necessarily it is originated.' So he says: 'We do not accept that change in the world exists; and if we accept that, still we do not accept that it is necessary.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage describes a specific type of objection: objecting to a premise conditionally upon the acceptance of another premise. This occurs in two contexts: disjunctive (tarīdāt) and non-disjunctive. In disjunctive reasoning, the proponent presents an exclusive disjunction, e.g., 'Either this is A or B; if A then C; if B then D.' The respondent may object to the conditioned consequence, accepting the disjunction but rejecting one conditional. For instance, in a kalam argument about whether the world is temporally originated, the proponent might say: 'The world is either changing or not; if changing, then originated; if not changing, then eternal.' The respondent could object: 'We do not accept that if it is changing, it is originated; even if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is not changing, it is eternal.' This is valid. In non-disjunctive cases, like the classic syllogism 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated,' the respondent may first object to the first premise ('We do not accept that the world is changing'), and if that is conceded, then object to the second premise ('We do not accept that everything changing is originated'). This sequential objection is permissible because each objection is independent but the second is contingent on acceptance of the first. The author notes that sometimes the second objection is obligatory when it is built on acceptance of the first, as in a case where the conclusion necessarily follows from the first premise—accepting the first premise forces the opponent to defend the second. For example, if the proponent argues 'Change exists in the world, so necessarily it is originated,' the respondent can object: 'We do not accept that change exists; and if we accept it, we do not accept that it necessitates origination.' This refines the dialectical strategy, allowing the respondent to challenge arguments systematically without conceding.

Key Terms
ترديدات (tarīdāt): Disjunctive statements or reasoning involving exclusive alternatives (e.g., 'either this or that'), used in dialectical arguments to present opposing possibilities.
تقدير التسليم (taqdīr al-taslīm): The condition of acceptance; objecting to a premise only on the hypothetical acceptance of another premise.
معلل (muʿallil): The proponent or reasoner who gives the argument; the one who presents the proof in dialectic.
سائل (sāʾil): The respondent or questioner in dialectic; the one who raises objections to the proponent's argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a disjunctive argument, if the proponent says 'Either the world is changing or not; if changing then originated; if not then eternal,' what is a valid conditional objection?
A) We do not accept that the world exists.
B) We do not accept that if it is changing then it is originated, even if we accept the disjunction.
C) We do not accept that the world is either changing or not.
D) We accept both conditionals.

Key Takeaway
Conditional objections allow a respondent to deconstruct an argument without conceding its force, by selectively targeting parts of the reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional objection to the following argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Use the style: 'We do not accept that every intoxicant is forbidden; and if we accept that, we do not accept that nabidh is an intoxicant.'
?
Identify the difference between objecting unconditionally and conditionally in the disjunctive argument: 'Either the act is wajib or mubah; if wajib, then reward; if mubah, then no punishment.' Formulate both a direct rejection and a conditional objection.

Ordered Prevention in Jadal: Conditional and Disjunctive Examples
Explanation of ordered prevention with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an ordered prevention in a jadal exchange and determine whether the questioner's second prevention is logically dependent on the acceptance of the first.

Core Idea
In jadal, preventing a premise on the assumption of another's acceptance creates a structured dependency the defender must respect.

This lesson dives into a key dialectical tactic: the ordered prevention (man' al-muqaddima 'ala man' muqaddima ukhra). When the questioner rejects one premise, then after the defender accepts it, rejects another, the sequence must follow logical dependency. The commentator illustrates with conditional and disjunctive examples from classical jadal, such as the world's temporality and Muhammad's location.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defender's original proof
Locate the mu'allil's initial argument, which typically includes a disjunction (e.g., 'either this or that') or a simple syllogism.
The proof sets up the premises that the questioner may target.
2
Locate the questioner's first prevention
Find where the questioner says 'We do not accept that...' for the first premise. This is the initial man' mentioned in the ordered sequence.
The first prevention may target either the first or second part of the disjunction.
3
Find the second prevention and the assumption of acceptance
The second prevention follows after the phrase 'and if we accept that (wa-in sallamnāhu)...' indicating taqdir al-taslim. The questioner then prevents another premise.
This second prevention is conditional upon the first being hypothetically accepted.
4
Determine logical dependency
Check whether the second prevention would be meaningless without the first being accepted. If it is logically built on the first, it is a dependent ordered prevention; otherwise, it is a separate objection.
The text later distinguishes between these two cases as wajib and istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَنْعُ المَعْلومِ مُطلقًا مُكابَرَةٌ ، دُونَ الخَفيّ ، ومَنْعُ مُقَدِّمَةِ التَّنبيه فإِنَّهُ يَجُوزُ تَجوزا ومَنْعُ المُقدِّمَةِ عَلَى مَنْعِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أُخْرَى عَلَى تقدير التسليم سواء كانَ المَنْعُ في ( ومنع المقدمة ) مرتبا في الذكر ( على منع مقدمة أخرى على تقدير التسليم ) أي تسليم المقدس الأخرى سواء كان يمنع المقدمة المتقدمة أولا والمؤخرة ثانيا أو بالعكس ( سواء كان المنع المذكور ( في حدوثه ، فيقول . لا نسلم أن التغير في العالم موجود، وإن سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم كونه ضروري مطلقا فإن المنع الموجه عليه يكون مكابرة . وأما منع البديهي الخفي أو منع مقدمة التنبيه على البديهي فجائز، باعتبار أن كلا من منع مقدمة الدليل أو منع البديهي الخفي أو منع نقد التنبيه طلبا مطلقا (۱) هذا نوع آخر من أنواع المنوع . وهو المنع المرتب في الذكر على تقدير تسليم مقدمة أخرى وسواء كان ذلك المنع في الترديدات كما إذا قال المعلل : محمد إما أن يكون في البيت أو خارجه ، فإن كان في البيت فهو يكتب. وإن كان خارجه فهو لا يكتب فيقول السائل : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان في البيت فهو يكتب . . إذا سلمناه فلا نسلم أنه إذا كان خارج البيت فلا يكتب وله أن يعكس ويقول : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان خارج البيت فهو لا يكتب . وإن سلمناه لا تسلم أنه إذا كان في البيت فهو يكتب . أو في غير الترديدات كالمثال الذي أتى به الشارح . ثم قال: التَّردِيداتِ أَوْلاً عَلَى تَفاوُتٍ . وقد لا يضَرُّ المَنْعُ فللمُعَلِّلِ أنْ يُردّدَ ويقول : إن كانت المُقَدِّمَةُ ثَابِتَةٌ فَيمُ الحدوث ، وعلى ذلك التقدير فالمنع الثاني مبني على تقدير التسليم And preventing the known absolutely is obstinacy, except the obscure, and preventing the premise of notification is permissible metaphorically, and preventing a premise based on the prevention of another premise upon the assumption of acceptance, whether the prevention is ordered in mention (preventing a premise upon preventing another premise upon the assumption of acceptance) i.e. accepting the other premises, whether he prevents the earlier premise first and the later second or vice versa, whether the mentioned prevention (is in the disjunctions, as when the raisonneur says: It is not free from being either this or that; if it is this then such-and-such, and if it is that then such-and-such; then the questioner says: We do not accept that if it is this then such-and-such, and if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is that then such-and-such; or he says the opposite, by saying: We do not accept that if it is that then such-and-such, and if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is this then such-and-such (or not) in it, as was said: The world is changing, and everything changing is originated; he says: We do not accept that the world is changing, and if we accept that, but we do not accept that everything changing is originated; or he says the opposite (1) But that prevention upon the assumption of acceptance may be by way of obligation, as when the second prevention is based on the assumption of acceptance, as when he says: Change exists in the world, so its origination is necessary; he says: We do not accept that change exists in the world, and if we accept that, but we do not accept it being absolutely necessary; then the prevention directed against it is obstinacy. As for preventing the obscure self-evident or preventing the premise of notification of the self-evident, it is permissible, considering that each of preventing the premise of the proof or preventing the obscure self-evident or preventing the critique of notification is an absolute request. (1) This is another type of prevention. It is the prevention ordered in mention upon the assumption of accepting another premise, whether that prevention is in disjunctions, as when the raisonneur says: Muhammad is either in the house or outside; if he is in the house he is writing, and if he is outside he is not writing; then the questioner says: We do not accept that if he is in the house he is writing; if we accept that, we do not accept that if he is outside the house he is not writing; and he may reverse and say: We do not accept that if he is outside the house he is not writing; and if we accept that, we do not accept that if he is in the house he is writing. Or in non-disjunctions, like the example the commentator gave. Then he said: The disjunctions first are upon variance. And prevention may not harm, so the raisonneur may disjunct and say: If the premise is established, then the origination is such; and upon that assumption, the second prevention is based on the assumption of the first acceptance; otherwise it is not directed, as is not hidden. And it may be by way of approval, which is when the prevention is not based as its example preceded. And this is the meaning of his words (may his secret be sanctified) 'upon variance' (1) i.e. existing upon it. And by what we mentioned of the meaning of the speech, it appears that his words 'preventing a premise' is the subject, and his words 'upon preventing' is a circumstantial adverbial phrase from it, and his words 'upon the assumption of acceptance' is an overlapping circumstantial, and his words 'upon variance' is its predicate. So understand this speech, for it is a slipping place for feet.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from the sharh of the Rashidiyya on the concept of 'preventing a premise upon the prevention of another premise' (man' al-muqaddima 'ala man' muqaddima ukhra) in dialectical argument (jadal). The commentator begins by stating that preventing the absolutely known is obstinacy (mukabara), except for the obscure (khafi). Preventing the premise of notification (tanbih) is permissible metaphorically. He then defines the main topic: preventing a premise based on the prevention of another premise upon the assumption of acceptance (taslim) of the other premises. This involves an ordered sequence: the questioner may first prevent one premise, then, after acceptance by the opponent, prevent another premise. The order can be reversed. This ordered prevention can occur in disjunctive arguments (tardedidat) or non-disjunctive ones. The commentator provides examples: in a disjunction, the raisonneur says 'Either this or that; if this then such, if that then such.' The questioner may prevent the conditional, e.g., 'We do not accept that if this then such; and if we accept that, we do not accept that if that then such.' The example of the world being changing and everything changing being originated illustrates a non-disjunctive case. The commentator then distinguishes between two types of ordered prevention: one by way of obligation (wujub) and one by way of approval (istihsan). The obligated prevention occurs when the second prevention logically depends on the acceptance of the first, as when the first premise is necessary for the second. The approving prevention occurs when the second prevention does not depend on the first; it is merely permissible. The passage ends with a grammatical analysis of the matn's phrasing, noting that 'preventing a premise' is the subject, 'upon preventing' is a circumstantial adverbial, 'upon the assumption of acceptance' is an overlapping circumstantial, and 'upon variance' is the predicate. This analysis shows the logical structure: prevention is ordered and varies in necessity.

Key Terms
منع
مقدمة
تقدير التسليم
ترديدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ordered prevention of a disjunction, what does the phrase 'وإن سلمناه' (wa-in sallamnāhu) indicate?
A) The questioner definitively accepts the premise.
B) The questioner hypothetically assumes the premise is accepted for the sake of the next objection.
C) The defender must respond immediately.
D) The prevention is automatically invalid.

Key Takeaway
Ordered prevention allows the questioner to chain rejections, but the defender only needs to respond when the second prevention logically depends on the first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Questioner says: 'We do not accept that if it is created then it is not eternal. And if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is uncreated then it is eternal.' Is this an ordered prevention? Explain the dependency.
?

Obligation vs. Approval in Ordered Prevention: Wujub and Istihsan
Distinction between obligatory and approved ordered prevention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an obligatory (wajib) and an approved (mustahsan) ordered prevention in jadal based on logical dependency.

Core Idea
Not all ordered preventions force a response; only those where the second prevention is built on the first are obligatory.

The commentator deepens the concept by dividing ordered preventions into two kinds: wujub (obligation) and istihsan (approval). The first arises when the second prevention is logically tied to the first premise being accepted; the second occurs when there is no such dependency. This distinction is crucial for the defender to know which objections demand a reply.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of ordered prevention
Ordered prevention involves a questioner preventing a premise after hypothetically accepting a prior one. The commentator now distinguishes two types based on whether the second prevention is 'built' on the first.
The key phrase is 'mabniyyan 'ala taqdir al-taslim' (built on the assumption of acceptance).
2
Locate the example of obligatory prevention
The text gives: 'Change exists in the world, so its origination is necessary.' The questioner first prevents 'change exists', then after acceptance prevents 'its origination is necessary'. The second prevention is built on the first because the necessity of origination depends on the existence of change.
This is wajib because the defender must address the second prevention to salvage the proof.
3
Contrast with the example of approved prevention
In the 'Muhammad in the house' example, the second prevention (about the conditional) does not depend on the first prevention being accepted; the questioner could have made either objection independently.
Here the defender may choose to respond or not; it is istihsan.
4
Apply the rule to determine obligation
Given a jadal exchange, ask: Does the second prevention logically require the first premise to be true? If yes, it is wajib; if no, it is istihsan.
The defender should prioritize responding to wajib preventions, as they threaten the logical chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَنْعُ المَعْلومِ مُطلقًا مُكابَرَةٌ ، دُونَ الخَفيّ ، ومَنْعُ مُقَدِّمَةِ التَّنبيه فإِنَّهُ يَجُوزُ تَجوزا ومَنْعُ المُقدِّمَةِ عَلَى مَنْعِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أُخْرَى عَلَى تقدير التسليم سواء كانَ المَنْعُ في ( ومنع المقدمة ) مرتبا في الذكر ( على منع مقدمة أخرى على تقدير التسليم ) أي تسليم المقدس الأخرى سواء كان يمنع المقدمة المتقدمة أولا والمؤخرة ثانيا أو بالعكس ( سواء كان المنع المذكور ( في الترديدات ، كما إذا قال المعلل : لا يخلو إما أن يكون هذا أو ذلك، فإن كان هذا فكذا ، وإن كان ذلك فكذلك ، فيقول السائل : لا نسلم أنه إن كان هذا فكذا ، وإن سلمناه فلا نسلم أنه إن كان ذلك فكذلك ، أو يقول بالعكس، بأن يقول : لا نسلم أنه إن كان ذلك فكذلك ، وإن سلم فلا نسلم أنه إن كان هذا فكذا (أولا) يكون فيها، كما قيل . العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث، فيقول . لا نسلم أن العالم متغير، وإن سلمنا ذلك ، لكن لا نسلم أن كل متغير حادث ، أو يقول بالعكس (۱) ولكن كون ذلك المنع على تقدير التسليم قد يكون بطريق الوجوب، كما إذا كان المنع الثاني مبنيا على تقدير التسليم، كما إذا قال التغير في العالم موجود ، فلابد من حدوثه ، فيقول . لا نسلم أن التغير في العالم موجود، وإن سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم كونه ضروري مطلقا فإن المنع الموجه عليه يكون مكابرة . وأما منع البديهي الخفي أو منع مقدمة التنبيه على البديهي فجائز، باعتبار أن كلا من منع مقدمة الدليل أو منع البديهي الخفي أو منع نقد التنبيه طلبا مطلقا (۱) هذا نوع آخر من أنواع المنوع . وهو المنع المرتب في الذكر على تقدير تسليم مقدمة أخرى وسواء كان ذلك المنع في الترديدات كما إذا قال المعلل : محمد إما أن يكون في البيت أو خارجه ، فإن كان في البيت فهو يكتب. وإن كان خارجه فهو لا يكتب فيقول السائل : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان في البيت فهو يكتب . . إذا سلمناه فلا نسلم أنه إذا كان خارج البيت فلا يكتب وله أن يعكس ويقول : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان خارج البيت فهو لا يكتب . وإن سلمناه لا تسلم أنه إذا كان في البيت فهو يكتب . أو في غير الترديدات كالمثال الذي أتى به الشارح . ثم قال: التَّردِيداتِ أَوْلاً عَلَى تَفاوُتٍ . وقد لا يضَرُّ المَنْعُ فللمُعَلِّلِ أنْ يُردّدَ ويقول : إن كانت المُقَدِّمَةُ ثَابِتَةٌ فَيمُ الحدوث ، وعلى ذلك التقدير فالمنع الثاني مبني على تقدير التسليم الأول ، وإلا لم يتوجه كما لا يخفى . وقد يكون بطريق الاستحسان ، وهو إذا لم يكن المنع مبنيا كما سلف مثاله ، وهذا معنى قوله قدس سره ) على تفاوت (۱) أي كائن عليه ، وبما ذكرنا من معنى الكلام ظهر أن قوله - منع المقدمة - مبتدأ ، وقوله - على منع - ظرف مستقر حال منه ، وقوله - على تقدير التسليم - حال متداخلة ، وقوله - على تفاوت - خبره فافهم هذا الكلام فإنه من مزال الأقدام .
And preventing the known absolutely is obstinacy, except the obscure, and preventing the premise of notification is permissible metaphorically, and preventing a premise based on the prevention of another premise upon the assumption of acceptance, whether the prevention is ordered in mention (preventing a premise upon preventing another premise upon the assumption of acceptance) i.e. accepting the other premises, whether he prevents the earlier premise first and the later second or vice versa, whether the mentioned prevention (is in the disjunctions, as when the raisonneur says: It is not free from being either this or that; if it is this then such-and-such, and if it is that then such-and-such; then the questioner says: We do not accept that if it is this then such-and-such, and if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is that then such-and-such; or he says the opposite, by saying: We do not accept that if it is that then such-and-such, and if we accept that, we do not accept that if it is this then such-and-such (or not) in it, as was said: The world is changing, and everything changing is originated; he says: We do not accept that the world is changing, and if we accept that, but we do not accept that everything changing is originated; or he says the opposite (1) But that prevention upon the assumption of acceptance may be by way of obligation, as when the second prevention is based on the assumption of acceptance, as when he says: Change exists in the world, so its origination is necessary; he says: We do not accept that change exists in the world, and if we accept that, but we do not accept it being absolutely necessary; then the prevention directed against it is obstinacy. As for preventing the obscure self-evident or preventing the premise of notification of the self-evident, it is permissible, considering that each of preventing the premise of the proof or preventing the obscure self-evident or preventing the critique of notification is an absolute request. (1) This is another type of prevention. It is the prevention ordered in mention upon the assumption of accepting another premise, whether that prevention is in disjunctions, as when the raisonneur says: Muhammad is either in the house or outside; if he is in the house he is writing, and if he is outside he is not writing; then the questioner says: We do not accept that if he is in the house he is writing; if we accept that, we do not accept that if he is outside the house he is not writing; and he may reverse and say: We do not accept that if he is outside the house he is not writing; and if we accept that, we do not accept that if he is in the house he is writing. Or in non-disjunctions, like the example the commentator gave. Then he said: The disjunctions first are upon variance. And prevention may not harm, so the raisonneur may disjunct and say: If the premise is established, then the origination is such; and upon that assumption, the second prevention is based on the assumption of the first acceptance; otherwise it is not directed, as is not hidden. And it may be by way of approval, which is when the prevention is not based as its example preceded. And this is the meaning of his words (may his secret be sanctified) 'upon variance' (1) i.e. existing upon it. And by what we mentioned of the meaning of the speech, it appears that his words 'preventing a premise' is the subject, and his words 'upon preventing' is a circumstantial adverbial phrase from it, and his words 'upon the assumption of acceptance' is an overlapping circumstantial, and his words 'upon variance' is its predicate. So understand this speech, for it is a slipping place for feet.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from the sharh of the Rashidiyya on the concept of 'preventing a premise upon the prevention of another premise' (man' al-muqaddima 'ala man' muqaddima ukhra) in dialectical argument (jadal). The commentator begins by stating that preventing the absolutely known is obstinacy (mukabara), except for the obscure (khafi). Preventing the premise of notification (tanbih) is permissible metaphorically. He then defines the main topic: preventing a premise based on the prevention of another premise upon the assumption of acceptance (taslim) of the other premises. This involves an ordered sequence: the questioner may first prevent one premise, then, after acceptance by the opponent, prevent another premise. The order can be reversed. This ordered prevention can occur in disjunctive arguments (tardedidat) or non-disjunctive ones. The commentator provides examples: in a disjunction, the raisonneur says 'Either this or that; if this then such, if that then such.' The questioner may prevent the conditional, e.g., 'We do not accept that if this then such; and if we accept that, we do not accept that if that then such.' The example of the world being changing and everything changing being originated illustrates a non-disjunctive case. The commentator then distinguishes between two types of ordered prevention: one by way of obligation (wujub) and one by way of approval (istihsan). The obligated prevention occurs when the second prevention logically depends on the acceptance of the first, as when the first premise is necessary for the second. The approving prevention occurs when the second prevention does not depend on the first; it is merely permissible. The passage ends with a grammatical analysis of the matn's phrasing, noting that 'preventing a premise' is the subject, 'upon preventing' is a circumstantial adverbial, 'upon the assumption of acceptance' is an overlapping circumstantial, and 'upon variance' is the predicate. This analysis shows the logical structure: prevention is ordered and varies in necessity.

Key Terms
وجوب
استحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, when is the second prevention considered obligatory (wajib)?
A) When the questioner reverses the order of prevention.
B) When the second prevention is based on the assumption of the first prevention being accepted.
C) When the first premise is self-evident (badihi).
D) When the prevention occurs in non-disjunctive form.

Key Takeaway
The defender is obligated to respond only to ordered preventions that are logically tied to the initial premise acceptance; others are merely approved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the exchange: Defender: 'This water is najis because it is changed. If it is changed, then it is impure.' Questioner: 'We do not accept that the water is changed. And if we accept that, we do not accept that every changed water is impure.' Is the second prevention obligatory or approved? Justify your answer with the dependency criterion.
?
Provide your own example of ordered prevention where the second prevention is clearly obligatory, using an Islamic ruling from fiqh.
