When Prevention Does Not Harm: Concept of Harmless Manʿ
Commentator defines harmless prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a prevention (manʿ) by the questioner is harmless to the defender in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
If a premise's truth and falsity both lead to the defender's conclusion, prevention is futile.

In jadal, the questioner's prevention aims to block a premise. However, sometimes blocking a premise actually helps the defender because the conclusion follows regardless. The commentator explains this subtlety and shows how to recognise such cases, a key skill for any debater.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prevented premise
Locate which premise the questioner is attacking in the defender's proof.
The premise is usually explicitly stated in the proof.
2
Determine the defender's matlub
What is the desired conclusion the defender aims to prove? This is the end goal of the argument.
The matlub is typically the central claim being defended.
3
Assess entailment of truth and falsity
Check whether the truth of the premise entails the matlub, and whether its falsity also entails the matlub. If both entailments hold, the prevention is harmless.
The commentator explicitly defines harmless prevention as when the denial of the premise entails the matlub.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد لا يضَرُّ المَنْعُ، بأن يكون انتقاء تلك المقدمة مستلزما لمطلوبه الذي يستدل عليه بالدليل الذي هو يتوقف عليها ( فللمعلل، في جواب ذلك المنع ( أن يردد ويقول : إن كانت المقدمة الممنوعة (ثاتبة ) في نفس الأمر ( فيتم الدليل، وإلا ، أي وإن لم تكن ثابتة ( فالدعوى ثابتة على ذلك التقدير ) أي تقدير عدم ثبوتها أيضا ، كما إذا قال المعلل في إثبات حدوث الأعيان الثابتة إنها متغيرة، وكل متغير لا يخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حادث ، أما كونها متغيرة فظاهر، وأما كون كل متغير محلا للحوادث ، فلأن التغير إنما هو انتقال الشيء من (۱) ومعنى قول المصنف على تفاوت أي أن المنع المرتب على تقدير تسليم المقدمة الأخرى متفاوت فقد يكون التسليم واجبا كما إذا كان المنع الثاني متوقفا على تسليم المقدمة الأخرى. وقد يكون التسليم المبني عليه المنع مستحسنا كما إذا لم يكن المنع متوقفا على تسليم المقدمة وقد ذكر الشارح مثاليهما (۲) المنع من وظائف السائل. والغرض منه التوجه على مقدمة معينة من مقدمات دليل المعلل . لكن قد يمنع السائل مقدمة من مقدمات المعلل ولا يضر منعها المعلل كما إذا اتفق وكان ثبوت المقدمة مستلزما للمطلوب كما أن عدم ثبوتها مستلزما لمطلوبه أيضا . ولقد ساق الشارح دليلا لبيان هذا المنع الذي لا يضر المعلل فلا حاجة إلى ذكره .
And prevention may not harm, such that the denial of that premise entails his desired conclusion which he is proving by the proof that depends on it. (So the raisonneur, in answer to that prevention, may disjunct and say: If the prevented premise is established in actual fact, then the proof is complete; otherwise, i.e. if it is not established, then the claim is established upon that assumption) i.e. the assumption of its non-establishment also, as when the raisonneur says in proving the origination of permanent entities: They are changing, and everything changing is not free of events, and everything like that is originated. As for their being changing, it is apparent; and as for everything changing being a locus for events, because change is nothing but the transfer of a thing from... (1) And the meaning of the author's words 'upon variance' is that the prevention ordered upon the assumption of accepting another premise is variant; sometimes the acceptance is obligatory, as when the second prevention depends on the acceptance of the other premise; and sometimes the acceptance upon which the prevention is based is approved, as when the prevention does not depend on the acceptance of the premise; and the commentator has given examples of both. (2) Prevention is one of the functions of the questioner. Its purpose is to direct attention to a particular premise from the premises of the defender's proof. But sometimes the questioner prevents a premise from the defender's premises and his prevention does not harm the defender, as when it happens that the establishment of the premise entails the desired conclusion and its non-establishment also entails his desired conclusion. And the commentator has given a proof to explain this prevention that does not harm the defender, so there is no need to mention it.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage continues the sharh's discussion on prevention (man') in jadal, focusing on cases where the questioner's prevention does not harm the defender. The commentator explains that sometimes denying a particular premise actually leads to the same conclusion the defender is proving. In such a case, the prevention is harmless. The defender's response is to use a disjunction: 'If the prevented premise is true, the proof is complete; if not, the claim still stands on that assumption.' The example given is proving the origination (huduth) of permanent entities: the defender argues they are changing, and everything changing is subject to events, thus originated. If the questioner prevents one of these premises, say 'everything changing is subject to events,' the defender can reply that even if that premise is not accepted, the claim of origination may still be established through another argument. The passage also reiterates that prevention is the questioner's tool to attack specific premises. However, if both the truth and falsity of a premise lead to the defender's desired conclusion, the prevention is futile. The commentator notes that the author of the matn uses 'upon variance' (ala tafawut) to indicate that the ordered prevention varies in necessity: sometimes the second prevention obligates a response, sometimes it is merely commendable. The thorough explanation of this subtle point is deemed important because it is a common pitfall (mazall al-aqdam).

Key Terms
لا يضر
مطلوب
انتقاء
مستلزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a prevention (manʿ) considered harmless to the defender?
A) When the questioner admits the premise is true.
B) When the truth and falsity of the premise both entail the defender's conclusion.
C) When the defender has no other arguments.
D) When the questioner is a novice.

Key Takeaway
A prevention is harmless if the defender's conclusion is entailed by both the truth and falsity of the prevented premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Kalam argument for huduth: The world is changing; whatever changes is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated. If a questioner prevents 'whatever changes is temporally originated', show that the conclusion may still be established by another argument (e.g., the impossibility of infinite regress). Explain why this prevention is harmless.

Defender's Disjunctive Response to Harmless Prevention
Commentator gives defender's disjunctive response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a disjunctive (conditional) response to a harmless prevention in jadal.

Core Idea
The defender responds with a conditional disjunction: if premise true, proof complete; if false, claim still stands.

When faced with a harmless prevention, the defender does not need to defend the contested premise directly. Instead, he uses a disjunctive counter-argument: regardless of whether the premise is true or false, the conclusion remains established. This response is both elegant and logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the harmless prevention
First, determine that the prevention is harmless using the condition from the previous lesson (truth and falsity both entail the matlub).
This step is crucial; the disjunctive response only works if the prevention is truly harmless.
2
Formulate the disjunction
The defender says: 'If the prevented premise is true (in actual fact), the proof is complete; if it is not true, the claim is still established on that assumption.' This creates two branches, both leading to the same conclusion.
This is a constructive dilemma.
3
Justify both branches
The defender must show how each branch leads to the matlub. Typically, if the premise is true, the original proof stands; if false, an alternative proof or reasoning supports the claim.
In the given example, the defender uses the change and events argument.
4
Apply to a real Kalam example
Practice with the huduth argument: if the questioner prevents the premise 'everything changing is subject to events', respond with the disjunction and ensure both branches are justified.
The commentator uses this exact example.

Source Text

What is the defender's logical response to a harmless prevention?

Non-Harmful Denial and the Power of Tardid
Explanation of non-harmful denial and tardid strategy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-harmful denial and construct a tardid (dichotomous) response in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
A denial that does not harm can be neutralized by a dichotomous response that makes either alternative support the conclusion.

In Islamic dialectical theory (jadal), not every denial of a premise is fatal. The reasoner (muʿallil) can use tardid—a dichotomous response—to show that the conclusion follows regardless of whether the denied premise holds. This lesson examines when denial does not harm and how tardid works, using the classic kalām argument for the world's origination as an example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denied premise
In the dialectical exchange, find the premise that the objector (māniʿ) denies. In the example, the objector denies the exhaustive division of bodies into rest or motion.
The denial is not of the whole argument but of a specific premise.
2
Determine if the denial is harmful
Check whether the proof depends on the denied premise. If the conclusion can still be reached without that premise, the denial may be non-harmful. The muʿallil can show that the conclusion follows even if the premise is false.
3
Construct a tardid response
Formulate a dichotomous response: 'If the premise is true, the proof stands; if it is false, the conclusion still holds.' This is exactly what the muʿallil does: either the exhaustive division is established (proof complete) or not (directly implies origination).
The tardid must ensure both alternatives lead to the same conclusion.
4
Verify the alternatives lead to the conclusion
Check each alternative to confirm that the conclusion is necessarily entailed. In the example, if the division is not exhaustive, the body is not preceded by a location, which means it is temporally originated.
This step ensures the denial truly does not harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّردِيداتِ أَوْلاً عَلَى تَفاوُتٍ . وقد لا يضَرُّ المَنْعُ فللمُعَلِّلِ أنْ يُردّدَ ويقول : إن كانت المُقَدِّمَةُ ثَابِتَةٌ فَيمُ الحدوث ، وعلى ذلك التقدير فالمنع الثاني مبني على تقدير التسليم الأول ، وإلا لم يتوجه كما لا يخفى . وقد يكون بطريق الاستحسان ، وهو إذا لم يكن المنع مبنيا كما سلف مثاله ، وهذا معنى قوله قدس سره ) على تفاوت (۱) أي كائن عليه ، وبما ذكرنا من معنى الكلام ظهر أن قوله - منع المقدمة - مبتدأ ، وقوله - على منع - ظرف مستقر حال منه ، وقوله - على تقدير التسليم - حال متداخلة ، وقوله - على تفاوت - خبره فافهم هذا الكلام فإنه من مزال الأقدام . وقد لا يضر المنع ، بأن يكون انتقاء تلك المقدمة مستلزما لمطلوبه الذي يستدل عليه بالدليل الذي هو يتوقف عليها ( فللمعلل، في جواب ذلك المنع ( أن يردد ويقول : إن كانت المقدمة الممنوعة (ثاتبة ) في نفس الأمر ( فيتم الدليل، وإلا ، أي وإن لم تكن ثابتة ( فالدعوى ثابتة على ذلك التقدير ) أي تقدير عدم ثبوتها أيضا ، كما إذا قال المعلل في إثبات حدوث الأعيان الثابتة إنها متغيرة، وكل متغير لا يخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حادث ، أما كونها متغيرة فظاهر، وأما كون كل متغير محلا للحوادث ، فلأن التغير إنما هو انتقال الشيء من حالة إلى حالة أخرى، وتلك الأخرى حادثة ، لأنها وجدت فيه بعد ما لم تكن موجودة ، ثم تلك الأخرى قائمة بذلك الشيء المتغير لامتناع قيام الصفة بدون موصوفها ، فيكون ذلك الشيء المتغير محلا للحوادث، فإن الشيء - عند كل تغير وانتقال - يكون محلا لحادث لم يكن هو محله . وأما أن كل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، فلأن الأعيان الثابتة لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وهما حادثان ، وبيان عدم الخلو أن الأعيان لا تخلو عن الكون في حيز ، فإن كانت من حيث كونها في ذلك الحيز الآن مسبوقة بكون آخر فيه فهي ساكنة ، وإن لم تكن مسبوقة بكون آخر فيه، بل تكون في حيز آخر فمتحركة ، ويقول المانع : لا نسلم ذلك الانحصار ، لم لا يجوز أن لا تكون مسبوقة يكون آخر أصلا كما في آن الحدوث ، فحينئذ تكون خالية عن الحركة والسكون كليهما، فللمعلل حينئذ أن يردد ويقول : إما أن يكون الانحصار ثابتا أولا، فإن كان ثابتا فقد تم الدليل، وإلا يلزم ثبوت المطلوب وهو حدوث الأعيان ، لأنه إذا لم يكن الشيء مسبوقا بكون آخر فلا شك في حدوثه وقيل بخلافه أيضا ) يعني أن بعضهم قالوا : ليس المعلل أن يقول ذلك ، بل لابد له من إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة أو التغيير إلى دليل آخر .
The tardids are firstly based on a disparity. And sometimes the denial does not harm, so the reasoner may perform tardid and say: If the premise is established, then the proof is complete; on that estimation, the second denial is based on the estimation of the first admission, otherwise it does not apply, as is not hidden. And it may be by way of approval (istihsan), which is when the denial is not based [on a prior admission] as was previously exemplified. This is the meaning of his words, may God sanctify his secret: 'on a disparity', i.e., it is there. And from what we have mentioned of the meaning of the speech, it appears that his words 'denial of the premise' is the subject, his words 'on a denial' is an adverbial circumstance as a predicate state from it, and his words 'on the estimation of admission' is an embedded state, and his words 'on a disparity' is its predicate. So understand this speech for it is a slippery area. And sometimes the denial does not harm, such that the absence of that premise entails his goal which he argues for by the proof that depends on it. So the reasoner, in answer to that denial, may perform tardid and say: If the denied premise is established in reality, then the proof is complete; otherwise—that is, if it is not established—then the claim is established on that estimation as well, i.e., the estimation of its non-establishment. For example, when the reasoner says in proving the temporal origination of stable entities: they are changing, and every changing thing does not lack accidents, and everything that does not lack accidents is temporally originated. As for their being changing, it is apparent. As for every changing thing being a locus for accidents, it is because change is only the transition of a thing from one state to another, and that other state is an accident, because it came to exist in it after it did not exist. Then that other state subsists in that changing thing because it is impossible for an attribute to subsist without its subject. So that changing thing becomes a locus for accidents, for the thing—at every change and transition—becomes a locus for an accident that was not its locus. And as for everything that does not lack accidents being temporally originated, it is because stable entities do not lack motion and rest, and both are accidents. The proof of not lacking is that stable entities do not lack being in a location. If they, by virtue of being in that location now, are preceded by another location, then they are at rest. If they are not preceded by another location but are in another location, then they are in motion. And the objector says: We do not admit that exhaustive division; why is it not permissible for it not to be preceded by any other location at all, as at the moment of origination? Then it would be free from both motion and rest. Then the reasoner may perform tardid and say: Either the exhaustive division is established or not. If it is established, then the proof is completed; otherwise, the establishment of the goal follows, which is the temporal origination of the entities, because if a thing is not preceded by a location, there is no doubt about its origination. And it was also said differently, i.e., some said: The reasoner should not say that, rather he must prove the denied premise or change to another proof.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concept of 'man' al-muqaddima' (denial of a premise) in jadal (dialectical argumentation). The author explains that denials can vary in their impact: some are harmful to the reasoner's case, while others are not. The key idea is that a reasoner (mu'allil) can respond to a denial by using tardid (dichotomizing or presenting alternatives) to show that regardless of whether the denied premise holds or not, the conclusion follows. The passage begins by restating that tardids (alternatives) are based on a disparity (tafawut). Then it describes the scenario where denial does not harm: the mu'allil performs tardid by saying 'If the premise is established, then the proof is complete; if not, then the claim is established on that estimation as well.' This is illustrated with the famous kalām argument for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies. The argument proceeds through three premises: stable entities are changing; every changing thing is a locus for accidents; and everything that does not lack accidents is originated. The commentator expands on each premise, explaining why change entails being a locus for accidents, and why not lacking accidents entails origination. The objector (māniʿ) denies the exhaustive division (inhisar) of bodies into either at rest or in motion, suggesting the possibility of a body at the moment of its origination being free from both. The mu'allil then uses tardid: either the exhaustive division is established (so the proof stands) or not (which directly entails origination, since if not preceded by a location, the body is originated). The passage concludes by noting that some scholars disagree, insisting that the mu'allil must instead prove the denied premise or change to a different proof. This commentary thus highlights the dialectical strategy of using a dichotomous response to neutralize objections, a hallmark of mature jadal practice.

A denial is non-harmful when the muʿallil can show that the conclusion follows whether the denied premise is true or false.

When Should the Preventer Object? Timing of Objections in Dialectical Debate
Author discusses whether the preventer should pause until the prover completes his proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of pausing in dialectical debates to determine when a preventer should object, and explain the rationale behind the two opinions.

Core Idea
The preventer should wait until the prover finishes, because the prover may prove the very premise he objected to.

In any dialectical exchange, timing matters. Should the preventer (al-manī') jump in as soon as a premise seems questionable, or should he hold his tongue until the entire proof is laid out? This passage from the Rashidiyya discusses exactly that question, using a famous kalam argument about the temporality of bodies as an illustration. By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preferred opinion and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
The preventer (al-manī') is the objector who challenges a premise; the prover (al-mu'allil) provides the proof (dalīl). Understand their functions in the dialectical exchange.
2
Analyze the example argument
The prover argues that every body is temporally originated because it is never free from motion and rest (both originated). Trace the logical steps: bodies are always in a place, leading to motion or rest, so they are never free from occurrences.
This is a standard kalam proof for ḥudūth al-ajsām.
3
Spot the preventer's objection and the prover's dilemma
The preventer objects that the body could be free from both motion and rest at the moment of origination (ān al-ḥudūth). The prover then uses a dilemma: either the exclusivity of being moving or resting is established or not. If established, the proof stands; if not, the conclusion (origination) follows directly.
This dilemma is a key dialectical tactic (taraddud).
4
Compare the two opinions on pausing
Opinion 1: The preventer should pause until the prover completes the proof, because the prover might later prove the contested premise. Opinion 2: The preventer should object immediately, because the prover might abandon the argument, prolonging the debate. The author favors the first opinion.
The author also notes that for objection (naqḍ) and counter-argument (muʿāraḍa), pausing is obligatory by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحسن توقف المانع إلى إتمام الدليل . حالة إلى حالة أخرى، وتلك الأخرى حادثة ، لأنها وجدت فيه بعد ما لم تكن موجودة ، ثم تلك الأخرى قائمة بذلك الشيء المتغير لامتناع قيام الصفة بدون موصوفها ، فيكون ذلك الشيء المتغير محلا للحوادث، فإن الشيء - عند كل تغير وانتقال - يكون محلا لحادث لم يكن هو محله . وأما أن كل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، فلأن الأعيان الثابتة لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وهما حادثان ، وبيان عدم الخلو أن الأعيان لا تخلو عن الكون في حيز ، فإن كانت من حيث كونها في ذلك الحيز الآن مسبوقة بكون آخر فيه فهي ساكنة ، وإن لم تكن مسبوقة بكون آخر فيه، بل تكون في حيز آخر فمتحركة ، ويقول المانع : لا نسلم ذلك الانحصار ، لم لا يجوز أن لا تكون مسبوقة يكون آخر أصلا كما في آن الحدوث ، فحينئذ تكون خالية عن الحركة والسكون كليهما، فللمعلل حينئذ أن يردد ويقول : إما أن يكون الانحصار ثابتا أولا، فإن كان ثابتا فقد تم الدليل، وإلا يلزم ثبوت المطلوب وهو حدوث الأعيان . وقيل بخلافه أيضا ) يعني أن بعضهم قالوا : ليس المعلل أن يقول ذلك ، بل لابد له من إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة أو التغيير إلى دليل آخر ، فإنه ادعى إلى إثبات الحكم بالدليل، ولا يتحقق ذلك إلا بذينك الطريقين (، وما اختاره المصنف هو الأظهر، لأن المقصد الأصلي من إثبات المقدمة ثبوت المطلوب، فمتى ثبت بدونه لا حاجة إليه ، وإليه أشار بقوله : قيل : ( ويستحسن توقف المانع إلى إتمام ) المعلل ( الدليل ، لأن المعلل ربما يثبت المقدمة بعد إتمام الدليل، فيستغنى السائل عن المنع . وقيل بخلافه ) لأن المعلل كثيرا ما لا يتمكن من إثبات تلك المقدمة ، فيترك الدليل ويشتغل بدليل آخر ، فيأمن من طول المناظرة ، والأول أولى ، لأن الظاهر من حال المعلل الإثبات ( دون النقض والمعارضة ) يجوز أن يتعلق بقوله – ويستحسن - وهو الظاهر، ويحتمل أن يكون متعلقا بالاختلاف المفهوم من السابق ( فإن التوقف فيهما واجب ) بالاتفاق ، أما في النقض، فلأنه كلام على الدليل، فما لم يتم لم يتجه ، وأما في المعارضة فلأنها مقابلة الدليل بالدليل، فقبل تمامه لم يتحقق.
It is considered good for the preventer to pause until the prover completes the proof. ... [transition] from one state to another, and that other is originated, because it occurs in it after it was not existing, then that other is subsisting by that changing thing due to the impossibility of a quality subsisting without its possessor, so that changing thing becomes a locus for occurrences. For every change and transition, the thing becomes a locus for an occurrence that was not its locus. As for everything that is never free from occurrences, it is originated, because permanent entities are never free from motion and rest, both of which are originated. The demonstration of non-freeness is that entities are never free from being in a place. If, by being in that place now, it is preceded by another being in it, then it is at rest. If it is not preceded by another being in it, but rather is in another place, then it is moving. The preventer says: We do not accept that exclusivity; why is it not possible that it is not preceded by any other being at all, as in the moment of origination? Then it would be free from both motion and rest. Then the prover can reply by a dilemma: Either the exclusivity is established or not. If it is established, then the proof is complete; otherwise, the required conclusion follows, namely the origin of entities. And it is also said contrary to it, meaning some said: It is not for the prover to say that, but rather he must either prove the denied premise or change to another proof, for he claims to establish the ruling by proof, and that is only achieved by those two ways. What the author chose is more apparent, because the primary purpose of proving the premise is the establishment of the conclusion; so if it is established without it, there is no need for it. To that he alluded by his saying: It is said: 'It is good for the preventer to pause until the prover completes the proof, because the prover might prove the premise after completing the proof, and then the questioner is spared from objecting.' And it is said contrary to it, because the prover often cannot prove that premise, so he leaves the proof and engages in another proof, thus being safe from a lengthy debate. The first is preferable, because the apparent state of the prover is proof. (As for objection and counter-argument), it may relate to his saying 'it is good', which is apparent, and it may relate to the disagreement understood from the previous. (Thus, pausing in both is obligatory) by agreement. As for objection, it is speech about the proof, so until it is completed it is not directed. As for counter-argument, it is confronting proof with proof, so before completion it is not realized.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses a procedural rule in dialectical debate: whether the preventer (al-manī') should pause immediately to object to a premise of the prover (al-mu'allil) or wait until the prover completes his entire proof. The author first presents an example from the classical kalam argument for the temporal origination of bodies. The prover argues that every body is never free from motion and rest, both of which are originated, so the body is originated. The preventer objects by saying the body might be free from both at the moment of origination (ān al-ḥudūth). The prover then uses a dilemma (taraddud): either the exclusivity of the body being either moving or resting is established or not. If established, the proof stands; if not, the body's origination is directly implied. This shows how the prover can force a conclusion without immediately proving the denied premise. The author then reports two opinions: (1) It is recommended for the preventer to pause until the prover finishes his proof, because the prover may later prove the premise, making the objection unnecessary. (2) Others say the preventer should not wait, because the prover might abandon the proof and start anew, prolonging the debate. The author favors the first opinion, as the prover's primary aim is to establish the conclusion, not the premise itself. Finally, the author clarifies that for objection (naqḍ) and counter-argument (muʿāraḍa), pausing is obligatory by consensus, since both are critiques of the proof and cannot be made before the proof is complete.

Key Terms
المانع (al-manī'): The preventer: the interlocutor who objects to a premise in a dialectical argument, seeking to block the prover's inference. [Note: In later jadal works, this role is often called 'al-muʿāriḍ' or 'al-sā'il', but the function is identical.]
المعلل (al-mu'allil): The prover: the interlocutor who provides a proof (dalīl) for a claim in a dialectical exchange. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Pausing: delaying an objection or counter-argument until after the prover has completed his exposition of the proof. [Note: No significant shift.]
النقض (al-naqḍ): Objection: a direct critique of a specific premise or inference within a proof, claiming it is false or insufficient. [Note: In fiqh usul, naqḍ can also mean 'inconsistency' but here it is strictly the dialectical operation.]
المعارضة (al-muʿāraḍa): Counter-argument: presenting a rival proof that contradicts the prover's conclusion or method. [Note: In later jadal, muʿāraḍa is distinguished from naqḍ as a separate offensive move.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred opinion in the passage, when should the preventer (al-manī') raise his objection against a premise in a dialectical debate?
A) Immediately after the premise is stated.
B) After the prover completes his entire proof.
C) Only if the prover explicitly asks for objections.
D) He should never object; he must only ask questions.

Key Takeaway
The preventer should often pause until the prover finishes the proof, because the prover may prove the contested premise later or use a dilemma to bypass the objection.

In a dialectical debate, the prover argues: 'Every revealed law (sharī'a) either originated or is eternal. It is not eternal, so it originated.' The preventer objects to the premise that 'Every revealed law is either originated or eternal.' According to the preferred opinion in the passage, should the preventer interrupt immediately or wait? Objecting to Claims of Self-Evidence in Dialectic
Author states that objecting to a claim asserted as self-evident is permissible because it reduces to challenging the claim of self-evidence with a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical objection to a claim of self-evidence and assess its permissibility according to the rule presented.

Core Idea
Objecting to self-evidence targets the claim, not the truth.

In dialectical debates, some claims are asserted as self-evident to halt further discussion. But is it ever permissible to object to such a claim? This passage from the Rashidiyya addresses this very question, presenting a view that allows objection under a specific condition: the objection must be supported by proof and directed at the self-evidence, not the proposition itself. However, the author adds a note of caution, signaling that this view is not universally accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assertion of self-evidence
Find in the passage the case where a claim is said to be 'decisive' (muḥkam) and in which self-evidence (badāha) is claimed. This is the target of the objection.
In dialectic, such assertions are often used to close discussion.
2
Understand the nature of the objection
The passage says the objection is not against the content of the claim, but against the claim of self-evidence. The objector provides a proof (sanad) to show the claim is not self-evident.
The objection reduces to 'manʿ al-badāha' with evidence.
3
Analyze the logical reduction
The objection is permissible because it is essentially a challenge to the self-evidence, not a denial of the truth. This is a meta-level move.
This preserves the possibility of constructive debate.
4
Consider the author's reservation
The author adds 'wa fīhi naẓar' (and in it there is a consideration), indicating that this view is debated. Reflect on why someone might object: truly self-evident propositions cannot be proved false by a proof.
This prepares you for deeper analysis in advanced munāẓara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا : يجوز نَقْضُ محكم ادعِي فيه البَدَاهَةُ ، لِرُجُوعِهِ إلَى مَنْعِ البَدَاهَةِ مَعَ السَّنَدِ . وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ .
And they said: It is permissible to object to a decisive claim in which self-evidence is claimed, because it reduces to preventing the self-evidence with a proof. And in it there is a consideration.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific point in dialectical method: whether it is permissible to object (naqḍ) to a claim that someone asserts as self-evident (badīhī). The author reports that some scholars permit such an objection, arguing that objecting in this case is not an attack on the content of the claim itself, but rather a challenge to the assertion of self-evidence. The objector is essentially saying, 'This is not self-evident to me,' and he supports his objection with a proof (sana'd). For example, if someone says, 'The whole is greater than the part, and this is self-evident,' an objector might respond, 'I do not accept that as self-evident, because it can be demonstrated through a proof.' In effect, the objection becomes a denial of the claim's self-evident status, not a denial of the truth of the proposition. The author, however, injects a note of caution: 'And in it there is a consideration' (wa fīhi naẓar), indicating that this view is not universally accepted and requires further scrutiny. The consideration might be that if the claim is truly self-evident, no proof can refute its self-evidence, or that allowing such objections could lead to endless debate.

Key Terms
البداهة (al-badīha): self-evidence
السند (al-sanad): proof or evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it permissible to object to a claim asserted as self-evident?
A) Because the objector can always provide a stronger self-evident claim.
B) Because the objection reduces to challenging the self-evidence with a proof, not the proposition itself.
C) Because the claimant's assertion is automatically invalid.
D) Because the objector must be a recognized authority.

Key Takeaway
Objecting to a claim of self-evidence is permissible in dialectic when the objection is supported by proof and is directed at the self-evidence, not at the truth of the claim, though the author cautions that this view is not without consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a debate about whether 'the world is temporally originated' is self-evident, construct an objection according to the rule described in the passage. Ensure your objection targets the self-evidence, not the truth of the statement.
?
Task 2: Evaluate the following objection using the passage's framework: Claimant: 'The existence of the Creator is self-evident.' Objector: 'That is not self-evident because many people doubt it.' Does this objection meet the criteria? Why or why not?

Dialectic -- Jadal

To Pause or Not to Pause? Two Opinions on Tawaqquf in Man'
Author presents two opinions on pausing in man' and selects the first.

Learning Objective
Contrast the two opinions on whether the objector should pause before the proponent completes his proof and state the author's preferred view.

Core Idea
The objector's pause is strategic; the author prioritizes waiting for the full argument.

In dialectical debate, the objector may deny a premise (man'). But should he wait until the proponent finishes his entire proof? There are two views: one says pausing is commendable; the other says it is not. The Rashidiyya commentary explains both and chooses the first. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Find the two statements: one says pausing is considered good (يستحسن), the other says contrary to that (وقيل بخلافه).
The first is presented as the author's preference.
2
Analyze reasoning for the first position
The proponent might establish the premise after completing the proof, making the objection unnecessary.
3
Analyze reasoning for the second position
The proponent often fails to prove the premise, so he might switch to another proof, lengthening debate. Hence objecting earlier saves time.
4
Determine the author's preference
The author says 'الأول أولى' (the first is better), because the proponent's default condition is to provide proof.
This follows the principle of assuming the proponent can prove his case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقِيلَ بِخِلافِهِ ، دُونَ النَّقض والمُعارضة ، فإنَّ التَّوَقُفِ فيهما واجب . وقالوا : يجوز نَقْضُ محكمُ ادّعِي فيه البَدَاهَةُ ، لِرُجُوعِهِ إلَى مَنْعِ البَدَاهَةِ مَعَ السَّنَدِ . وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ .
الحكم بالدليل، ولا يتحقق ذلك إلا بذينك الطريقين (۱) ، وما اختاره المصنف هو الأظهر، لأن المقصد الأصلي من إثبات المقدمة ثبوت المطلوب، فمتى ثبت بدونه لا حاجة إليه ، وإليه أشار بقوله : قيل : ( ويستحسن توقف المانع إلى إتمام ) المعلل ( الدليل ، لأن المعلل ربما يثبت المقدمة بعد إتمام الدليل، فيستغنى السائل عن المنع . وقيل بخلافه ) لأن المعلل كثيرا ما لا يتمكن من إثبات تلك المقدمة ، فيترك الدليل ويشتغل بدليل آخر ، فيأمن من طول المناظرة ، والأول أولى ، لأن الظاهر من حال المعلل الإثبات ( دون النقض والمعارضة ) يجوز أن يتعلق بقوله – ويستحسن - وهو الظاهر، ويحتمل أن يكون متعلقا بالاختلاف المفهوم من السابق ( فإن التوقف فيهما واجب ) بالاتفاق ، أما في النقض، فلأنه كلام على الدليل، فما لم يتم لم يتجه ، وأما في المعارضة فلأنها مقابلة الدليل بالدليل، فقبل تمامه لم يتحقق (۳) .
ويَنْدَرجُ الحَل في المنعِ لِنَوعِ مُناسبة وإنْ خَالَفَهُ بِوَجْهِ ، إِذْ قَدْ يُقْصَدُ بِهِ تَفْسِيدُ مَوْضِعِ الغَلَطِ لِسُوء الفهم .
البداهة مع السند وهو ما ذكر لإثبات نقيضه ) وفيه نظر لإمكان إرجاعه إلى النقض، بل إلى المعارضة أيضا، كذا في الحاشية . والحاصل أن ما ذكره الناقض يمكن أن يجعل من أفراد النقض الحقيقي بأن يقال : دعوى بداهة دليل على دعواه ، والنقض في الحقيقة راجع إلى ذلك الدليل، وكذا يمكن أن يكون من أفراد المعارضة بأن يكون الدليل المثبت للنقض معارضا لدعوى البداهة التي هي بمنزلة نصب الدليل فلا وجه لإرجاعه إلى منع البداهة مع صحة كونه من أفراد النقض، ولا لاختياره على المعارضة ، ويمكن أن يوجه النظر بوجه آخر ، وهو أنه وإن سلم كون دعوى البداهة بمنزلة الدليل، لكن لا يجوز إرجاعه إلى المنع ، إذ هو طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة ولا يطلب على مقدمة الدعوى شيء كما لا يخفى (1) . ثم لما كان ههنا سؤال : وهو أنه قد يسأل السائل بالحل ، أعني تعيين موضع من الغلط ، فلا يصح يصح حصر الأسئلة في الثلاثة المذكورة ، فأجاب بقوله : ( ويندرج الحل في المنع لنوع مناسبة ) يعني من حيث هو تعرض للمقدمة المعينة ، كما كان المنع كذلك ، ( وإن خالفه بوجه ، إذ يقصد به أي بالحل و تعيين موضع الغلط ) لسوء الفهم، لا طلب الدليل .
And it was said contrary to it, excluding refutation and opposition, for pausing in them is obligatory. And they said: It is permissible to refute a ruling claimed to be self-evident, because it reverts to denying self-evidence with evidence. And there is consideration in it. The ruling by evidence, and it is not realized except by those two ways (1), and what the author chose is the most apparent, because the main purpose of establishing the premise is establishing the sought conclusion, so when it is established without it, there is no need for it. And to it he pointed by his saying: It is said: (It is considered good for the objector to pause until the proponent completes) the proof, because perhaps the proponent will establish the premise after completing the proof, so the questioner becomes free of the objection. And it was said contrary to it) because the proponent often cannot establish that premise, so he abandons the proof and busies himself with another proof, thus he is safe from lengthy debate, and the first is better, because the apparent condition of the proponent is proof (excluding refutation and opposition) it is permissible that it is related to his saying – and it is considered good – and that is apparent, and it is possible that it is related to the difference understood from the previous (for pausing in them is obligatory) by agreement, as for refutation, because it is speech upon the proof, so if it is not completed, it is not directed, and as for opposition, because it is confronting proof with proof, so before its completion, it is not realized (3). And solution is included in objection due to a type of appropriateness, even if it differs from it in one aspect, since it may be intended by it to reveal the place of error due to poor comprehension. The self-evidence with evidence is what was mentioned to prove its opposite) and there is consideration in it because of the possibility of reverting it to refutation, rather also to opposition, as in the margin. The summary is that what the refuter mentioned can be made an instance of true refutation by saying: The claim of self-evidence is evidence for its claim, and the refutation in reality reverts to that evidence, and likewise it can be an instance of opposition by making the evidence that proves the refutation opposing the claim of self-evidence which is like setting up evidence, so there is no face for reverting it to denying self-evidence while it is valid as an instance of refutation, nor for choosing it over opposition, and it is possible to direct consideration in another way, which is that even if it is conceded that the claim of self-evidence is like evidence, it is not permissible to revert it to objection, for it is a request for evidence on a specific premise and nothing is requested on the premise of the claim, as is not hidden (1). Then when there is here a question: namely that the questioner may ask by solution, meaning specifying the place of error, so it is not correct to restrict questions to the three mentioned, so he answered by his saying: (And solution is included in objection due to a type of appropriateness) meaning insofar as it is an engagement with the specific premise, just as objection is, (even if it differs from it in one aspect, since it is intended by it i.e. by solution and specifying the place of error) due to poor comprehension, not a request for evidence.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary addresses several key dialectical rules in debate (jadal). It opens with a statement that there is an opinion contrary to what was previously presented regarding pausing in refutation (naqd) and opposition (mu'arada), but for these two, pausing is obligatory. Then it reports another view that it is permissible to refute a ruling claimed to be self-evident (badihi), because such a refutation essentially becomes a denial of self-evidence backed by evidence. However, there is a consideration (nazar) with this view. The commentary then explains the two opinions about the objector (mani') pausing until the proponent (mu'allil) completes his proof. The first opinion (considered good) is that the objector should wait because the proponent might establish the premise after completion, making the objection unnecessary. The second opinion is that the objector should not wait, because the proponent often fails and then switches to another proof, lengthening the debate. The author chooses the first as superior because the proponent's default state is to provide proof. Then the passage explicitly states that pausing is obligatory in naqd and mu'arada by agreement, explaining that naqd is speech on the proof so not directed until completion, and mu'arada is confronting proof with proof, so it is not realized before the proof is complete. The discussion then turns to the inclusion of 'solution' (hal) under objection (man'). Hal is intended to pinpoint the place of error due to poor understanding, and although it differs from man' in seeking the location of error rather than a premise, it is included under man' because it also engages with a specific premise. The passage then critiques the view that refuting a self-evident claim reverts to denying self-evidence; it argues that such a refutation could be classified as genuine naqd or even mu'arada, and there is no need to revert it to man'. Finally, it addresses a potential objection that there are more than three types of questions (man', naqd, mu'arada) since hal is used, but the author responds that hal is included in man' due to appropriate similarity.

Key Terms
المنع
التوقف
المعلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Rashidiyya, why does the author prefer the opinion that the objector should pause?
A) Because the proponent might give up and waste time.
B) Because the proponent might establish the premise later, making the objection unnecessary.
C) Because the objector needs time to prepare his objection.
D) Because the proponent often fails to prove the premise.

Key Takeaway
The preferred view is for the objector to pause in man' because the proponent is expected to prove his premise; this balances efficiency with fairness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate on whether the world is eternal, the objector wants to deny the premise that 'everything temporally originated has a cause.' Should he pause until the proponent finishes his argument? Justify by referring to the two opinions and the author's preference.

Identify a situation in a contemporary Islamic debate where applying the preferred opinion would be more beneficial than the opposing view.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Obligatory Pause in Naqd and Mu'arada, and Inclusion of Hal in Man'
Author discusses obligatory pausing in naqd and mu'arada and the inclusion of hal in man'.

Learning Objective
Distinguish between man', naqd, mu'arada, and hal, and explain why pausing is obligatory for naqd and mu'arada and how hal is classified under man'.

Core Idea
Naqd and mu'arada require a complete proof; hal finds error within a premise, hence it falls under man'.

Not all objections are created equal. Refutation (naqd) and opposition (mu'arada) differ from simple denial (man') in that they attack the proof itself, thus they must wait until the proof is fully presented. Meanwhile, solution (hal), which identifies a misunderstanding, is considered a type of man' because it targets a specific premise. This lesson clarifies these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of objections
Find the terms naqd, mu'arada, and hal in the passage. Understand their definitions.
Man' was covered in the previous lesson.
2
Explain the obligation to pause in naqd and mu'arada
The passage states pausing is obligatory (واجب) because naqd is speech on the proof and mu'arada is confronting proof with proof, so they cannot be directed before the proof is complete.
This is by agreement (بالاتفاق).
3
Analyze the inclusion of hal under man'
Hal is similar to man' in that it engages with a specific premise (مناسبة). However, hal aims to identify the place of error due to poor understanding, not to request evidence.
It differs in its goal (وجه), but is still included under man'.
4
Test your understanding
Given a sample objection, classify it as man', naqd, mu'arada, or hal.

Source Text

Can You Refute a Self-Evident Claim? The Debate on Naqd of Badihi
Author examines the permissibility and classification of refuting a self-evident claim.

Learning Objective
Analyze the controversy over refuting a claim of self-evidence and explain why some consider it a form of man', naqd, or mu'arada.

Core Idea
Even a claim of self-evidence can be challenged, but the proper classification of that challenge is disputed among scholars.

What if a proponent claims his premise is self-evident (badihi) and beyond proof? Can the objector still refute it? The passage presents a view that it is permissible, but there is consideration (nazar). The latter part of the passage explores whether such a refutation should be classified as man', naqd, or mu'arada. This lesson delves into that nuanced discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being discussed
The passage states: 'They said: it is permissible to refute a ruling claimed to be self-evident.' Find the Arabic for this.
2
Understand the initial justification
The justification is that such refutation reverts to denying self-evidence with evidence (لِرُجُوعِهِ إلَى مَنْعِ البَدَاهَةِ مَعَ السَّنَدِ).
3
Analyze the consideration (نظر)
The author says 'وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ' (there is consideration). The commentary explains that this refutation could be seen as genuine naqd or even mu'arada, not simply man'.
The summary in the margin discusses the possibilities.
4
Evaluate the alternative classifications
The passage argues that the refutation can be made an instance of true naqd by seeing the claim of self-evidence as evidence itself, or an instance of mu'arada by seeing the refuting evidence as opposing that claim. Therefore, there is no need to revert it to man'.
It also mentions another angle: even if the claim is like evidence, it cannot be reverted to man' because man' is a request for evidence on a specific premise, and the claim itself is not a premise in that sense.

Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary addresses several key dialectical rules in debate (jadal). It opens with a statement that there is an opinion contrary to what was previously presented regarding pausing in refutation (naqd) and opposition (mu'arada), but for these two, pausing is obligatory. Then it reports another view that it is permissible to refute a ruling claimed to be self-evident (badihi), because such a refutation essentially becomes a denial of self-evidence backed by evidence. However, there is a consideration (nazar) with this view. The commentary then explains the two opinions about the objector (mani') pausing until the proponent (mu'allil) completes his proof. The first opinion (considered good) is that the objector should wait because the proponent might establish the premise after completion, making the objection unnecessary. The second opinion is that the objector should not wait, because the proponent often fails and then switches to another proof, lengthening the debate. The author chooses the first as superior because the proponent's default state is to provide proof. Then the passage explicitly states that pausing is obligatory in naqd and mu'arada by agreement, explaining that naqd is speech on the proof so not directed until completion, and mu'arada is confronting proof with proof, so it is not realized before the proof is complete. The discussion then turns to the inclusion of 'solution' (hal) under objection (man'). Hal is intended to pinpoint the place of error due to poor understanding, and although it differs from man' in seeking the location of error rather than a premise, it is included under man' because it also engages with a specific premise. The passage then critiques the view that refuting a self-evident claim reverts to denying self-evidence; it argues that such a refutation could be classified as genuine naqd or even mu'arada, and there is no need to revert it to man'. Finally, it addresses a potential objection that there are more than three types of questions (man', naqd, mu'arada) since hal is used, but the author responds that hal is included in man' due to appropriate similarity.

Refuting a claim of self-evidence is permissible, but its classification as man', naqd, or mu'arada is subject to scholarly debate, with implications for procedural rules in dialectic.

Inclusion of al-Hall under al-Man' in Jadal
Author argues why al-Hall is included under al-Man' in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Hall is considered a subtype of al-Man' in jadal and distinguish the contexts in which each is used.

Core Idea
In dialectic, every objection targets a premise; al-Hall and al-Man' differ in intent but share structural premise-focus.

The passage addresses a subtle point: why is al-Hall (specifying the error) not a separate category of question? The answer lies in its shared focus on a specific premise with al-Man' (demanding proof). Here we dissect the reasoning behind this inclusion and learn how jadal categorizes objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three standard question-types
According to the passage, the three standard questions in jadal are al-Man', al-Naqd, and al-Mu'arada. Note that al-Hall is not among them.
2
Understand the purpose of al-Man'
Al-Man' is a request for proof on a specific premise. It targets the premise itself, not the whole argument.
3
Understand the purpose of al-Hall
Al-Hall aims to locate and expose the exact error in the opponent's reasoning, often due to misunderstanding. It does not request proof.
4
Find the common ground
Both al-Man' and al-Hall are directed at a specific premise. This is the 'appropriateness' (munasaba) that allows al-Hall to be subsumed under al-Man'.
The passage says 'due to a kind of appropriateness'.
5
Recognize the difference
Al-Hall is not a request for proof; it points out error. The passage says it differs 'bi-wajh' (in one aspect) – the request vs. exposition.

(1) The questioner may claim the self-evidence of a ruling. For example, he claims that the world's being temporally originated is self-evident. Then the questioner prevents this claim with a support, such as saying: 'The world's being temporally originated is not self-evident, because if it were self-evident, scholars would not have disagreed about it. But they disagreed, so it is not self-evident. Rather, it is theoretical.' If this occurs, is this prevention by the questioner permissible or not?
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Rashidiyya dealing with the functions of the questioner (sa'il) in dialectical debate (jadal). It opens by asserting that 'al-Hall' (identifying the point of error) falls under 'al-Man'' (prevention/objection) due to a kind of appropriateness, even though they differ in aspect. The appropriateness is that both involve directing attention to a specific premise. The difference is that al-Man' seeks a proof for a premise, while al-Hall aims to expose an error resulting from poor understanding.

The commentator then discusses the concept of 'badaha' (self-evidence) and its role. He notes that when a questioner claims a ruling to be self-evident, that claim itself can be seen as a proof. The counterpart's response—challenging this claim with a support (sanad)—can be categorized either as a 'naqd' (counterexample) if it targets the proof, or as a 'mu'arada' (counter-argument) if it sets up a competing proof. The text shows that such a response is not merely a man' (objection) because the claim of self-evidence is itself a proof. The commentator points out that forcing it into the category of man' is problematic, as man' asks for proof on a particular premise, whereas the claim of self-evidence is not a premise but a whole claim.

The passage then addresses a possible question: since the questioner may use al-Hall to point out an error, why are there only three types of questions? The answer is that al-Hall is subsumed under al-Man' because both deal with specific premises, even though their purposes differ. Finally, the footnote provides a concrete example from kalam: one might claim the world's temporal origination is self-evident; the opponent then argues that if it were self-evident, scholars would not have disagreed, but they did, so it is not self-evident. This shows how a claim of badaha is countered by a sanad, and how such a counter can be seen as naqd or mu'arada.

المنع

Key Takeaway
In jadal, al-Hall is subsumed under al-Man' because both objections operate on a specific premise, even though al-Hall focuses on error while al-Man' demands proof.

Handling Claims of Badaha (Self-Evidence) in Dialectical Debate
Author shows how a claim of badaha is countered by sanad and classified as naqd or mu'arada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize a response to a claim of badaha as naqd or mu'arada, and explain why it cannot be treated as a simple man'.

Core Idea
A claim of self-evidence functions as a proof; countering it is either a counterexample (naqd) or a counter-argument (mu'arada), not a mere objection (man').

This passage explores what happens when a debater claims a ruling is self-evident (badihi). Instead of demanding proof (man'), the opponent can respond with a support (sanad) that undermines the claim of self-evidence. Here we learn how to classify that response according to the categories of naqd and mu'arada, and why man' is inappropriate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of badaha
A questioner asserts that a certain ruling is self-evident, e.g., 'The world's temporal origination is self-evident.' This claim is treated as a proof in itself.
2
Recognize the opponent's response (sanad)
The opponent provides a support to show the ruling is not self-evident, e.g., 'If it were self-evident, scholars would not have disagreed. But they did.'
3
Determine if the response fits man'
Man' is a request for proof on a specific premise. But the claim of badaha is not a premise; it is a holistic claim. So man' does not apply.
4
Categorize as naqd or mu'arada
If the opponent's support targets the proof underlying the badaha claim, it is naqd (counterexample). If it sets up a competing proof, it is mu'arada (counter-argument). The passage shows both possibilities.

ويَنْدَرجُ الحَل في المنعِ لِنَوعِ مُناسبة وإنْ خَالَفَهُ بِوَجْهِ ، إِذْ قَدْ يُقْصَدُ بِهِ تَفْسِيدُ مَوْضِعِ الغَلَطِ لِسُوء الفهم .
البداهة مع السند وهو ما ذكر لإثبات نقيضه ) وفيه نظر لإمكان إرجاعه إلى النقض، بل إلى المعارضة أيضا، كذا في الحاشية . والحاصل أن ما ذكره الناقض يمكن أن يجعل من أفراد النقض الحقيقي بأن يقال : دعوى بداهة دليل على دعواه ، والنقض في الحقيقة راجع إلى ذلك الدليل، وكذا يمكن أن يكون من أفراد المعارضة بأن يكون الدليل المثبت للنقض معارضا لدعوى البداهة التي هي بمنزلة نصب الدليل فلا وجه لإرجاعه إلى منع البداهة مع صحة كونه من أفراد النقض، ولا لاختياره على المعارضة ، ويمكن أن يوجه النظر بوجه آخر ، وهو أنه وإن سلم كون دعوى البداهة بمنزلة الدليل، لكن لا يجوز إرجاعه إلى المنع ، إذ هو طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة ولا يطلب على مقدمة الدعوى شيء كما لا يخفى (1) . ثم لما كان ههنا سؤال : وهو أنه قد يسأل السائل بالحل ، أعني تعيين موضع من الغلط ، فلا يصح يصح حصر الأسئلة في الثلاثة المذكورة ، فأجاب بقوله : ( ويندرج الحل في المنع لنوع مناسبة ) يعني من حيث هو تعرض للمقدمة المعينة ، كما كان المنع كذلك ، ( وإن خالفه بوجه ، إذ يقصد به أي بالحل و تعيين موضع الغلط ) لسوء الفهم، لا طلب الدليل .
لأنه كثيرا ما يعجز المعلل عن إثبات مقدمات دليله حتى لا يطول وقت المناظرة . وأما النقض في النقض والمعارضة فإن الواجب أن يتوقف الناقض - لأن النقض موجه على الدليل فلا يصح التوجه عليه قبل تمامه . . وكذا يتوقف المعارض حتى يتم المعلل دليله وإلا لا يصح اتجاه المعارضة قبل إتمام الدليل لأنه مقابلة دليل بدليل ولا تتم هذه المقابلة إلا بتمام دليل المبلل.
(۱) قد يدعي السائل بداهة حكم من الأحكام. كأن يدعي بأن كون العالم حادث بديهي . ثم يمنع السائل ادعايه هذا بسند كأن يقول : ليس كون العالم حادثا بديهيا لأنه لو كان بديهيا لما اختلف فيه العلماء . لكنهم اختلفوا فلا يكون بديهيا . بل يكون نظريا . إذا حصل هذا هل يكون منع السائل هذا جائز أولا .
And al-Hall is included under al-Man' due to a kind of appropriateness, even if it differs from it in one aspect, because it may be intended by it to corrupt the place of error due to poor understanding. Self-evidence with the support, and it is what is mentioned to prove its opposite) and in it there is a consideration because it is possible to refer it to al-Naqd, or even to al-Mu'arada as well, as in the gloss. And the summary is that what the objector mentioned can be made one of the instances of true Naqd by saying: The claim of self-evidence is a proof for his claim, and the Naqd in reality refers to that proof, and likewise it can be one of the instances of Mu'arada by making the proof establishing the Naqd a counter-argument to the claim of self-evidence which is like the setting up of a proof, so there is no basis for referring it to Man' of self-evidence while it is correct that it is one of the instances of Naqd, nor for preferring it over Mu'arada. And it is possible to direct the consideration in another way: that even if it is granted that the claim of self-evidence is like a proof, it is not permissible to refer it to Man', because it is a request for a proof on a specific premise, and nothing is requested on the premise of the claim, as is not hidden (1). Then, since here there is a question: that the questioner may ask by al-Hall, meaning specifying the place of error, so it is not correct to limit the questions to the three mentioned, so he answered by his saying: 'And al-Hall is included under al-Man' due to a kind of appropriateness' meaning insofar as it is an engagement with the specific premise, just as al-Man' is, (even if it differs in one aspect, because what is intended by it, i.e., by al-Hall and specifying the place of error, is for poor understanding, not a request for proof. Because often the definer is unable to prove the premises of his proof so that the time of debate may not be prolonged. As for al-Naqd in al-Naqd and al-Mu'arada, the objector must pause – because al-Naqd is directed at the proof, so it is not valid to direct it before its completion. And likewise the counter-argumenter pauses until the definer completes his proof, lest the direction of al-Mu'arada before completion of the proof be invalid, because it is a matching of proof with proof, and this matching is not completed except with the complete proof of the definer. (1) The questioner may claim the self-evidence of a ruling. For example, he claims that the world's being temporally originated is self-evident. Then the questioner prevents this claim with a support, such as saying: 'The world's being temporally originated is not self-evident, because if it were self-evident, scholars would not have disagreed about it. But they disagreed, so it is not self-evident. Rather, it is theoretical.' If this occurs, is this prevention by the questioner permissible or not?
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Rashidiyya dealing with the functions of the questioner (sa'il) in dialectical debate (jadal). It opens by asserting that 'al-Hall' (identifying the point of error) falls under 'al-Man'' (prevention/objection) due to a kind of appropriateness, even though they differ in aspect. The appropriateness is that both involve directing attention to a specific premise. The difference is that al-Man' seeks a proof for a premise, while al-Hall aims to expose an error resulting from poor understanding.

The commentator then discusses the concept of 'badaha' (self-evidence) and its role. He notes that when a questioner claims a ruling to be self-evident, that claim itself can be seen as a proof. The counterpart's response—challenging this claim with a support (sanad)—can be categorized either as a 'naqd' (counterexample) if it targets the proof, or as a 'mu'arada' (counter-argument) if it sets up a competing proof. The text shows that such a response is not merely a man' (objection) because the claim of self-evidence is itself a proof. The commentator points out that forcing it into the category of man' is problematic, as man' asks for proof on a particular premise, whereas the claim of self-evidence is not a premise but a whole claim.

The passage then addresses a possible question: since the questioner may use al-Hall to point out an error, why are there only three types of questions? The answer is that al-Hall is subsumed under al-Man' because both deal with specific premises, even though their purposes differ. Finally, the footnote provides a concrete example from kalam: one might claim the world's temporal origination is self-evident; the opponent then argues that if it were self-evident, scholars would not have disagreed, but they did, so it is not self-evident. This shows how a claim of badaha is countered by a sanad, and how such a counter can be seen as naqd or mu'arada.

Key Terms
البداهة
السند
النقض
المعارضة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot a response to a claim of badaha be classified as man'?
A) Because man' is only for premises, and the claim of badaha is a premise.
B) Because man' requests proof on a specific premise, but the claim of badaha is the whole claim, not a separate premise.
C) Because man' is used only for definitions.
D) Because the opponent does not have the right to object.

Key Takeaway
A claim of self-evidence in jadal is treated as a proof; countering it requires naqd or mu'arada, not a simple man'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A debater claims it is self-evident that every act requires a divine command. How would you object using a sanad? Categorize your objection as naqd or mu'arada and justify based on the passage.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Conditions of the Correct Support (Al-Sanad al-Sahih)
Author defines the correct support and states its condition of non-generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given support is 'correct' by checking if it is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise and if it is not more general than the premise.

Core Idea
A correct support must be a necessary condition, not just any proof, and cannot be more general.

In this section, the author defines the 'correct support' (al-sanad al-sahih) as something that is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise and strengthens the prevention, even if only according to the preventer's claim. Crucially, the support cannot be more general than the premise it supports, either absolutely or in some respect, otherwise it fails to be a necessary condition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of correct support
Find the sentence that defines al-sanad al-sahih. The author states: 'the correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention.'
2
Identify the two key functions
The support has two functions: it is a necessary condition (malzum) for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention (man').
These are not independent; the support's strength comes from its necessity.
3
Understand the subjective qualification
The author adds 'even if only in the claim of the preventer', meaning the support does not have to be objectively true, only that the preventer claims it serves as a necessary condition and strengthens.
This prevents the opponent from demanding absolute proof.
4
State the prohibition on generality
The support cannot be absolutely more general (a'mm mutlaq) nor more general in some respect (a'mm min wajh) than the premise.
The commentator clarifies the grammatical attachment of 'mutlaqan' to emphasize both types are prohibited.
5
Test with an example
If the support is 'animal' and the premise is 'human', animal can exist without human, so it is absolutely more general and fails the condition. Similarly, 'a'mm min wajh' cannot be a necessary condition from every respect.
Thus the support must be coextensive or narrower than the premise.

Source Text

من جُمْلَةِ المَعْلُومِ أنَّ السَّنَدَ الصَّحِيحَ مَلْزُومٌ لِحَفَاءِ المُقدِّمة، ومُقو للمَنْع ولو يزَعْمِ المَانِعِ ، فَلا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يكونَ أَعَمَّ مُطلقًا.
وقوله - لسوء الفهم - متعلق بالغلط ، وقد يذكر الحل في مقابلة المنع لهذه المخالفة (1) .
من جملة المعلوم أن السند الصحيح ملزوم لخفاء المقدمة ، ومقو للمنع ولو ) كان ملزوميته وتقويته ( بزعم المانع ، فلا يجوز أن يكون ) السند الصحيح ( أعم ) من المقدمة الممنوعة ( مطلقا ) يجوز أن يكون مطلقا متعلقا بقوله - فلا يكون - فيكون المعنى لا يكون أعم لا مطلقا ولا من وجه ويجوز أن يكون متعلقا بقوله - أعم - فيكون المعنى - لا يكون أعم مطلقا لا من وجه - والظاهر الموافق بالسياق الأول ، لأن الأعم من وجه لا يكون ملزوما ومقويا من كل وجه (٢)
لكون المدعي لم يقم دليلا على بداهة الحكم ؟ فذهب علماء المناظرة إلى أن مثل هذا المنع جائز . لكن على أنه نقض . ويكون دليله سندا . أو على أنه معارضة . ويكون دليله دليلا آخر على خلاف ما ادعاه الخصم . وهذا معنى المعارضة وفي كلتا الحالتين تعتبر دعوى البداهة كدليل للمعلل على كون الحكم بديهيا . (۱) من وظائف السائل الحل وهو تعيين موضع من الغلط في دعوى المدعي أو دليله وعلى هذا تكون وظائف السائل أربعة لا ثلاثة - كما ذكرنا - وهي المنع والنقض والمعارضة . ثم يزاد عليها الحل تكون أربعة ، لكن المصنف لم يجعل الحل وظيفة بل جعله راجعا إلى المنع فيكون وظائف السائل كما هي ثلاثة لا أربعة . لأن اهي كلا من المنع والحل تعرض للمقدمة وإن اختلفا من ناحية أخرى.

Fifth Section: It is among the known things that the correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention, even if [only] in the claim of the preventer. So it is not permissible for it to be absolutely more general. And his saying – 'due to poor understanding' – is connected to the error, and al-Hall may be mentioned in contrast to al-Man' for this difference (1). It is among the known things that the correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention, even if its being a necessary condition and its strengthening are according to the preventer's claim. So it is not permissible for the correct support to be more general than the prevented premise absolutely. It is possible for 'absolutely' to be connected to his saying 'so it is not', so the meaning is: it is not more general, neither absolutely nor in some respect. And it is possible for it to be connected to his saying 'more general', so the meaning is: it is not absolutely more general, not [even] in some respect. The apparent meaning agrees with the first context, because that which is more general in some respect cannot be a necessary condition and strengthening from every respect (2). Since the claimant did not present a proof for the self-evidence of the ruling? So the scholars of dialectic held that such a prevention is permissible, but on the basis that it is a naqd, and its proof is a support, or that it is a mu'arada, and its proof is another proof contrary to what the opponent claimed. This is the meaning of mu'arada. In both cases, the claim of self-evidence is considered a proof for the definer that the ruling is self-evident. (1) Among the functions of the questioner is al-Hall, which is specifying the place of error in the claimant's claim or proof. On this basis, the functions of the questioner are four, not three – as we mentioned – namely al-Man', al-Naqd, and al-Mu'arada; then al-Hall is added to them, making four. But the author did not make al-Hall a function; rather, he made it revert to al-Man', so the functions of the questioner remain three, not four, because each of al-Man' and al-Hall engages with the premise, even if they differ in another respect. (2) The support is what is mentioned to strengthen the prevention. That is not necessary in reality, but only according to what the preventer thinks. And since al-Man' is a request for proof on a specific premise, when a support is mentioned with this prevention, its mention is like a proof for the concealment of the premise. The proof is a necessary condition and the claim is necessary. For this reason, the author said: The correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise and strengthens the prevention. And if the support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, it is not correct for it to be absolutely more general, nor more general in some respect – because the existence of the more general does not necessitate the existence of the more specific, like animal and human: animal may exist without human. As for the more general in some respect, it may exist –
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is the fifth section of the Rashidiyya commentary and addresses the nature of the 'correct support' (al-sanad al-sahih) in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating a known principle: the correct support must be a necessary condition (malzum) for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention (man'), even if this necessity and strengthening are only according to the preventer's own claim. This means that the support is not intrinsically linked to the premise in reality, but it functions as a justification that the premise is not evident. Consequently, the support cannot be more general than the premise it supports, neither absolutely (a'mm mutlaq) nor in some respect (a'mm min wajh). The commentator explains the different grammatical attachments of 'mutlaqan' (absolutely) to clarify that the support's generality must not exceed the premise's scope.

The passage then revisits the earlier discussion of badaha: if a claimant asserts a ruling is self-evident but provides no proof, scholars allow a prevention (man') but consider it as either a naqd (counterexample) with the support as proof, or a mu'arada (counter-argument) with a different proof. In both cases, the claim of badaha is treated as a proof that the ruling is self-evident. This connects back to the functions of the questioner: al-Hall (specifying the error) could be seen as a fourth function, but the author subsumes it under al-Man' because both involve engaging with a specific premise, even if their aims differ.

The footnote (2) elaborates on the definition of sanad: it is what is mentioned to strengthen the man', but this strengthening is only in the preventer's estimation, not necessarily objectively true. The author gives a logical example: if the support is more general (e.g., 'animal') than the premise (e.g., 'human'), it cannot be a necessary condition because the general can exist without the specific. The same applies to 'a'mm min wajh', where the relationship is not one of necessity from every aspect. Thus, the support must be coextensive or narrower than the premise to serve as a genuine necessary condition.

Key Terms
السند الصحيح
المقدمة
الأعم المطلق
الأعم من وجه

C) Because the sanad must be exactly identical to the premise.

Key Takeaway

Functions of the Questioner: Naqd, Mu'arada, and Al-Hall
Author explains responses to badaha claims and the functions of the questioner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the dialectical functions of naqd, mu'arada, and man' and explain how al-Hall is subsumed under man'.

Core Idea
When a claim is asserted as self-evident, the questioner responds with naqd or mu'arada, and al-Hall is not a separate function.

This section addresses the situation where a claimant asserts a ruling is self-evident (badaha) without providing proof. The scholars of dialectic hold that the questioner's prevention (man') is permissible but is treated as either a naqd (counterexample) or a mu'arada (counter-argument). The passage also discusses whether al-Hall (specifying the error) is a separate function, concluding that the author subsumes it under man'.

Guided Path
1
Set the scenario
The claimant asserts a ruling is self-evident (badaha) without providing proof. This triggers a questioner's response.
Badaha means the ruling is obvious and needs no proof.
2
Identify the questioner's possible responses
Scholars allow the questioner to perform man' (prevention), but they treat it as either naqd (counterexample) with its own proof as support, or mu'arada (counter-argument) with a different proof.
The man' is not simply a denial; it is a structured objection.
3
Understand the role of badaha claim
In both naqd and mu'arada, the claim of badaha is considered a proof for the definer (the claimant) that the ruling is self-evident.
This means the opponent's response implicitly acknowledges the claimant's position.
4
Analyze the functions of the questioner
The main functions are man', naqd, and mu'arada. Some add al-Hall (specifying the error in the claimant's claim or proof), but the author argues that al-Hall reverts to man' because both engage with the same premise.
Thus the functions remain three, not four.

Source Text
