المَبْحثُ الخَامِسُ
من جُمْلَةِ المَعْلُومِ أنَّ السَّنَدَ الصَّحِيحَ مَلْزُومٌ لِحَفَاءِ المُقدِّمة، ومُقو للمَنْع ولو يزَعْمِ المَانِعِ ، فَلا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يكونَ أَعَمَّ مُطلقًا.
وقوله - لسوء الفهم - متعلق بالغلط ، وقد يذكر الحل في مقابلة المنع لهذه المخالفة (1) .
من جملة المعلوم أن السند الصحيح ملزوم لخفاء المقدمة ، ومقو للمنع ولو ) كان ملزوميته وتقويته ( بزعم المانع ، فلا يجوز أن يكون ) السند الصحيح ( أعم ) من المقدمة الممنوعة ( مطلقا ) يجوز أن يكون مطلقا متعلقا بقوله - فلا يكون - فيكون المعنى لا يكون أعم لا مطلقا ولا من وجه ويجوز أن يكون متعلقا بقوله - أعم - فيكون المعنى - لا يكون أعم مطلقا لا من وجه - والظاهر الموافق بالسياق الأول ، لأن الأعم من وجه لا يكون ملزوما ومقويا من كل وجه (٢)
لكون المدعي لم يقم دليلا على بداهة الحكم ؟ فذهب علماء المناظرة إلى أن مثل هذا المنع جائز . لكن على أنه نقض . ويكون دليله سندا . أو على أنه معارضة . ويكون دليله دليلا آخر على خلاف ما ادعاه الخصم . وهذا معنى المعارضة وفي كلتا الحالتين تعتبر دعوى البداهة كدليل للمعلل على كون الحكم بديهيا . (۱) من وظائف السائل الحل وهو تعيين موضع من الغلط في دعوى المدعي أو دليله وعلى هذا تكون وظائف السائل أربعة لا ثلاثة - كما ذكرنا - وهي المنع والنقض والمعارضة . ثم يزاد عليها الحل تكون أربعة ، لكن المصنف لم يجعل الحل وظيفة بل جعله راجعا إلى المنع فيكون وظائف السائل كما هي ثلاثة لا أربعة . لأن اهي كلا من المنع والحل تعرض للمقدمة وإن اختلفا من ناحية أخرى.
(۲) السند ما يذكر لتقوية المنع . وليس ذلك بلازم في الواقع . بل ولو بحسب ما يراه المانع ولما كان المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة . فإذا ذكر مع هذا المنع منذ كان ذكره بمثابة الدليل على خفاء المقدمة . والدليل ملزوم والدعوى لازمة . ولهذا قال المصنف : السند الصحيح ملزوم لاخفاء المقدمة ومقر للمنع، وإذا كان السند ملزوما لخفاء المقدمة فلا يصح أن يكون أعم مطلقا ولا من وجه - لأنه لا يلزم من وجود الأعد - وجود الأخص كالحيوان والإنسان فقد يوجد الحيوان بدون الإنسان . وأما الأعم من وجه فقد يوجد -
Fifth Section: It is among the known things that the correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention, even if [only] in the claim of the preventer. So it is not permissible for it to be absolutely more general. And his saying – 'due to poor understanding' – is connected to the error, and al-Hall may be mentioned in contrast to al-Man' for this difference (1). It is among the known things that the correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention, even if its being a necessary condition and its strengthening are according to the preventer's claim. So it is not permissible for the correct support to be more general than the prevented premise absolutely. It is possible for 'absolutely' to be connected to his saying 'so it is not', so the meaning is: it is not more general, neither absolutely nor in some respect. And it is possible for it to be connected to his saying 'more general', so the meaning is: it is not absolutely more general, not [even] in some respect. The apparent meaning agrees with the first context, because that which is more general in some respect cannot be a necessary condition and strengthening from every respect (2). Since the claimant did not present a proof for the self-evidence of the ruling? So the scholars of dialectic held that such a prevention is permissible, but on the basis that it is a naqd, and its proof is a support, or that it is a mu'arada, and its proof is another proof contrary to what the opponent claimed. This is the meaning of mu'arada. In both cases, the claim of self-evidence is considered a proof for the definer that the ruling is self-evident. (1) Among the functions of the questioner is al-Hall, which is specifying the place of error in the claimant's claim or proof. On this basis, the functions of the questioner are four, not three – as we mentioned – namely al-Man', al-Naqd, and al-Mu'arada; then al-Hall is added to them, making four. But the author did not make al-Hall a function; rather, he made it revert to al-Man', so the functions of the questioner remain three, not four, because each of al-Man' and al-Hall engages with the premise, even if they differ in another respect. (2) The support is what is mentioned to strengthen the prevention. That is not necessary in reality, but only according to what the preventer thinks. And since al-Man' is a request for proof on a specific premise, when a support is mentioned with this prevention, its mention is like a proof for the concealment of the premise. The proof is a necessary condition and the claim is necessary. For this reason, the author said: The correct support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise and strengthens the prevention. And if the support is a necessary condition for the concealment of the premise, it is not correct for it to be absolutely more general, nor more general in some respect – because the existence of the more general does not necessitate the existence of the more specific, like animal and human: animal may exist without human. As for the more general in some respect, it may exist –
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is the fifth section of the Rashidiyya commentary and addresses the nature of the 'correct support' (al-sanad al-sahih) in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating a known principle: the correct support must be a necessary condition (malzum) for the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the prevention (man'), even if this necessity and strengthening are only according to the preventer's own claim. This means that the support is not intrinsically linked to the premise in reality, but it functions as a justification that the premise is not evident. Consequently, the support cannot be more general than the premise it supports, neither absolutely (a'mm mutlaq) nor in some respect (a'mm min wajh). The commentator explains the different grammatical attachments of 'mutlaqan' (absolutely) to clarify that the support's generality must not exceed the premise's scope.

The passage then revisits the earlier discussion of badaha: if a claimant asserts a ruling is self-evident but provides no proof, scholars allow a prevention (man') but consider it as either a naqd (counterexample) with the support as proof, or a mu'arada (counter-argument) with a different proof. In both cases, the claim of badaha is treated as a proof that the ruling is self-evident. This connects back to the functions of the questioner: al-Hall (specifying the error) could be seen as a fourth function, but the author subsumes it under al-Man' because both involve engaging with a specific premise, even if their aims differ.

The footnote (2) elaborates on the definition of sanad: it is what is mentioned to strengthen the man', but this strengthening is only in the preventer's estimation, not necessarily objectively true. The author gives a logical example: if the support is more general (e.g., 'animal') than the premise (e.g., 'human'), it cannot be a necessary condition because the general can exist without the specific. The same applies to 'a'mm min wajh', where the relationship is not one of necessity from every aspect. Thus, the support must be coextensive or narrower than the premise to serve as a genuine necessary condition.

Key Terms
المنع
النقض
المعارضة
الحل
السند الصحيح
المقدمة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between al-Hall and al-Man' in the functions of the questioner?
A) al-Hall is a separate fourth function.
B) al-Hall is subsumed under al-Man'.
C) al-Hall is a type of naqd.
D) al-Hall is not a function at all.

Key Takeaway
The questioner's functions are three: man', naqd, mu'arada; al-Hall is not separate but falls under man'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, the claimant says 'The world is created' and claims it is self-evident. The questioner responds with a counterexample: 'If the world were created, it would have a creator, which is not self-evident.' Identify the function used (naqd or mu'arada) and justify your answer.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Condition of the Sanad in Dialectic Rejection
Author defines the sanad and its condition of being coextensive with the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given sanad meets the condition of being necessarily connected to the concealed premise and not more general.

Core Idea
A valid sanad must be specifically tied to the premise; generality undermines its force.

In dialectical debate, when a premise is rejected (man‘), the objector may provide a sanad (support) to show the premise is not self-evident. However, not every support is valid. The author explains that the sanad must be necessarily connected to the concealment of the premise and cannot be more general. Understanding this condition is crucial for constructing valid objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sanad and the premise
In a debate scenario, find the statement given as support and the premise it is supposed to strengthen. For example, the premise 'Wine is forbidden' might be supported by the sanad 'Wine is an intoxicant'.
The sanad is meant to show the premise is not self-evident.
2
Check if the sanad entails concealment
Determine if the sanad logically implies that the premise is not obvious. The author says a correct sanad is 'necessarily connected' (malzum) to the concealment. This means if the sanad is true, it should make clear that the premise requires proof.
The entailment can be according to the objector's claim.
3
Test the sanad for generality
Ask: is the sanad more general (absolutely or partially) than the premise? The author gives examples: animal is absolutely more general than man; white is more general in some respects relative to man. A more general sanad does not guarantee the premise's concealment.
The existence of the more general does not necessitate the more specific.
4
Reject if more general
If the sanad is more general (absolutely or partially), it fails the condition and is invalid. The author concludes that such a sanad cannot strengthen the objection because it does not entail the premise's concealment.
This is a key rule for evaluating objections in jadal.

Source Text

من جملة المعلوم أن السند الصحيح ملزوم لخفاء المقدمة، ومقوٍ للمنع ولو بزعم المانع، فلا يجوز أن يكون أعم مطلقًا. وقوله - لسوء الفهم - متعلق بالغلط، وقد يذكر الحل في مقابلة المنع لهذه المخالفة. من جملة المعلوم أن السند الصحيح ملزوم لخفاء المقدمة، ومقو للمنع ولو كان ملزوميته وتقويته بزعم المانع، فلا يجوز أن يكون السند الصحيح أعم من المقدمة الممنوعة مطلقًا يجوز أن يكون مطلقًا متعلقًا بقوله - فلا يكون - فيكون المعنى لا يكون أعم لا مطلقًا ولا من وجه، ويجوز أن يكون متعلقًا بقوله - أعم - فيكون المعنى - لا يكون أعم مطلقًا لا من وجه - والظاهر الموافق بالسياق الأول، لأن الأعم من وجه لا يكون ملزومًا ومقوِيًا من كل وجه. لكون المدعي لم يقم دليلا على بداهة الحكم؟ فذهب علماء المناظرة إلى أن مثل هذا المنع جائز. لكن على أنه نقض. ويكون دليله سندًا. أو على أنه معارضة. ويكون دليله دليلاً آخر على خلاف ما ادعاه الخصم. وهذا معنى المعارضة وفي كلتا الحالتين تعتبر دعوى البداهة كدليل للمعلل على كون الحكم بديهيًا. من وظائف السائل الحل وهو تعيين موضع من الغلط في دعوى المدعي أو دليله وعلى هذا تكون وظائف السائل أربعة لا ثلاثة - كما ذكرنا - وهي المنع والنقض والمعارضة. ثم يزاد عليها الحل فتكون أربعة، لكن المصنف لم يجعل الحل وظيفة بل جعله راجعًا إلى المنع فيكون وظائف السائل كما هي ثلاثة لا أربعة. لأن كلاً من المنع والحل تعرض للمقدمة وإن اختلفا من ناحية أخرى. السند ما يذكر لتقوية المنع. وليس ذلك بلازم في الواقع. بل ولو بحسب ما يراه المانع ولما كان المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة. فإذا ذكر مع هذا المنع سند كان ذكره بمثابة الدليل على خفاء المقدمة. والدليل ملزوم والدعوى لازمة. ولهذا قال المصنف: السند الصحيح ملزوم لخفاء المقدمة ومقو للمنع، وإذا كان السند ملزومًا لخفاء المقدمة فلا يصح أن يكون أعم مطلقًا ولا من وجه - لأنه لا يلزم من وجود الأعم وجود الأخص كالحيوان والإنسان فقد يوجد الحيوان بدون الإنسان. وأما الأعم من وجه فقد يوجد أحدهما بدون الآخر. كالإنسان والأبيض. فإن كلا منهما يوجد بدون الآخر. فقد يوجد إنسان وليس بأبيض. كما أنه قد يوجد شيء أبيض وليس بإنسان. ولما كان الغرض من الدليل أنه إذا وجد وجد مدلوله. فإنه لا يجوز أن يكون أعم مطلقًا ولا من وجه.
It is known that the correct support is necessarily connected to the concealment of the premise, and it strengthens the objection even if only in the claim of the objector. So it must not be absolutely more general. His statement 'due to poor understanding' is related to error; and the solution may be mentioned in response to the objection for this violation. It is known that the correct support is necessarily connected to the concealment of the premise and strengthens the objection even if its strict entailment and strengthening are only in the claim of the objector. So the correct support must not be more general than the rejected premise absolutely—it may be that 'absolutely' is attached to 'must not be', so the meaning is 'it must not be more general, neither absolutely nor in some respects', or it may be attached to 'more general', so the meaning is 'it must not be absolutely more general, not in some respects'—and the apparent meaning agrees with the first context because what is more general in some respects cannot be strictly entailing and strengthening in every respect. Because the claimant did not provide proof of the self-evidence of the ruling? So the scholars of debate held that such an objection is permissible. But it is considered a counterexample, and its proof becomes a support. Or it is considered a counter-argument, and its proof becomes another proof contradicting what the opponent claimed. This is the meaning of counter-argument. In both cases, the claim of self-evidence is considered a proof for the reasoner that the ruling is self-evident. Among the functions of the questioner is the solution, which is identifying the location of error in the claimant's assertion or proof. Accordingly, the functions of the questioner are four, not three—as we mentioned—namely: objection, counterexample, and counter-argument. Then solution is added to them, so they become four. But the author did not make solution a separate function; instead, he made it revert to objection, so the functions of the questioner remain three, not four. Because both objection and solution address the premise, even if they differ in other respects. The support is that which is mentioned to strengthen the objection. And that is not necessary in reality, but only according to what the objector claims. Since the objection is a request for proof regarding a specific premise, when a support is mentioned with this objection, its mention serves as proof of the concealment of the premise. The proof entails, and the claim is entailed. Therefore the author said: the correct support entails the concealment of the premise and strengthens the objection. And if the support entails the concealment of the premise, it is not valid for it to be absolutely more general or more general in some respects—because the existence of the more general does not necessitate the existence of the more specific, like animal and man: animal may exist without man. As for what is more general in some respects, one may exist without the other, like man and white: each may exist without the other. Man may exist not white, and something white may exist not a man. And since the purpose of the proof is that if it exists, its conclusion exists, it is not permissible for it to be absolutely more general or more general in some respects.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the nature and condition of the 'sanad' (support) in dialectical debate. The author first states the principle: a correct sanad is necessarily tied to the premise being concealed, and it strengthens the objection even if only in the objector's claim. Because of this, the sanad must not be absolutely more general than the premise. The commentator then explains the ambiguities in the phrasing: whether 'absolutely' negates both absolute generality and partial generality, or only absolute generality, and opts for the first interpretation because partial generality cannot entail and strengthen in every respect. The discussion then shifts to the functions of the questioner: objection, counterexample, counter-argument, and solution. The commentator notes that while some count four functions, the author considers solution as part of objection, thus keeping three. The remainder of the passage elaborates on the definition of sanad: it is something mentioned to strengthen the objection, even if not actually true, and serves as proof of the premise's non-obviousness. Using examples like animal/man and human/white, the commentator illustrates why a sanad cannot be more general (either absolutely or partially): the more general can exist without the more specific, so it would not entail the concealed premise. The condition is grounded in the logical requirement that a proof must necessitate its conclusion.

Functions of the Questioner in Dialectic
Author discusses the functions of the questioner and subsumes solution under objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three (or four) functions of the questioner in dialectic and compare the author's classification to alternative views.

Core Idea
The questioner has three core functions: objection, counterexample, and counter-argument; solution is subsumed under objection.

In dialectical debate, the questioner (sā'il) has specific tools to challenge the respondent. This passage lists four functions: man' (objection), naqd (counterexample), mu'āraḍah (counter-argument), and ḥall (solution). However, the author subsumes ḥall under man', reducing the count to three. Understanding this classification helps in organizing one's objections effectively.

Guided Path
1
List the four functions as given by some scholars
The passage mentions man', naqd, mu'āraḍah, and ḥall. Recognize each as a distinct tool of the questioner.
Some authorities count four separate functions.
2
Define each function briefly
Man' is rejection of a premise. Naqd is a counterexample that challenges the premise using the opponent's own logic. Mu'āraḍah is a counter-argument with a different proof. Ḥall is identifying the location of error in the opponent's claim or proof.
These definitions are standard in jadal.
3
Identify the author's position
The author argues that ḥall is not a separate function but is part of man'. He reasons that both man' and ḥall address the premise, differing only in perspective.
Thus the author's count is three: man' (including ḥall), naqd, and mu'āraḍah.
4
Evaluate the rationale
Consider why merging ḥall with man' might be justified. Both involve challenging the premise; ḥall specifically points out the error while man' simply denies. The author considers this a unified operation.
This shows how classifications can be refined in Islamic logic.

The author first states the principle: a correct sanad is necessarily tied to the premise being concealed, and it strengthens the objection even if only in the objector's claim. Because of this, the sanad must not be absolutely more general than the premise. The commentator then explains the ambiguities in the phrasing: whether 'absolutely' negates both absolute generality and partial generality, or only absolute generality, and opts for the first interpretation because partial generality cannot entail and strengthen in every respect. The discussion then shifts to the functions of the questioner: objection, counterexample, counter-argument, and solution. The commentator notes that while some count four functions, the author considers solution as part of objection, thus keeping three. The remainder of the passage elaborates on the definition of sanad: it is something mentioned to strengthen the objection, even if not actually true, and serves as proof of the premise's non-obviousness. Using examples like animal/man and human/white, the commentator illustrates why a sanad cannot be more general (either absolutely or partially): the more general can exist without the more specific, so it would not entail the concealed premise. The condition is grounded in the logical requirement that a proof must necessitate its conclusion.

نقض
معارضة
حل
سائل

The questioner's functions are primarily three: man', naqd, and mu'āraḍah; ḥall is subsumed under man'.

Responding to Sophistical Objections in Dialectic
Author states that any premise can be objected to sophistically and lists response phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a sophistical objection in a dialectical exchange and respond using the standard phrases listed by the author.

Core Idea
Any premise can be sophistically denied, but the true sage dismisses it as mere stubbornness.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), disputants sometimes reject a premise without evidence, relying on the doctrines of the Sophists who deny reality. This passage from al-Rashidiyya teaches us that such objections are always possible, but they are not taken seriously by genuine scholars. The author then provides the typical phrases used after such an objection, like 'why is it not permissible?' and 'how not?' Understanding these conventions helps you navigate debates and distinguish valid objections from mere stubbornness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author states that every premise can be objected to using sophistical support. Why is this significant?
This implies that sophistry is a universal threat to rational discourse.
2
Recognize the sage's stance
The sage (al-ḥakīm) considers such objections as stubbornness (mukābarah). What does this tell us about the value of sophistical objections?
They are not considered valid or worth serious engagement.
3
List the standard response phrases
Find the three main phrases: 'why is it not permissible?', 'why is it not such?', and 'how not?' with wāw al-ḥāl. Understand each.
These phrases are used to dismiss the objection by questioning its necessity.
4
Note the rare use of 'innamā'
The author mentions that sometimes 'innamā' is used, but it's rare. Why might that be?
It implies a conditional that is too strong for a mere sophistical objection.

Source Text

And from here they said: There is no premise except that it can be objected to, supported by what the Sophists went to. But the sage considers it stubbornness. And in most cases, after it he mentions: 'Why is it not permissible?' or 'Why is it not such?' and 'How not?' with the waaw of the situation. From here, i.e., because the support is necessary and strengthening, they said: i.e., the people of speculation: There is no premise existing in any state except that it is possible to object to it, supported by what the Sophists went to—the deniers of the reality of things. But the sage who affirms reality considers it, i.e., that support, to be stubbornness and not listened to. And in most cases, i.e., in most times of objection with support, after it, i.e., after the objection, he says: 'Why is it not permissible?' as in: 'What you mentioned is objected to; why is it not permissible for it to be such?' and 'Why is it not?' as in: 'This is objected to; why is it not such?' and 'How not?' with the waaw of the situation, i.e., accompanied by the word 'how not' with the waaw, as in: 'That is not accepted; how not, since the matter is such.' Commentary
This passage is an expansion on a statement about the pervasiveness of sophistical objection in dialectic. It begins with the claim that any premise can be objected to using the doctrines of the Sophists (those who deny the reality of things). However, a genuine philosopher (al-ḥakīm) regards such supports as mere stubbornness (mukābarah) and not worthy of acceptance. The passage then lists the common phrases used after a supported objection: 'why is it not permissible?' (lima lā yajūz?), 'why is it not such?' (lima lā yakūnu kadhā?), and 'how not?' The phrase 'how not' indicates a contradiction with a known fact. Additionally, the use of 'innamā' is mentioned but rare, as in 'we do not accept that premise; it would be such only if such-and-such.' The author's phrase 'in most cases' indicates that these phrases are common but not universal. The overall lesson is that sophistical objection is always possible, but it is not considered valid in serious discourse.

The Universal Possibility of Sophistical Objection
Commentary explains that any premise can be objected to with a sophistical basis, but the philosopher dismisses it as obstinacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a legitimate objection and sophistical obstinacy in a dialectical exchange, identifying when an objection is based on a valid support versus mere stubborn denial.

Core Idea
Any premise can be sophistically denied, but the philosopher rejects such denial as obstinacy, not argument.

In dialectical debate, the opponent can always raise an objection, but not all objections are valid. This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary explains a key boundary: the Sophists provide a theoretical basis to deny any premise, yet the philosopher dismisses such denial as mere obstinacy. Understanding this distinction protects sound reasoning from being overwhelmed by baseless challenges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Find the statement that 'there is no premise except that it is possible to object to it based on what the Sophists adhered to.' This is the universal claim about the possibility of objection.
The term 'sanad' (support) is crucial—here it means the basis for the objection.
2
Understand the Sophists' Position
The Sophists (السوفسطائية) are defined as those who deny the reality of things (ثبوت حقائق الأشياء). They provide a blanket reason to reject any premise.
This is an extreme skeptical stance, not a reasoned argument.
3
Analyze the Philosopher's Response
The philosopher (الحكيم) who affirms the reality of things considers such an objection to be mere obstinacy (مكابرة) and does not accept it as a valid move in debate.
The passage says 'the philosopher considers it obstinacy that is not heeded.'
4
Apply the Distinction
In a debate, check whether an objection is grounded in a real support (e.g., a counter-premise or evidence) or is simply a refusal to accept. If it relies on the sophistical denial of reality, it is inadmissible.
This prevents debates from being derailed by baseless denials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ هَهُنا قالوا : مَا مِنْ مُقَدِّمَة إِلَّا  َويُمْكِنُ مَنْعُه مُسْتَنَدًا بمَا ذَهَبَ إلَيهِ السوفسطائية ، لكن الحَكِيمُ يُعِدُّهُ مُكابَرَةً . (ومن هنا) أي من أجل أن السند ملزوم مقوم وقالوا: أي أهل النظر (ما من مقدمة) موجودة في حال من الأحوال إلا و الحال أنه (يمكن منعه مستندا بما ذهب إليه السوفسطائية: النافون لثبوت حقائق الأشياء لكن الحكيم المثبت لها يعده) أي ذلك السند (مكابرة) غير مسموعة.
And from here they said: There is no premise except that it is possible to object to it based on what the Sophists adhered to, but the philosopher considers it obstinacy. (And from here) meaning because the support is a necessary concomitant and a constituent, and they said: i.e., the people of consideration: (there is no premise) existing in any state except that it is the case that (it is possible to object to it based on what the Sophists adhered to: the deniers of the reality of things, but the philosopher who affirms them considers it) i.e., that support (to be obstinacy) that is not heeded.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement from the matn. It explains a fundamental principle of dialectical debate: any premise, regardless of its apparent certainty, can be objected to on the basis of a sophistical position. The Sophists (السوفسطائية) are those who deny the reality of things (ثبوت حقائق الأشياء). The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'from here' (ومن هنا) refers to the fact that the support (السند) is a necessary concomitant (ملزوم) and a constituent (مقوم) of the objection. The people of consideration (أهل النظر) – the logicians – assert that there is no premise for which an objection cannot be raised, relying on the sophistical stance. However, the philosopher (الحكيم) who affirms the reality of things considers such a support to be mere obstinacy (مكابرة) that is not admissible in sound debate. Thus, the passage establishes the boundary between legitimate objection based on valid reasoning and the sophistical denial that the philosopher rejects.

Expressions for Objection with Support in Jadal
Listing and explaining the verbal formulas of objection with support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correctly use the three common objection formulas (لم لا يجوز، لم لا يكون، كيف لا مع واو الحال) and recognize the rare formula إنما in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
The words you use in objection show your level of adab and precision in jadal.

In a jadal (dialectical) debate, after you reject a premise (man‘ musnad), you often need to ask a pointed question that challenges the opponent's claim. The classical tradition codifies specific expressions for this purpose. This lesson unpacks the most common formulas—why something is not permissible, why it is not so, how it is not—and the rare use of 'innama'. Understanding these formulas trains you in the etiquettes of objection, a cornerstone of Islamic disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of objection with support
Recognize that the objection (man‘) is accompanied by a support (sanaid) – a reason or authority. This is the standard context for using these formulas.
The support may be a Quranic verse, hadith, or rational argument.
2
Locate the three common formulas
In the Arabic passage, find 'لم لا يجوز؟', 'لم لا يكون كذا؟', and 'كيف لا؟' with the waw of circumstance. These are the primary expressions.
The waw of circumstance (واو الحال) is attached to 'كيف لا' indicating 'how is it not in such a state?'
3
Understand the function of each formula
'لم لا يجوز' asks about permissibility or possibility; 'لم لا يكون' asks why something is not the case; 'كيف لا' asks about the state while appealing to a given circumstance.
Each formula shifts the burden of proof back to the opponent.
4
Recognize the rare formula 'إنما'
Find the mention of 'إنما' and note that it introduces a conditional restriction: 'rather it would be so if it were so'. The commentator says it is rare (قليل).
This formula is used only in specific contexts, which is why the matn says 'في الأكثر' (in most cases).
5
Explain why the author says 'in most cases'
The phrase 'في الأكثر' indicates that the common formulas are the norm, while 'إنما' is exceptional. This shows the author's awareness of variation in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُذْكَرُ في الأَكْثَرِ بَعْدَهُ : لِمَ لا يَجُوزُ ؟ أَوْ لمَ لَا يَكُونُ كَذَا؟ وكيف لا ؟ وَوَاوُ الحَالِ . (ويذكر في الأكثر أي في أكثر أوقات المنع مستندا ( بعده ) أي بعد المنع «لم لا يجوز؟ كما يقال: ما ذكرت ممنوع، لم لا يجوز أن يكون كذا؟ ( ولم لا يكون ؟ كما يقال: هذا ممنوع، لم لا يكون أن يكون كذا؟ وكيف لا وواو الحال، أي مقرونا لفظ ( كيف لا مع واو الحال، كما يقال ذلك غير مسلم، كيف لا والأمر كذا، وقد يذكر كلمة إنما أيضا كما يقال: لا نسلم تلك المقدمة، وإنما يكون كذا لو كان كذا، وهو قليل (۳) ولذا قال - فى الأكثر.
And it is mentioned in most cases after it: 'Why is it not permissible?' or 'Why is it not so?' and 'How is it not?' and with the waw of circumstance. (And it is mentioned in most) i.e., in most times of objection with a support (after it) i.e., after the objection: 'Why is it not permissible?' as it is said: 'What you mentioned is objectionable; why is it not permissible that it be so?' and 'Why is it not?' as it is said: 'This is objectionable; why is it not that it be so?' and 'How is it not?' with the waw of circumstance, i.e., the word 'how is it not' is accompanied by the waw of circumstance, as it is said: 'That is not accepted; how is it not so?' and the word 'innama' is also sometimes mentioned, as it is said: 'We do not accept that premise; rather it would be so if it were so,' and it is rare. Therefore he said 'in most'.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the standard phrases used by a debater when formulating an objection that has a support (سند). The matn states that in most cases (في الأكثر) after stating the objection, the debater uses expressions like 'Why is it not permissible?' (لم لا يجوز), 'Why is it not so?' (لم لا يكون), and 'How is it not?' (كيف لا) accompanied by the waw of circumstance (واو الحال). The commentator explains each phrase with examples: after saying 'I object to this premise,' one may say 'Why is it not permissible that it be so?' or 'Why is it not that it be so?' or 'How is it not so while the matter is such?' The waw of circumstance is attached to 'كيف لا' to indicate the state. Additionally, the word 'إنما' (rather) may be used, as in 'We do not accept that premise; rather it would be so if it were so.' However, this is rare (قليل), which is why the matn says 'in most' (في الأكثر). This lesson teaches the formal etiquette of objection in jadal.

When to Investigate Strengthening Support in Jadal
Commentary on when investigation of strengthening support is allowed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exceptional condition under which a respondent may investigate a questioner's strengthening support in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Strengthening support is allowed, but investigating it is only proper when it equals the contradictory of the disputed premise.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle point: while it is generally not proper to investigate the questioner's strengthening of his support (sanad), there is an exception. When the strengthening support is equivalent to the negation of the premise in dispute, then it becomes legitimate to scrutinize it. This rule prevents wasteful objections while allowing valid challenges. Let's examine the conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the strengthening support
Look for the extra element the questioner adds to back his original support. This is the thing mentioned 'لتقوية السند'.
2
Determine its logical relation to the disputed premise
Ask: Is this strengthener equivalent (مساو) to the contradictory (نقيض) of the premise that the respondent is objecting to?
3
Apply the exception
If the strengthener is equivalent, the respondent may investigate it. If not, he cannot, and the mere possibility of a flaw in the strengthener is sufficient—he does not need to prove it.
4
Understand the rationale
Invalidating the strengthener would not refute the objected premise unless they are equivalent. Hence, investigation is only useful in that case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يُذكُرُ شَيءٌ لتقوية السَّنَدِ وتَوْضِيحه بصُورةِ الدليل، ولا يَحْسُنُ البَحْتُ فيهِ ولا في السَّندِ سِوَى ما اسْتُثنى ، ولا يلزمُ إِثْبَاتُه . وَلَا يَجُوزُ لِلسَّائلِ إِثْبَاتُ مُنافِي المُقَدِّمَةِ للزومِ الغَصْبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ ضَرُورَةٍ لِوجُودِ مَا (وقد يذكر شيء لتقوية السند وتوضيحه بصورة الدليل، بأن يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يكون كذلك؟ لأنه كذا وكذا ( ولا يحسن البحث فيه ) أي في المذكور لتقوية السند، لأنه لا يفيد شيئا، لأن إبطال ما يؤيد السند لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة الذي هو مقصود المعلل ( ولا في السند سوي ما استثنى ، وهو الإبطال بعد إثبات كونه مساويا لنقيض المقدمة الممنوعة ( ولا يلزم إثباته ، أي المذكور من مقوي السند والسند إن أورد عليه البحث لكفاية مجرد الاحتمال (۱) . ولا يجوز للسائل إثبات منافي المقدمة المعينة قبل إقامة المعلل الدليل عليها ، أما بعدها فيجوز، ويكون مناقضة على سبيل المعارضة ، أما كونه مناقضة فلأنه كلام على المقدمة المعينة ، وأما كونه على سبيل المعارضة فظاهر ، ولا يلزم الغصب من غير ضرورة ، لأنه لا يجوز منع المقدمة بعد إقامة الدليل عليها، فمست.
And sometimes something is mentioned to strengthen the support and clarify it in the form of a proof, and it is not proper to investigate it nor the support except what is excepted, and its proof is not required. And it is not permissible for the questioner to establish a contradictory of the premise due to the necessity of coercion without need for existence. (And sometimes something is mentioned to strengthen the support and clarify it in the form of a proof, such as saying: 'Why is it not permissible that it be so? Because such and such.' And it is not proper to investigate it) i.e., what is mentioned to strengthen the support, because it does not benefit anything, for invalidating what supports the support does not entail establishing the objected premise which is the aim of the one giving the proof. (Nor in the support except what is excepted, which is invalidation after establishing that it is equivalent to the contradictory of the objected premise. And its proof is not required, i.e., what is mentioned of the strengthener of the support and the support if it is investigated, for the mere probability is sufficient. (1) And it is not permissible for the questioner to establish a contradictory of the specific premise before the one giving the proof establishes proof upon it. After that it is permissible, and it would be a contradiction by way of counterargument. As for its being a contradiction, it is because it is speech about the specific premise, and as for its being by way of counterargument, it is apparent. And there is no necessity for coercion without need, because it is not permissible to object to the premise after the proof is established upon it, so it is based.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two rules of dialectical debate. First, the questioner (السائل) may sometimes present something to strengthen his support (السند) and clarify it in the form of a proof. However, it is not proper for the respondent (المعلل) to investigate that strengthener or the support itself, except in one exceptional case: when the strengthener or the support can be invalidated and that invalidation leads to establishing the opposite of the objected premise, i.e., when they are equivalent (مساو) to the negation of the premise. In that case, the respondent may attack them. Otherwise, the mere possibility of a flaw is sufficient, and the respondent is not required to prove the strengthener. Second, the questioner is not allowed to establish a contradictory (منافي) of a specific premise before the respondent has provided his proof. After the proof is given, it becomes permissible, and such an act would be a contradiction (مناقضة) by way of counterargument (معارضة). The reason for the prohibition before the proof is to avoid coercion (الغصب) without necessity; after the proof, the premise is already supported, so objecting to it by establishing its opposite is a legitimate counter.

Key Terms
تقوية السند
البحث
الاستثناء
المساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Never, because it is always improper.

Key Takeaway
The respondent may only investigate the strengthener if it is equivalent to the opposite of the disputed premise; otherwise, he must accept it as sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Write a short dialogue illustrating when the respondent should and should not probe the strengthener.

Timing of Establishing a Contradictory in Jadal
Commentary on the prohibition of establishing a contradictory before proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a questioner may legitimately establish a contradictory premise in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
The questioner cannot establish a contradictory before proof; afterward, it becomes a valid counterargument.

This lesson focuses on a crucial procedural rule in jadal: the questioner is prohibited from asserting a contradictory of a specific premise until after the respondent has provided proof. Once proof is given, establishing the contradictory is not only allowed but becomes a formal counterargument (معارضة). This prevents unfair coercion and ensures orderly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific premise
Locate the premise (المقدمة المعينة) that the respondent has put forward and that is under dispute.
2
Check if proof has been established
Has the respondent provided a formal proof (دليل) for that premise? This is the key factor.
3
If no proof, the questioner cannot assert a contradictory
Without proof, doing so would be coercion (غصب) without necessity, which is prohibited.
4
If proof is given, the questioner may assert the contradictory
After proof, establishing a contradictory is permissible and becomes a contradiction (مناقضة) on that premise, specifically as a counterargument (معارضة).
5
Understand the rationale
After proof, the premise is already supported, so objecting is legitimate; before proof, it would force acceptance unfairly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يُذكُرُ شَيءٌ لتقوية السَّنَدِ وتَوْضِيحه بصُورةِ الدليل، ولا يَحْسُنُ البَحْتُ فيهِ ولا في السَّندِ سِوَى ما اسْتُثنى ، ولا يلزمُ إِثْبَاتُه . وَلَا يَجُوزُ لِلسَّائلِ إِثْبَاتُ مُنافِي المُقَدِّمَةِ للزومِ الغَصْبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ ضَرُورَةٍ لِوجُودِ مَا (وقد يذكر شيء لتقوية السند وتوضيحه بصورة الدليل، بأن يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يكون كذلك؟ لأنه كذا وكذا ( ولا يحسن البحث فيه ) أي في المذكور لتقوية السند، لأنه لا يفيد شيئا، لأن إبطال ما يؤيد السند لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة الذي هو مقصود المعلل ( ولا في السند سوي ما استثنى ، وهو الإبطال بعد إثبات كونه مساويا لنقيض المقدمة الممنوعة ( ولا يلزم إثباته ، أي المذكور من مقوي السند والسند إن أورد عليه البحث لكفاية مجرد الاحتمال (۱) . ولا يجوز للسائل إثبات منافي المقدمة المعينة قبل إقامة المعلل الدليل عليها ، أما بعدها فيجوز، ويكون مناقضة على سبيل المعارضة ، أما كونه مناقضة فلأنه كلام على المقدمة المعينة ، وأما كونه على سبيل المعارضة فظاهر ، ولا يلزم الغصب من غير ضرورة ، لأنه لا يجوز منع المقدمة بعد إقامة الدليل عليها، فمست.
And sometimes something is mentioned to strengthen the support and clarify it in the form of a proof, and it is not proper to investigate it nor the support except what is excepted, and its proof is not required. And it is not permissible for the questioner to establish a contradictory of the premise due to the necessity of coercion without need for existence. (And sometimes something is mentioned to strengthen the support and clarify it in the form of a proof, such as saying: 'Why is it not permissible that it be so? Because such and such.' And it is not proper to investigate it) i.e., what is mentioned to strengthen the support, because it does not benefit anything, for invalidating what supports the support does not entail establishing the objected premise which is the aim of the one giving the proof. (Nor in the support except what is excepted, which is invalidation after establishing that it is equivalent to the contradictory of the objected premise. And its proof is not required, i.e., what is mentioned of the strengthener of the support and the support if it is investigated, for the mere probability is sufficient. (1) And it is not permissible for the questioner to establish a contradictory of the specific premise before the one giving the proof establishes proof upon it. After that it is permissible, and it would be a contradiction by way of counterargument. As for its being a contradiction, it is because it is speech about the specific premise, and as for its being by way of counterargument, it is apparent. And there is no necessity for coercion without need, because it is not permissible to object to the premise after the proof is established upon it, so it is based.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two rules of dialectical debate. First, the questioner (السائل) may sometimes present something to strengthen his support (السند) and clarify it in the form of a proof. However, it is not proper for the respondent (المعلل) to investigate that strengthener or the support itself, except in one exceptional case: when the strengthener or the support can be invalidated and that invalidation leads to establishing the opposite of the objected premise, i.e., when they are equivalent (مساو) to the negation of the premise. In that case, the respondent may attack them. Otherwise, the mere possibility of a flaw is sufficient, and the respondent is not required to prove the strengthener. Second, the questioner is not allowed to establish a contradictory (منافي) of a specific premise before the respondent has provided his proof. After the proof is given, it becomes permissible, and such an act would be a contradiction (مناقضة) by way of counterargument (معارضة). The reason for the prohibition before the proof is to avoid coercion (الغصب) without necessity; after the proof, the premise is already supported, so objecting to it by establishing its opposite is a legitimate counter.

Key Terms
المنافي
المقدمة المعينة
الغصب
المناقضة
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only when the premise is false.
D) Only when the respondent is silent.

Key Takeaway
The questioner may only establish a contradictory after the respondent has provided proof; doing so before is coercion.

When Not to Investigate the Strengthener in Jadal
Author explains when something mentioned to strengthen the sanad is not to be investigated further.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an element mentioned to strengthen the sanad in jadal may be investigated and when it may not.

Core Idea
Not every argument element is open to challenge; the strengthener is protected unless it contradicts the denied premise.

In jadal, the opponent seeks to undermine the respondent's support (sanad). However, not everything the respondent says to reinforce his position can be attacked. This lesson clarifies a key rule: the strengthener (muqawwi) is off-limits unless it is equivalent to the contradictory of the denied premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule about investigating the strengthener
Read the passage and find what the author says about investigating the strengthener. The rule is that it should not be investigated because it does not benefit the opponent's aim.
The author says: 'وَلا يُحْسَنُ الْبَحْثُ فِيهِ' (it is not good to investigate it).
2
Understand the exception
Look for the condition under which investigation is allowed. The passage mentions 'إِلاَّ مَا اسْتُثْنِيَ' (except what is excepted). This exception is when the strengthener is equivalent to the contradictory of the denied premise.
The exception requires the opponent to first establish equivalence between the strengthener and the contradictory.
3
Apply the rule to a hypothetical jadal exchange
Given a scenario, decide if the strengthener can be challenged. For example, in a debate about the temporality of the world, can the opponent investigate the respondent's strengthener? Use the rule to decide.
Remember: mere possibility suffices for the strengthener to be valid, so it is not easily overturned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And something may be mentioned to strengthen the sanad and clarify it in the form of a proof, by saying: 'Why should it not be so? Because such and such.' (And it is not good to investigate it) i.e. what was mentioned to strengthen the sanad, because it does not benefit anything, since invalidating what supports the sanad does not necessitate proving the denied premise which is the aim of the respondent. Commentary
This passage explains a rule in dialectical argumentation (jadal). The author states that a disputant may mention something to strengthen his support (sanad) and clarify it in the form of a proof. However, it is not appropriate for the opponent to investigate this strengthening element (the 'mentioned thing') because refuting it does not achieve the opponent's goal, which is to disprove the original denied premise. The opponent is only allowed to investigate the sanad itself in one excepted case: when he can show that the sanad or its strengthener is equivalent to the contradictory of the denied premise, thereby indirectly proving his own point. Even then, proving the strengthener is not required if a mere possibility suffices. This reflects the principle in jadal that not every element of an argument is open to challenge; only those that directly affect the central point of contention.

When to Prove the Contradictory: Timing in Jadal
Author forbids the questioner from proving the contradictory of a premise before the respondent provides proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct timing for a questioner to prove the contradictory of a premise in a jadal debate, and distinguish between a simple denial and a refutation via counterargument.

Core Idea
In jadal, timing is everything: the questioner cannot prove a premise’s contradictory until the respondent provides proof.

This lesson examines a key procedural rule in Islamic dialectic (jadal): the questioner's ability to prove the contradictory of a premise depends on whether the respondent has already provided evidence. The passage from the Rushidiyya clarifies that before proof, such action is forbidden; after proof, it becomes a valid refutation (naqd) in the form of a counterargument (mu'arada). We will analyze the rule, its rationale, and the given example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
Recognize the two key participants: the questioner (al-sa'il) who denies premises and seeks proof, and the respondent (al-mu'allil) who provides the proof.
2
Locate the ruling
Find the sentence "ولا يجوز للسائل إثبات منافي المقدمة المعينة قبل إقامة المعلل الدليل عليها". This states: it is not permissible for the questioner to prove the contradictory of a specific premise before the respondent establishes proof upon it.
3
Find the exception
Read the next part: "أما بعدها فيجوز". After the respondent provides proof, it becomes permissible to prove the contradictory.
4
Understand the transformation
After proof, proving the contradictory is called a refutation (naqd) by way of counterargument (mu'arada). The text explains: it is a refutation because it is about the specific premise; it is a counterargument obviously because it presents a contrary case.
5
Examine the example
The respondent claims "كل أفراد المخلوقات حادثة" (all individuals of created things are originated). The questioner counters: "بعض المخلوقات قديمة كعقول الأفلاك" (some created things are eternal like the intellects of the spheres). This is a counterargument because it provides an exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز للسائل إثبات منافي المقدمة المعينة قبل إقامة المعلل الدليل عليها، أما بعدها فيجوز، ويكون مناقضة على سبيل المعارضة، أما كونه مناقضة فلأنه كلام على المقدمة المعينة، وأما كونه على سبيل المعارضة فظاهر، ولا يلزم الغصب من غير ضرورة، لأنه لا يجوز منع المقدمة بعد إقامة الدليل عليها، فمست يكون كذلك لو كانت كل أفراد المخلوقات حادثة وليس كذلك لأن بعض المخلوقات قديمة كعقول الأفلاك وتقوسها
It is not permissible for the questioner to prove the contradictory of a specific premise before the respondent establishes proof upon it. As for after it, it is permissible, and it becomes a refutation (naqd) by way of counterargument (mu'arada). As for it being a refutation, it is because it is speech on the specific premise. As for it being a counterargument, it is apparent. And the necessity of usurpation is not required without necessity, because it is not permissible to deny the premise after proof has been established upon it. So what is established is that if all individuals of created things were originated, and it is not so because some created things are eternal like the intellects of the spheres and their curvature.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage addresses the timing of the questioner's (sa'il) actions in jadal. The questioner is not allowed to prove the contradictory of a particular premise before the respondent (mu'allil) has provided evidence for it. After the respondent has provided evidence, it becomes permissible and then it is called a refutation (naqd) in the form of a counterargument (mu'arada). The reasoning is that before proof, the premise is merely denied; after proof, the denial is no longer valid, so the questioner must use a different method. The example given: if the respondent claims that all created things are originated (haditha), the questioner might counter by showing that some created things are eternal, like the intellects of the spheres. This illustrates the distinction between a simple denial (man') and a counterargument (mu'arada). The text also notes that this action is not considered usurpation (ghasb) when done after proof, because there is a need to respond.

Key Terms
السائل
المعلل
المناقضة
المعارضة
الغصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In jadal, proving the contradictory of a premise is only permissible after the respondent has provided proof, at which point it constitutes a refutation by counterargument.

Distinguishing Specific and General Sanad in Jadal
Author defines the specific and general sanad in dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the specific sanad (al-sanad al-akhass) and the general sanad (al-sanad al-a'amm) in a dialectical exchange, and identify whether the denial is realized with or without the converse.

Core Idea
In dialectic, the specific sanad holds even when the denial is absent; the general sanad is broader than the denial.

In jadal, the skill of choosing the right sanad (ground for objection) is crucial. This lesson unpacks the author's classification of sanad into 'specific' and 'general' based on its logical relationship to the denial (man'). By working through the examples, you will learn to identify which type of sanad the questioner is using and what it implies for the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denial and the sanad in an example
In the first example, the claimant says 'This is a human' and the questioner says 'Why not a horse?' The denial is 'not human' and the sanad is 'being a horse'. Note which is broader.
The denial is the opposite of the claimant's premise.
2
Check if the denial holds without the sanad's opposite
If the object is a donkey (not a horse), is it still not human? Yes. So the denial is realized even when the sanad is absent. This makes the sanad 'specific' (akhass).
The converse test: if the sanad implies the denial but not vice versa.
3
Analyze the two types of general sanad
The text gives two cases: absolute generality (e.g., 'non-laugher' vs 'not human') and generality from one aspect (e.g., 'white' vs 'not human'). Determine whether the sanad is always accompanied by the denial.
Absolute generality means whenever the denial is true, the sanad is also true. One-aspect means partial overlap.
4
Apply the converse criterion
Ask: Does the converse hold? That is, if the sanad is present, is the denial always present? If yes, the sanad is more general; if no, it is more specific.
The author uses 'with the converse (ma'a al-'aks)' to indicate generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة : السند الأخص هو أن يتحقق المنع مع انتفائه أيضا من غير عكس ، ومع العكس أعم . أي هذا مبصر ، عبر عن اسم الفاعل بالمصدر : أعني (۱) التبصرة مبالغة ، كما يقال : للمذكر تذكرة . و السند الأخص أن يتحقق المنع ، أي انتفاء المقدمة الممنوعة وخلافها ( مع انتفائه أيضا كما يتحقق مع وجوده مثل أن يقول مدع في دليله : هذا إنسان ، فيقول السائل : لا نسلم ذلك ، لم لا يجوز أن ،، يكون فرسا ؟ فالسند وهو كونه فرسا أخص من عدم كونه إنسانا ، لتحقق عدم كونه إنسانا مع عدم كونه فرسا أيضا، مثل أن يكون حمارا مثلا ( من غير عكس ، وهو أن يتحقق السند مع انتفاء المنع بالمعنى المذكور ( ومع العكس أعم ) مطلقا أو من وجه أما الأول : فمثل أن يقول المعلل في دليله : هذا إنسان ، فقيل : لا نسلم ذلك . لم لا يجوز أن يكون غير ضاحك الفعل، فالسند - وهو عدم الضحك بالفعل - أعم من عدم كونه إنسانا ، لأنه كلما يوجد عدم الإنسانية يوجد عدم الضحك بالفعل، من غير عكس كلي . وأما الثاني : فكما إذا قال المعلل في دليله : هذا إنسان ، ويقول السائل : لا نسلم ذلك : لم لا يجوز أن يكون أبيض ؟ فالسند
Insight: The more specific sanad is that the denial is realized also with its absence, without its converse. And with the converse, it is more general. That is, this is an insight, the verbal noun is used instead of the active participle: I mean the insight is exaggeration, as it is said for the masculine 'reminder'. And the more specific sanad is that the denial (i.e. the absence of the denied premise and its opposite) is realized also with its absence, just as it is realized with its existence. For example, if a claimant says in his proof: 'This is a human', and the questioner says: 'We do not affirm that; why should it not be a horse?' So the sanad, which is its being a horse, is more specific than its not being a human, because its not being a human is also realized with its not being a horse, such as it being a donkey, for example. Without converse, i.e. the sanad is realized with the absence of denial in the mentioned sense. And with the converse, it is more general, either absolutely or from one aspect. As for the first: for example, if the respondent says in his proof: 'This is a human', and it is said: 'We do not affirm that; why should it not be a non-laugher by act?' So the sanad — which is the absence of laughter by act — is more general than its not being a human, because whenever non-humanity is present, absence of laughter by act is present, but not the full converse. As for the second: for example, if the respondent says in his proof: 'This is a human', and the questioner says: 'We do not affirm that; why should it not be white?' So the sanad...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage provides a clarification (tabsira) on the types of sanad in jadal. It first defines the specific sanad (sanad akhass) as one where the denial (man') holds true both when its opposite is absent and when it is present, without the converse relationship. The example: The claimant says 'This is human,' the questioner denies and suggests it might be a horse. The denial of humanity holds if it is a horse and also if it is a donkey, so the sanad (being a horse) is more specific than the denial. The text then contrasts this with the general sanad (a'amm), which occurs when the sanad is realized with the converse of the denial. There are two types: absolute generality (e.g., denying humanity by suggesting non-laughter, which is a broader category) and generality from one aspect (e.g., suggesting it is white, which overlaps partially). The examples illustrate the logical relationships between the premise and the sanad. This classification helps the disputant choose appropriate responses.

السند الأعم
العكس
المنع

D) The sanad and denial are equivalent.

Key Takeaway
The specific sanad holds even when its converse is absent, while the general sanad covers cases beyond the denial.

Classifying Sanad in Jadal: Akhass, A'amm, Musawi
Commentary defines the three types of sanad in jadal and distinguishes them from naqd and mu'arada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an objector's support (sanad) as more specific (akhass), more general (a'amm), or coextensive (musawi) relative to the denied premise in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
The strength of an objection depends on the logical relationship between its support and the denial.

This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary equips the debater with a precise tool for evaluating the quality of an objector's support (sanad). Understanding whether the sanad is more specific, more general, or coextensive with the denial determines whether the objection is effective or requires further proof. The commentary also clarifies that a simple denial (man') does not require proof, whereas a genuine objection (naqd) or counter-argument (mu'arada) does.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denial and the support
In a jadal exchange, first locate the objector's denial (man') of the mu'allil's premise. Then identify the support (sanad) the objector provides after 'Why may it not be...?'.
2
Determine the extensional relationship
Compare the set of cases where the denial is true and the set where the support is true. Ask: Is the support's set a subset, superset, or equal to the denial's set?
3
Classify the sanad
If every case of support is also a case of denial but not vice versa, it is akhass (most specific). If every case of denial is a case of support but not vice versa, it is a'amm (more general). If they are exactly the same, it is musawi (coextensive).
4
Apply the passage example
The mu'allil says 'This is a human.' The objector says 'Why may it not be a horse?' The support 'it is a horse' is akhass because being a horse implies not human, but not human does not imply horse.
5
Test with an Islamic ruling scenario
Example: The mu'allil says 'This transaction is a sale (بيع).' The objector says 'Why may it not be a gift (هبة)?' Determine the type. Since being a gift implies not being a sale, but not being a sale does not imply being a gift, the support is akhass.

It takes its place, meaning the denial (man'), unlike the objection (naqd) and counter-argument (mu'arada). Insight: The most specific support (sanad akhass) is that the denial occurs while it also does not occur, without converse, and with the converse it is more general. The necessity of that, and it is not permissible (the compulsory implication without necessity) because there exists what takes its place, meaning the denial, unlike the objection and counter-argument, for in both, proof is required: in the objection, it is proof of the opposite or impossibility; in the counter-argument, it is proof of the opposite of what the claimant asserted. That is, this is a 'mubsir' (insight), expressing the active participle by the infinitive: I mean 'tabṣira' as a hyperbole, just as it is said for the masculine 'tadhkira'. The most specific support is that the denial is realized, i.e., the non-existence of the denied premise and its opposite, with its non-existence also, as it is realized with its existence, like when a claimant says in his proof: this is a human, and the questioner says: we do not accept that, why may it not be a horse? The support, that it is a horse, is more specific than it not being a human, because it not being a human is realized also when it is not a horse, like if it were a donkey, for example, without converse, i.e., the support is realized while the denial in the mentioned sense is absent. And with the converse it is more general, absolutely or from one aspect. As for the first: like when the mu'allil says in his proof: this is a human, and it is said: we do not accept that, why may it not be non-laughing actually? The support, which is the absence of laughter actually, is more general than it not being a human, because whenever non-humanity exists, the absence of laughter actually exists, without total converse. As for the second: like when the mu'allil says in his proof: this is a human, and the questioner says: we do not accept that, why may it not be white? The support, that it is white, is more general from one aspect than it not being a human, because its being white exists with it being a human also, just as it exists with its non-existence, and similarly, it not being a human exists with its non-whiteness. It is not hidden from you that invalidating the absolutely general support proves the denied premise, for if the absence of actual laughter is invalid, its being a human is established. And (it is not) meaning the general support (is not a support in reality) because it does not strengthen the denial in reality, even if it strengthens realizing the meaning of generality, and because it is not a support in reality, it is not repelled, otherwise perhaps the general is a concomitant of the specific, so its invalidation is useful because invalidating the concomitant necessitates invalidating the concomitant, as you learned in the explanation of the definition of support. And the support (the coextensive) is that one of them does not separate from the other in the two cases of realization and non-existence, meaning that whenever the support exists and ceases, the non-existence of the denied premise exists and ceases, and vice versa. For example, if the mu'allil makes his statement: this is a human, a premise in his proof, and the objector says: we do not accept that, why may it not be a non-human? For whenever the absence of its being human is realized, its being non-human is realized, and whenever it ceases, it ceases, and whenever it is realized ... The support which the objector mentions to strengthen his denial is divided into three types: more specific than the denial of the premise (i.e., its non-existence), more general, and coextensive. The author mentioned this most specific support in his statement: 'The most specific support is that the denial occurs with its also not occurring', i.e., the denial, which is the non-existence of the mu'allil's premise, occurs with the non-existence of the questioner's support, because the more specific is that the more general exists but does not exist with it. The general support is that it exists without the existence of the denial, i.e., the non-existence of the premise, i.e., the support is realized while the denial is not. The commentator gave examples for the two types, i.e., the most specific and the general support, and their examples are clear. The author (may God have mercy on him) pointed to the general support by his statement: 'And with the converse it is more general.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary explains a key concept in jadal (dialectical debate): the sanad (support) used by the objector (man'') to strengthen his denial of a premise. The commentary first distinguishes the man' (denial) from naqd (objection) and mu'arada (counter-argument), stating that man' requires no proof initially, whereas naqd and mu'arada require establishing something. Then it introduces a 'tabṣira' (insight) defining the 'sanad akhass' (most specific support): it is when the denial (of the premise) occurs, but the support is also absent in some cases, without the converse relationship. Conversely, when the support is present even without the denial, it is 'a'amm' (more general). The author gives examples: for akhass, if the claimant says 'this is a human' and the objector says 'why may it not be a horse?', the support 'it is a horse' is more specific than 'it is not a human' because the absence of being human still holds when it is not a horse (e.g., a donkey). For a'amm mutlaq (absolutely general), the support 'it is not actually laughing' is more general than 'it is not a human' because whenever it is not a human, it is not laughing, but the converse is not always true. For a'amm min wajh (general from one aspect), the support 'it is white' is more general from one aspect than 'it is not a human' because whiteness can exist with humanity or without it. The commentary explains that invalidating the absolutely general support proves the denied premise because its falsity entails the premise's truth. However, the general support is not a 'support in reality' because it does not strengthen the denial effectively, though it has some logical utility as a concomitant. The third type, 'musawi' (coextensive), is where the support and the denial of the premise are mutually entailing: whenever one exists or ceases, so does the other. Example: 'it is a non-human' as a support for 'it is not a human'—the two are coextensive. The footnote summarizes the three divisions: akhass, a'amm, and musawi, confirming the author's initial mention.

al-man'
al-naqd
al-mu'arada
al-sanad al-akhass
al-sanad al-a'amm
al-sanad al-musawi

A) Sanad akhass (most specific)
B) Sanad a'amm (more general)
C) Sanad musawi (coextensive)
D) Not a valid sanad

Key Takeaway
The logical relationship between the support (sanad) and the denial (man') determines the type of sanad, which in turn affects the strength of the objection in jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Three Types of Sanad in Dialectical Debate
Author defines the three types of sanad used in munazara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of sanad (a'amm, akhass, musawi) and explain which types genuinely strengthen an objection (man') in munazara.

Core Idea
Not all supports are equal: only specific and equal sanad truly strengthen the objection.

In the science of munazara, when an objector (mani') denies a premise, he often brings additional support (sanad) to strengthen his objection. But not all supports are equally effective. The scholars classify sanad into three types based on the logical relationship between the support and the prevention (man'). Understanding these types is essential for evaluating whether an objection is genuinely supported or merely appears to be.

Guided Path
1
Understand the debate context
The mu'allil (proponent) puts forward a premise. The mani' (objector) denies it (man'). The sanad is the additional evidence the objector brings to bolster his denial.
2
Identify the three logical relationships
The sanad can be more general (a'amm) than the prevention, more specific (akhass), or equal (musawi). The passage defines these: a'amm: support occurs without prevention; akhass: prevention occurs without support; musawi: both always occur together.
3
Analyze the example given
The author gives the example of 'being white' as more general than 'not being human'. Being white can occur with being human or not, so it does not guarantee the prevention. This is an a'amm sanad.
4
Determine 'true support'
Only akhass and musawi are considered 'true supports' (sanad fi al-haqiqa) because they entail the prevention. A'amm is not a true support; it only appears to strengthen.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a specific debate scenario, decide which type of sanad is used and whether the objection stands. If a'amm, the objector's argument is weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is divided into three types: more specific than the prevention of the premise (i.e., its non-existence), more general, and equal. And the author mentioned this more specific support in his statement: the more specific support is that the prevention occurs with its non-existence as well, i.e., the prevention (which is the non-existence of the mu'allil's premise) occurs with the non-existence of the sā'il's (questioner's) support. Because the more specific is that which the more general exists without the other existing with it. And the more general support is that it occurs with the absence of the prevention (i.e., non-existence of the premise): the support is realized but the prevention is not realized. Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of sanad (support) used in dialectical debate to strengthen an objection (man'). The author first explains that the equal support (musawi) is when the support and the prevention (non-existence of the premise) are always co-extensive: whenever one exists, the other exists, and whenever one ceases, the other ceases. He gives the example of the mu'allil claiming 'this is a human' as a premise, and the mani' objects by suggesting it might not be a human. The equal support is then 'not being human' which is co-extensive with 'being not human' — these are equivalent. The author also discusses the more general support (a'amm), which is not a true support because its existence does not entail the prevention; it only has a general relation. For instance, 'being white' is more general than 'not being human' because 'being white' can exist with being human or with not being human. Invalidating a more general support only works if the general is a necessary concomitant of the specific. The passage then clarifies the threefold division as mentioned by the author: more specific (akhass), more general (a'amm), and equal (musawi). The more specific support is when the prevention occurs even when the support is absent, i.e., the prevention is more specific than the support. The more general support is when the support occurs without the prevention. The commentator (al-mu'allif, i.e., the author of the matn) is referenced. This is a typical dialectical (jadal) discussion from the science of munazara.

Key Takeaway
Only the akhass and musawi types of sanad provide genuine support for an objection in a debate; the a'amm type does not truly reinforce the man'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, the mu'allil asserts: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. This drink is intoxicating. Therefore it is forbidden.' The mani' objects: 'We do not accept that this drink is intoxicating.' He adds: 'Because if the world were temporally originated, it would require a creator, but we deny that.' However, the sanad is exactly the premise being denied. What is the logical relationship?

Why Naqd Needs a Witness in Dialectical Debate
Author states that naqd requires a witness to the flaw, unlike munaqada, and explains the difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish naqd from munaqada and man' mujarrad, and determine when a witness is required for a critical objection in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Naqd attacks the whole proof; a witness prevents mere confusion of the opponent.

In dialectical debate (jadal), not every objection is treated equally. This lesson examines a key procedural rule: when a critic (sa'il) raises a naqd (critical objection) against a proof, he must bring a witness (shahid) that points to the specific flaw. If the flaw is self-evident, the objection is no longer naqd but pure prevention (man' mujarrad). Understanding this rule is essential for fair and productive theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of objection mentioned
The passage distinguishes naqd (requires a witness), munaqada (no witness needed), and man' mujarrad (self-evident flaw, no witness). List their key features from the Arabic.
Munaqada is contradiction, man' mujarrad is simple denial of a premise when the flaw is obvious.
2
Understand why naqd requires a witness
The author explains that naqd attacks the entire proof without specifying which premise is corrupt. This would leave the proponent (mu'allil) confused about what to defend. A witness indicates the specific defect, allowing the debate to proceed meaningfully.
3
Analyze the exception for self-evident flaws
The passage considers the objection: what if the corruption is self-evident (badihi)? The answer: such a case is not naqd but man' mujarrad, because the corrupt premise is obvious and the opponent can address it directly. So no witness is needed.
This prevents misuse of the rule to require witnesses for trivial flaws.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical debate
Imagine a proof with two premises. If the objector says 'The whole proof is invalid', that is naqd and needs a witness. If he says 'The first premise is false' and it is obviously false, that is man' mujarrad. Practise distinguishing.
Use an Islamic example like a proof for the obligation of prayer.

البَحْتُ السَّادِسُ لا يُسْمِعُ النَّقْضُ مِنْ غيرِ شاهد بخلافِ المُناقضَةِ والفَرْقُ ثَابِتٌ . لا يسمع النقض من غير شاهد يدل على فساد دليل المعلل . قيل فيه نظر، لأن فساد الدليل قد يكون بديهيا ، فلا يحتاج إلى شاهد . وجعله داخلا في الشاهد يخل بحصره في التخلف ولزوم المحال ، ويلزم منه إبطال أن يكون المنع المتوجه بداهة منعا مجردا ، والأمر بخلافه . ولعله أشار بقوله - قيل - إلى ضعفه ، لأن كلامنا في الدليل المسموع من حيث الظاهر، والدليل الفاسد بداهة غير مسموع . على أنه يمكن أن يقال : كلما كان فساده بديهيا تعين المقدمة الفاسدة فيندرج في المنع المجرد ، دون النقض . بخلاف المناقضة ، فإنها تسمع من غير شاهد . ولابد ههنا من الفرق بينهما . والفرق ثابت ، وهو أن السائل إذا منع مقدمة معينة يعلم المعلل أن دخله في أية مقدمة ، فيشتغل بدفعه، وأما إذا منع مجموع الدليل بدون تعيين مقدمة من مقدماته لم يعلم ذلك ، فيتحير ، فما لم يتكلم بما يدل على فساده لم يسمع، فالظاهر أن غرضه تحير المعلل .
The sixth investigation: The naqd (critical objection) is not heard without a witness (shahid), unlike munaqada (contradiction), and the difference is established. The naqd is not heard without a witness that indicates the corruption of the mu'allil's proof. It was said regarding this: there is a consideration, because the corruption of the proof may be self-evident, so it does not need a witness. And including it in the witness would disrupt its restriction to [what is not self-evident] and lead to impossibility. And it would entail nullifying that the prevention (man') directed self-evidently is a pure prevention, but the matter is contrary. Perhaps he indicated by his saying 'it was said' its weakness, because our discussion is about the proof that is heard from the perspective of the apparent, and the self-evidently corrupt proof is not heard. However, it can be said: whenever its corruption is self-evident, the corrupt premise is determined, so it falls under the pure prevention (man' mujarrad), not under naqd. Unlike munaqada, for it is heard without a witness. And here we must find the difference between them. The difference is established, and that is that when the questioner (sa'il) prevents a specific premise, the mu'allil knows where his [proof] enters, so he occupies himself with repelling it. But when he prevents the entire proof without specifying a premise from its premises, he does not know that, so he becomes perplexed. So as long as he does not speak with something that indicates its corruption, it is not heard. So the apparent is that his purpose is to perplex the mu'allil.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule for naqd (critical objection) in dialectical debate. The author states that a naqd is not accepted unless it is accompanied by a witness (shahid) that demonstrates the flaw in the opponent's proof. This is unlike munaqada (contradiction), which does not require a witness. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: what if the flaw is self-evident (badihi)? In that case, one might argue that no witness is needed. The response is that if the flaw is self-evident, then it is not a matter of naqd but rather a simple prevention (man' mujarrad), because the corrupt premise is obvious and the opponent can see it. The speaker then clarifies the reason for the difference between naqd and munaqada. In naqd, the objector attacks the whole argument without pointing out a specific premise, which would leave the opponent confused as to what to defend. Hence, a witness is needed to specify the defect. In munaqada, the objector contradicts the conclusion or something else, and that contradiction is itself clear, so no witness is required. The purpose of requiring a witness in naqd is to avoid mere confusion (tahayyur) of the opponent.

الشاهد
المناقضة
