Naqd Requires a Witness: Distinction from Mana'
Author defines naqd, its requirement of a witness, and distinguishes it from mana'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between naqd and mana' in jadal and apply the rule that naqd requires a witness.

Core Idea
Not every objection needs proof—only refutation of the whole proof requires a witness.

In Islamic dialectics (jadal), the rules of debate ensure clarity and fairness. One key rule distinguishes between two types of objections: preventing a specific premise (mana') and refuting the entire proof (naqd). This lesson will help you understand why the latter requires a witness (shāhid) while the former does not, and how this rule keeps debates focused.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of objections
Read the passage and locate the terms 'mana' al-muqaddima' (preventing a premise) and 'mana' al-dalīl' (preventing the proof, i.e., naqd). Understand that mana' targets a specific premise, while naqd attacks the entire argument.
Mana' is a request for proof; naqd is a denial.
2
Understand why mana' does not require a witness
In mana', the objector simply asks for evidence for a particular premise. The prover (mu'allil) knows which premise is challenged and can respond directly. No external witness is needed because the burden of proof remains on the prover.
This keeps the debate manageable.
3
Understand why naqd requires a witness
Naqd denies the entire proof without specifying a flaw. The prover would not know where to defend. Therefore, the objector must provide a witness (shāhid)—an argument that demonstrates the corruption of the proof. Without it, the objection is not heard.
The witness indicates the location of the flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البَحْتُ السَّادِسُ
لا يُسْمِعُ النَّقْضُ مِنْ غيرِ شاهدٍ بخلافِ المُناقضَةِ والفَرْقُ ثَابِتٌ. وقيل: الفرق أن منع المقدمة عبارة عن طلب الدليل فلا يحتاج إلى شاهد، وأما منع الدليل فعبارة عن نفيه، فلابد له من دليل. والفرق ثابت، وهو أن السائل إذا منع مقدمة معينة يعلم المعلل أن دخله في أية مقدمة، فيشتغل بدفعه، وأما إذا منع مجموع الدليل بدون تعيين مقدمة من مقدماته لم يعلم ذلك، فيتحير، فما لم يتكلم بما يدل على فساده لم يسمع. وقد يسمى القدح في طرد التعريف وعكسه نقضا.
The sixth inquiry: The refutation is not heard without a witness, contrary to contradiction, and the difference is established. It is said: The difference is that preventing a premise is a request for a proof, so it does not need a witness, while preventing the proof is a denial of it, so it must have a proof. And the difference is established, which is that if the questioner prevents a specific premise, the prover knows which premise he entered into, and engages in defending it. But if he prevents the entire proof without specifying which of its premises, he does not know that, so he becomes perplexed. Thus, unless he speaks with something indicating its corruption, it is not heard. And the attack on the comprehensiveness and exclusiveness of a definition is called refutation.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical definition of two key jadal concepts: naqd (refutation) and mana' (objection to a premise). It begins by stating that naqd is not accepted without a witness (shāhid) — meaning evidence that demonstrates the corruption of the proof — whereas munāqaḍa (contradiction) is accepted without a witness. The difference is that when a questioner objects by preventing a specific premise (mana'), he is simply requesting a proof for that premise, which does not require an external witness because the burden of proof falls on the prover. However, when he refutes the entire proof (naqd), he is negating it, which is a claim that requires its own justification. The passage then explains the practical difference: if the questioner prevents a particular premise, the prover knows which premise is challenged and can defend it. But if he prevents the whole proof without specifying, the prover becomes perplexed because he doesn't know where the flaw lies. Therefore, naqd needs a witness that indicates the flaw's location. Finally, the passage notes that attacking the comprehensiveness (tarid) and exclusiveness ('aks) of a definition is also called naqd. This guards against a common misunderstanding: that any objection to a proof is naqd and thus requires a witness; in reality, mana' does not require one because it is a request for evidence, not a denial.

Key Terms
النقض
المناقضة
المنع
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Naqd requires a witness; mana' does not.
C) Mana' requires a witness; naqd does not.
D) Neither requires a witness.

Key Takeaway
Naqd requires a witness because it denies the entire proof, while mana' is simply a request for proof of a specific premise and thus needs no witness.

Distinction Between Man' and Naqd in Jadal and Definition
Distinguishing types of prevention and definitional failure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man' al-muqaddima, man' al-dalil, and naqd in a dialectical exchange, and identify violations of tard and aks in definitions.

Core Idea
In jadal, man' is a request or denial of a proof, while naqd is a refutation requiring a counterexample; in definitions, naqd means failure of mutual implication.

In Islamic dialectics, the questioner has specific tools to challenge the respondent's argument. The text distinguishes three forms of prevention: man' of a premise, man' of the proof, and naqd. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for proper debate protocol. Additionally, the same term 'naqd' is used metaphorically to criticize definitions that fail to be exclusive or comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the questioner's move
Read the passage and determine whether the questioner is requesting proof of a premise (man' al-muqaddima) or denying the entire proof (man' al-dalil). Note the difference in burden of proof.
Man' al-muqaddima is a request; man' al-dalil is a denial.
2
Examine the need for a shahid
Check whether the move requires a witness (shahid). Man' al-muqaddima does not; man' al-dalil and naqd do.
3
Analyze naqd in definitions
Look for the conditions of tard (affirmative concomitance) and 'aks (negative concomitance). A definition fails if it violates either condition, which is called naqd by metaphor.
Tard: if definition applies, defined applies. 'Aks: if definition does not apply, defined does not apply.
4
Apply the three types of sanad
Consider the equal chain, specific chain, and general chain to see which benefit the questioner in strengthening his prevention.
Only the equal and specific chains benefit the questioner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإجراء الدليل في غيره قد لا يكون بعينه، وقد يحتاج الشاهد إلى دليل أو تنبيه، وقد يسمى القدح في طرد التعريف وعكسه نقضا. وقيل: الفرق أن منع المقدمة عبارة عن طلب الدليل فلا يحتاج إلى شاهد، حاصله أن هذه المقدمة نظرية عندي وأطلب بيانها، وهذا مما لا يحتاج إلى شاهد، وأما منع الدليل فعبارة عن نفيه، وهو مجرد دعوى، فلابد له من دليل، وفيه أنه لا يجوز أن يكون طلب صحة الدليل وبيانه كالمنع، تم كلامه. ويمكن أن يقال إذا كان مقصود السائل من الكلام على الدليل طلب صحة الدليل وبيانه لم يكن كلتا مقدمتيه معلومتين له، فيكون متعين ولا يكون نقضا، فيلزم حصر وظيفة السائل في المنع والمعارضة. بعينه ه وإجراء الدليل في غيره أي غير مدلوله و قد لا يكون بعينه، والمراد بكونه أن يوجد الدليل في صورة أخرى مع أنه لا يختلف إلا باعتبار موضوع المطلوب، فإذا اختلف الدليل بحسب الحد الأوسط، بأن يجعل السائل مرادفه أو ملازمه مقامه لم يكن إجراؤه بعينه، وقد يحتاج الشاهد في الدلالة على فساد الدليل إلى دليل إذا كان نظريا غير معلوم للمعلل فيطلب عليه أو تنبيه إن كان بديهيا غير أولي، وقد يسمى القدح في طرد التعريف وعكسه نقضا وذلك لأن معنى الطرد هو التلازم في الثبوت بمعنى كل ما صدق عليه الحد صدق عليه المحدود، ومعنى العكس التلازم في الانتفاء، بمعنى أن كل ما لم يصدق عليه الحد لم يصدق عليه المحدود، فإذا لم يكن التعريف مانعا فقد انتقضت الكلية الأولى وإذا لم يكن جامعا انتقضت الثانية، فله مشابهة بالنقض الإجمالي، حيث يقال: هذا التعريف ليس بصحيح، لاستلزامه دخول فرد من أفراد غير المحدود فيه، أو خروج فرد من أفراده عنه، فيطلق عليه لفظ النقض بطريق الاستعارة المصرحة. والذي ينفع المانع من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة السند المساوي والسند الأخص إذ كل منهما ينفعه في تقوية منعه بخلاف السند الأعم فإنه لا ينفع إلا في تحقق معنى العموم.
And conducting the proof in something else may not be the same proof, and the witness may need a proof or a reminder, and the criticism of the definition's t ard and 'aks is sometimes called naqd. And it was said: The difference is that man' of a premise is a request for proof, so it does not need a witness; its summary is that this premise is theoretical in my view and I seek its clarification, and this does not require a witness. As for man' of the proof, it is a denial of it, which is a mere claim, so it must have a proof. And in it [the objection] that requesting the soundness of the proof and its clarification should not be like man'. End of his speech. And it can be said: If the questioner's intent in speaking about the proof is to request the soundness and clarification of the proof, then not both of its premises are known to him, so it is determinate and not a naqd, leading to limiting the questioner's function to man' and opposition. [Repetition] Conducting the proof in something else, i.e., other than its conclusion, may not be the same proof. What is meant by 'same' is that the proof is found in another form without differing except by the subject of the sought. So if the proof differs by the middle term, such that the questioner makes its synonym or concomitant replace it, then its conducting is not the same. And the witness in indicating the corruption of the proof may need a proof if it is theoretical and unknown to the reasoner, so it is requested, or a reminder if it is obvious but non-primary. And the criticism of the definition's t ard and 'aks is sometimes called naqd, because the meaning of t ard is concomitance in affirmation, i.e., everything to which the definition applies, the defined also applies; and the meaning of 'aks is concomitance in negation, i.e., everything to which the definition does not apply, the defined also does not apply. So if the definition is not exclusive, the first universality is violated; if it is not comprehensive, the second is violated. So it has a similarity to total naqd, where it is said: This definition is not sound because it necessitates the entry of an individual from outside the defined, or the exit of an individual from it. So the term naqd is applied to it by way of explicit metaphor. And what benefits the preventer from these three divisions is the equal chain and the specific chain, for each of them benefits him in strengthening his prevention, unlike the general chain, which benefits only in verifying the meaning of generality.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage expands on the distinction between man' (prevention) and naqd (refutation) in dialectical debate, specifically in the context of the questioner's obligations. The author clarifies that man' of a premise (man' al-muqaddima) is merely a request for proof and does not require a witness (shahid), whereas man' of the proof (man' al-dalil) is a denial of the proof itself and thus needs a proof. The discussion then turns to naqd, which targets the entire argument, and explains that the shahid (the counterexample) may itself need a proof or a reminder. The author also connects naqd to definitional critiques: if a definition fails to be exclusive (man') or comprehensive (jami'), it is said to be 'naqd' metaphorically. Finally, the three types of 'sanad' (chain) are distinguished: equal, specific, and general, with only the first two benefiting the questioner in strengthening his prevention.

Five Methods of Daf' al-Shahid in Jadal
Author explains five ways to refute a witness in dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five methods of daf' al-shahid and apply them to a given counterexample in Islamic legal debate.

Core Idea
Refuting a witness in jadal is not a single move—there are five distinct strategies to neutralize a counterexample.

In dialectical debate (jadal), when an opponent presents a shahid (witness/counterexample) to undermine your claim, you have several precise ways to respond. The author lists five methods, each addressing a different weakness in the witness. This lesson unpacks those methods and shows how they are used in Islamic legal debates, such as the debate on what nullifies wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim and the shahid
In a jadal exchange, first determine the mudda'i's claim and the mu'tarid's counterexample. For example, the claim 'Anything exiting from other than the two passages is impurity (hadath)' is met with the shahid 'blood from a wound does not break wudu'.
2
Match the method to the objection
The author lists five refutation methods: (1) man' al-jaryan (prevent application of proof), (2) man' al-takhalluf (prevent non-occurrence of judgment), (3) izhar anna al-takhalluf li-mani' (show non-occurrence is due to a preventer), (4) man' istilzamihi li-l-muhal (prevent entailment of impossibility), (5) man' al-istihala (prevent impossibility). Identify which method the answerer uses.
3
Analyze the example from fiqh
In the given example, the Shafi'i presents a shahid of blood from a wound. The answerer counters by preventing the application of the analogy (man' al-jaryan): the proof (analogy to urine) does not apply to blood because of a difference in ruling or cause.
The example is cut off; focus on the method used.
4
Examine the ordering objection
The author addresses a potential criticism: why list 'izhar anna al-takhalluf li-mani' after the two 'man'' methods? He explains that the 'izhar' is related to the discussion of takhalluf, so it naturally follows the prevention of takhalluf.
5
Apply the methods to a new scenario
Take a claim from usul al-fiqh, e.g., 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden (haram).' A shahid could be 'a small amount of alcohol that does not intoxicate'. Choose an appropriate method of daf' al-shahid and explain why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودفع الشاهد قد يكون بمنع جريان الدليل، أو بمنع التخلف، أو بإظهار أن التخلف لمانع، أو بمنع استلزامه للمحال، أو بمنع الاستحالة. لا يقال: المناسب أن يؤخر الإظهار عن المنوع لئلا يلزم الفصل بينهما، لأنا نقول: لما كان الإظهار كلاما على التخلف وصلا بمنع التخلف. مثال الأول: أنا نقول: إن الخارج من غير السبيلين حدث لأنه نجس خارج من بدن الإنسان كالبول، فيورد من قبل الشافعي رحمه الله النقض بخارج من غير السبيلين لم يسل، حيث يصدق
And refuting the witness may be by preventing the application of the proof, or by preventing the non-occurrence [of the judgment], or by showing that the non-occurrence is due to a preventer, or by preventing its entailment of the impossible, or by preventing the impossibility. It should not be said: It is more appropriate to delay the showing after the prevention to avoid separation between them, for we say: Since the showing is speech about the non-occurrence, it is connected to the prevention of non-occurrence. Example of the first: We say: Whatever exits from other than the two passages is impurity (hadath) because it is impure exiting from the human body like urine, so the Shafi'i—may Allah have mercy on him—objects with a refutation by something exiting from other than the two passages that does not invalidate wudu, where it applies
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage details the methods of daf' al-shahid (refuting the witness) in dialectical debate. The author lists five ways: (1) preventing the application of the proof in the claimed scenario, (2) preventing the non-occurrence of the judgment (i.e., denying that the judgment fails to apply), (3) showing that the non-occurrence is due to a specific preventer, (4) preventing that the scenario entails impossibility, and (5) preventing the impossibility itself. The author then addresses a possible objection about ordering, explaining that the showing (izhār) is tied to the prevention of non-occurrence. Finally, an example is given: a Shafi'i opponent refutes the claim that anything exiting from other than the two passages is impurity (hadath) by citing a case (like a wound) where something exits but wudu is not nullified, thus providing a counterexample. The example is cut off but illustrates the first method.

The Five Methods of Repelling the Witness in Jadal
Author lists and illustrates five methods of repelling evidence in dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the five methods of repelling a witness (daf' al-shāhid) in a jadal exchange, using examples from Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Core Idea
Repelling a witness is not just denial—five precise methods exist to counter evidence dialectically.

In dialectical argument (jadal), when an opponent brings a witness (shāhid) to support their claim, you must have a repertoire of responses. This passage from the logic text enumerates five distinct methods of repelling that witness, each with a specific logical function. Understanding these methods equips you to defend positions in theological and juristic debates with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five methods
Find the five ways listed by the author: (1) denying the proof's applicability (man' jaryān al-dalīl), (2) denying the non-occurrence of the ruling (man' al-takhaluf), (3) showing non-occurrence is due to an obstacle (iẓhār al-takhaluf li-māni'), (4) denying that the proof leads to absurdity (man' istilzām al-muḥāl), (5) denying the impossibility of the consequence (man' al-istiḥāla).
These are ordered logically; the author later responds to a potential objection about ordering.
2
Match examples to methods
For each example in the text, determine which method is being used. The first example (fluid from other than two passages) uses method 1: denying the proof by claiming the fluid is not impure. The second (blood not requiring washing) uses method 2: denying non-occurrence by showing the ruling is absent because the cause is absent. The third (wound fluid not breaking wudu) uses method 3: showing an obstacle prevents the ruling's manifestation. The fourth (reality of man) uses method 4: denying that the proof leads to absurdity. The fifth (Mu'tazilite objection on adultery) is cut off but likely uses method 5.
Note that the fourth example involves a complex regress argument; understanding the logical structure helps see why method 4 is appropriate.
3
Analyse the ordering objection and response
The author anticipates a critique: method 3 (showing obstacle) should come after method 2 (denying non-occurrence) to avoid separating them. The response is that showing the obstacle is actually a comment on the non-occurrence and thus linked to method 2, so the order is justified.
This shows attention to the internal coherence of the list.
4
Practice applying the methods
Given a new dialectical exchange, identify which of the five methods is used. For example, if an opponent says 'This deduction leads to infinite regress', the response 'We do not accept that regress follows' corresponds to method 4.
Try to formulate examples from fiqh (e.g., qiyas) or kalām (e.g., arguments for creation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودفع الشاهد قد يكون بمنع جريان الدليل في صورة ادعى السائل جريانه فيها أو بمنع التخلف (أي تخلف الحكم عن الدليل) أو (يكون) بإظهار أن التخلف (في تلك الصورة) لمانع أو بمنع استلزامه للمحال (بأن يقال: لا يلزم المحال) أو بمنع الاستحالة (بأن يقال: ما يلزم ليس بمحال). لا يقال: المناسب أن يؤخر الإظهار عن المنوع لئلا يلزم الفصل بينهما، لأنا نقول: لما كان الإظهار كلاما على التخلف وصلة بمنع التخلف، مثال الأول: أنا نقول: إن الخارج من غير السبيلين حدث لأنه نجس خارج من بدن الإنسان كالبول، فيورد من قبل الشافعي - رحمه الله - النقض بخارج من غير السبيلين لم يسل، حيث يصدق عليه أنه نجس خارج من بدن الإنسان كالبول، ولم يوجد الحكم، وهو كونه حدثا، فدفعه يمنع جريان الدليل بأن نقول: لا نسلم أنه نجس خارج، بل هو باد، لأن تحت كل جلد رطوبة، فإذا فارقها الجلد بدت، ومثال الثاني، كما إذا اندفع ذلك التخلف بأن نقول: إن ذلك الدم ليس بنجس، لأنه لا يلزم غسل ذلك الموضع، فانعدام الحكم لانعدام العلة، لا مع وجودها، ومثال الثالث أنه إذا أورد على ذلك التعليل أن ما يخرج من جرح صاحب الجرح السائل نجس خارج من بدن الإنسان، مع أنه ليس بحدث، حيث لم تنتقض به الطهارة مادام الوقت باقيا، لدفعه بأن نقول: ليس الحكم المطلوب متخلفا عن الدليل بل هو موجود، لكن لم يظهر في الحال لوجود مانع، وإلا لم يتمكن المكلف من الأداء، ولهذا يلزم الطهارة بعد خروج الوقت بذلك الحدث، لا بخروج الوقت، فإنه ليس بحدث بالإجماع، والحكم هو كونه حدثا موجبا للوضوء مطلقا، لا كونه موجبا له في مع وجود المانع، ومثال الرابع بأن يقول المدعي: حقيقة الإنسان موجودة، لأنه شيء، وحقائق الأشياء موجودة، فيورد عليه أنه على تقدير حقيقة من الحقائق يلزم محال، وهو أنه لو كانت موجودة فإما أن يكون وجودها موجودا أولا، فإن كان الثاني فكيف يوجد بدون الوجود؟ وإن كان الأول يتكلم في وجود ذلك الوجود، وهكذا، فإما أن ينتهي إلى وجود لا وجود له أو يتسلسل، وكلاهما محالان، وندفعه بأن لا نسلم لزوم المحال، وإنما يلزم أن لو كان حقيقة الوجود وجودية، ولا نسلم ذلك، ولو سلم فوجوده عينه، ومثال الخامس أنا نقول: إن فعل زيد وعمرو بخلق الله تعالى، لأنه فعل عبد، وأفعال العبد بخلقه تعالى، فيورد عليه النقض من قبل المعتزلي بالزنا، بأن يقول: الزنا فعل من أفعال العباد، وليس الحال
And repelling the witness may be by denying the running of the proof in the case where the questioner claimed its running, or by denying the non-occurrence (i.e., the non-occurrence of the ruling from the proof), or by showing that the non-occurrence (in that case) is due to an obstacle, or by denying that it necessitates the absurd, or by denying the impossibility. It should not be said: it is appropriate to postpone the showing after the denial? to avoid separation between them, for we say: since the showing is a discourse on the non-occurrence and a link to denying the non-occurrence. Example of the first: we say: that which exits from other than the two passages is an impurity because it is an impure substance exiting from a human body like urine. Then the refutation is brought from al-Shafi'i - may Allah have mercy on him - by something exiting from other than the two passages that did not flow, where it is truly described as an impure substance exiting from a human body like urine, and the ruling is not found, which is that it is an impurity. Its repelling is by denying the running of the proof by saying: we do not affirm that it is impure exiting, rather it is excreted, because under every skin is moisture, so when it leaves the skin it appears. Example of the second: as if that non-occurrence is repelled by saying: that blood is not impure, because washing that spot is not required, so the absence of the ruling is due to the absence of the cause, not with its existence. Example of the third: if it is brought against that reasoning that what comes out of the wound of a person with a running wound is an impure substance exiting from a human body, while it is not an impurity, since ritual purity is not nullified by it as long as the time remains; to repel it we say: the required ruling is not absent from the proof but rather exists, but did not appear at the moment due to the presence of an obstacle; otherwise the obligated person would not be able to perform; and for this reason purity becomes necessary after the time elapses due to that event, not due to the elapse of time, for it is not an event by consensus. And the ruling is that it is an event necessitating ablution absolutely, not that it necessitates it in the presence of an obstacle. Example of the fourth: as when the claimant says: the reality of man exists because it is a thing, and the realities of things exist. Then it is objected to him that upon assuming a reality among realities, an absurdity is necessitated, namely: if it exists, then either its existence exists or not; if the second, how does it exist without existence? And if the first, one speaks about the existence of that existence, and so on; then either it ends with an existence that has no existence, or an infinite regress; both are absurd. We repel it by saying: we do not affirm the necessity of the absurd; it is only necessitated if the reality of existence were existent, and we do not affirm that; and if affirmed, its existence is identical to it. Example of the fifth: we say: the action of Zayd and 'Amr is created by Allah Almighty because it is an action of a slave, and the actions of the slave are created by Him. Then the refutation is brought from the Mu'tazilite by adultery, by saying: adultery is an action of the actions of the slaves, and it is not the case.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition on the techniques of repelling a witness (shahid) in jadal (dialectical argument). The author, likely drawing from earlier logic texts, enumerates five methods: (1) denying the applicability of the proof (man' jarayan al-dalīl) in the specific case asserted by the opponent; (2) denying the non-occurrence of the ruling (man' al-takhaluf), i.e., asserting that the ruling does apply but the opponent claims it does not; (3) showing that the apparent non-occurrence is due to an obstacle (iẓhār anna al-takhaluf li-mani'), so the ruling is present but blocked; (4) denying that the proof leads to an absurdity (man' istilzamihi lil-muḥāl); (5) denying that the supposed absurdity is actually absurd (man' al-istiḥāla). The author then anticipates an objection about ordering: that showing the obstacle should be postponed after denial? The response is that showing is connected to denial of non-occurrence. Each method is illustrated with examples from fiqh and kalām. The first example: a Shafi'i argument that fluid from other than the two passages (urine/feces) is an impurity because it is an impure substance exiting the body like urine; the opponent objects with blood from a wound that does not nullify wudu; the response denies that blood is impure (it is excreted, not impure). The second example: blood is not impure because washing is not required; the ruling's absence is due to absence of the cause. The third: wound fluid is impure but does not nullify wudu immediately due to the obstacle (preserving ability to pray); after the time, it nullifies. The fourth: a proof that the reality of man exists leads to an absurd regress; repelled by denying the premise that existence is an attribute distinct from essence. The fifth example: the claim that all actions are created by Allah is refuted by the Mu'tazilite using the example of adultery; the text cuts off. These examples demonstrate how the five methods are applied in actual theological and juridical debates.

Checkpoint
In the first example of repelling the witness, what method is used to counter the objection that fluid from a wound is impure but does not nullify wudu?
A) Denying the proof (man' jaryan al-dalīl) by stating the fluid is not impure.
B) Denying the non-occurrence (man' al-takhaluf) by asserting the ruling does apply.
C) Showing the non-occurrence is due to an obstacle (iẓhār al-takhaluf li-māni')
D) Denying that the proof leads to absurdity (man' istilzām al-muḥāl)

Key Takeaway
There are five distinct methods to repel evidence in jadal, each with a specific logical function, and they are illustrated with concrete examples from fiqh and kalām.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a fiqh debate, someone argues: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this drink is an intoxicant, so it is forbidden.' The opponent objects: 'But vinegar is not forbidden even though it can be intoxicating in large quantities.' Identify which of the five methods can be used to repel this witness, and write the repelling response.
?
Analyse the fourth example (reality of man) in the passage. Identify the exact absurdity claimed by the opponent and then write the defender's repelling response using method (4).

Dialectic -- Jadal

8 min
The Three Methods of Daf' with Fiqh Examples
Introduces three methods of daf' and illustrates the first two with fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three methods of daf' and analyze the first two fiqh examples to determine which method is used.

Core Idea
Daf' is a structured argument, not a mere rejection.

In jadal, repelling an objection requires more than a simple 'no'. The scholars of dialectic have classified the ways to show that an objection fails into three distinct methods. Let's learn them and see them applied to concrete fiqh examples involving impurity and ablution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methods in the opening sentence
Find the three methods listed: takhalluf li-mani' (ruling does not follow due to a hindrance), man' istilzam al-muhal (denying that the evidence entails an impossibility), and man' al-istihala (denying the impossibility).
These are the core strategies for daf'.
2
Analyze the first example: denial of a premise
In the first example, the arguer claims urine is impure exiting the body, so it nullifies ablution. The objector repels by denying the premise: he argues the urine is not 'impure exiting' but merely moisture that appears. This is a direct denial of the premise (man' al-muqaddima).
This is a classic use of man'.
3
Analyze the second example: showing the ruling is absent due to cause absence
In the second example, the objector argues that blood from a wound is not impure because washing that spot is not required. This shows that the ruling (impurity) is absent, so the cause is not present. This is an instance of takhalluf li-mani' (or more precisely, takhalluf bi-Adam al-illa).
Contrast: in the first, the premise is denied; here, the link between cause and ruling is broken.
4
Compare the two methods used
Notice the difference: first method directly attacks the premise, second method shows that the conclusion does not follow because the cause is missing. Both are valid forms of daf'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّخَلُّفُ لِمَانِعٍ، أَوْ بِمَنْعِ اسْتِلْزَامِهِ لِلْمُحَالِ، أَوْ بِمَنْعِ الاسْتِحَالَةِ. وَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ: مَا إِذَا قَالَ الْمُعَلِّلُ: البَوْلُ نَجَسٌ خَارِجٌ مِنْ بَدَنِ الإِنْسَانِ، فَيَكُونُ حَدَثًا، فَيَدْفَعُهُ السَّائِلُ بِمَنْعِ جَرَيَانِ الدَّلِيلِ بِأَنْ يَقُولَ: لا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّهُ نَجَسٌ خَارِجٌ، بَلْ هُوَ بَادٍ، لِأَنَّ تَحْتَ كُلِّ جِلْدٍ رُطُوبَةً، فَإِذَا فَارَقَهَا الجِلْدُ بَدَتْ. وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي: كَمَا إِذَا انْدَفَعَ ذَلِكَ التَّخَلُّفُ بِأَنْ نَقُولَ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ الدَّمَ لَيْسَ بِنَجَسٍ، لِأَنَّهُ لا يَلْزَمُ غَسْلُ ذَلِكَ المَوْضِعِ، فَانْعِدَامُ الحُكْمِ لانْعِدَامِ العِلَّةِ، لا مَعَ وُجُودِهَا. وَمِثَالُ الثَّالِثِ: أَنَّهُ إِذَا أُورِدَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ التَّعْلِيلِ أَنَّ مَا يَخْرُجُ مِنْ جُرْحِ صَاحِبِ الجُرْحِ السَّائِلِ نَجَسٌ خَارِجٌ مِنْ بَدَنِ الإِنْسَانِ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَدَثٍ، حَيْثُ لَمْ تَنْتَقِضْ بِهِ الطَّهَارَةُ مَادَامَ الوَقْتُ بَاقِيًا، لِدَفْعِهِ بِأَنْ نَقُولَ: لَيْسَ الحُكْمُ المَطْلُوبُ مُتَخَلِّفًا عَنْ الدَّلِيلِ بَلْ هُوَ مَوْجُودٌ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يَظْهَرْ فِي الحَالِ لِوُجُودِ مَانِعٍ، وَإِلا لَمْ يَتَمَكَّنِ المُكَلَّفُ مِنَ الأَدَاءِ، وَلِهَذَا يَلْزَمُ الطَّهَارَةُ بَعْدَ خُرُوجِ الوَقْتِ بِذَلِكَ الحَدَثِ، لا بِخُرُوجِ الوَقْتِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَدَثٍ بِالإِجْمَاعِ، وَالحُكْمُ هُوَ كَوْنُهُ حَدَثًا مُوجِبًا لِلْوُضُوءِ مُطْلَقًا، لا كَوْنُهُ مُوجِبًا لَهُ مَعَ وُجُودِ المَانِعِ. وَمِثَالُ الرَّابِعِ: بِأَنْ يَقُولَ المُدَّعِي: حَقِيقَةُ الإِنْسَانِ مَوْجُودَةٌ، لِأَنَّهُ شَيْءٌ، وَحَقَائِقُ الأَشْيَاءِ مَوْجُودَةٌ، فَيُورَدُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ حَقِيقَةٍ مِنَ الحَقَائِقِ يَلْزَمُ مُحَالٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَتْ مَوْجُودَةً فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ وُجُودُهَا مَوْجُودًا أَوْ لا، فَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي فَكَيْفَ يُوجَدُ بِدُونِ الوُجُودِ؟ وَإِنْ كَانَ الأَوَّلُ يَتَكَلَّمُ فِي وُجُودِ ذَلِكَ الوُجُودِ، وَهَكَذَا، فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ إِلَى وُجُودٍ لا وُجُودَ لَهُ أَوْ يَتَسَلْسَلَ، وَكِلاهُمَا مُحَالاَنِ، وَنَدْفَعُهُ بِأَنْ لا نُسَلِّمُ لُزُومَ المُحَالِ، وَإِنَّمَا يَلْزَمُ لَوْ كَانَ حَقِيقَةُ الوُجُودِ وُجُودِيَّةً، وَلا نُسَلِّمُ ذَلِكَ، وَلَوْ سُلِّمَ فَوُجُودُهُ عَيْنُهُ. وَمِثَالُ الخَامِسِ: أَنَا نَقُولُ: إِنَّ فِعْلَ زَيْدٍ وَعَمْرٍو بِخَلْقِ اللهِ تَعَالَى، لِأَنَّهُ فِعْلُ عَبْدٍ، وَأَفْعَالُ العَبْدِ بِخَلْقِهِ تَعَالَى، فَيُورَدُ عَلَيْهِ النَّقْضُ مِنْ قِبَلِ المُعْتَزِلِيِّ بِالزِّنَا، بِأَنْ يَقُولَ: الزِّنَا فِعْلٌ مِنْ أَفْعَالِ العِبَادِ، وَلَيْسَ بِخَلْقِ اللهِ تَعَالَى، لِأَنَّهُ قَبِيحٌ، وَخَلْقُ القَبِيحِ قَبِيحٌ، وَاتِّصَافُهُ بِهِ تَعَالَى مُحَالٌ، وَنَدْفَعُهُ بِمَنْعِ كَوْنِ خَلْقِ الزِّنَا قَبِيحًا وَمُحَالاً، وَإِنَّمَا القَبِيحُ وَالمُحَالُ فِعْلُهُ لا خَلْقُهُ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا بَوْنٌ لا يُخْفَى.
The [objection's] conclusion not following due to a hindrance, or by denying its entailment of the impossible, or by denying impossibility. Example of the first: When the arguer says: Urine is impure exiting from the human body, so it is an event that invalidates ablution, then the questioner repels it by denying the application of the proof by saying: We do not accept that it is impure exiting; rather it is apparent, because under every skin is moisture, and when the skin leaves it, it appears. Example of the second: As when that non-following is repelled by saying: That blood is not impure, because washing that spot is not necessary, so the absence of the ruling is due to absence of the cause, not with its presence. Example of the third: When it is objected to that reasoning that what comes out of the wound of one with a flowing wound is impure exiting from the human body, while it is not an invalidating event, since purification is not nullified by it as long as the time remains, to repel it we say: The required ruling is not delayed from the evidence, but exists, but did not appear at the moment due to a hindrance; otherwise the legally responsible person could not perform [the prayer], and for this reason purification becomes necessary after the time exits due to that event, not due to time exit, for it is not an event by consensus, and the ruling is its being an event necessitating ablution absolutely, not its necessitating it in the presence of a hindrance. Example of the fourth: When the claimant says: The reality of man exists, because it is a thing, and the realities of things exist, then it is objected that upon the assumption of a reality among realities, an impossibility follows, namely that if it existed, then either its existence exists or not; if the second, how can it exist without existence? If the first, then one speaks about the existence of that existence, and so on, leading either to an existence that has no existence or to infinite regress, both impossible. And we repel it by not accepting the entailment of impossibility; it would only entail if the reality of existence were existential, and we do not accept that; and if accepted, its existence is identical to it. Example of the fifth: We say: The action of Zayd and Amr is by the creation of Allah, because it is the action of a servant, and the actions of the servant are by His creation. Then an objection is raised by the Mu'tazili regarding adultery, saying: Adultery is an action of the servants, and it is not by the creation of Allah, because it is evil, and creating evil is evil, and attributing it to Allah is impossible. And we repel it by denying that creating adultery is evil and impossible; rather the evil and impossible is its doing, not its creation, and between them is an obvious difference.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines three methods of repelling objections (daf') in dialectical argumentation and provides five examples. The three methods are: (1) showing that the conclusion (ruling) does not follow from the evidence due to the presence of a hindrance (takhalluf li-mani'), (2) denying that the evidence entails an impossibility (man' istilzamih li-l-muhal), and (3) denying the impossibility alleged by the objector (man' al-istihala). The author then illustrates each with concrete examples from fiqh and kalam.

The first example involves a claim that urine is an impure substance exiting the body, thus invalidating ablution. The objector repels this by denying that urine is impure; he argues it is merely moisture that appears under the skin, so it does not have the property of being impure. This is an instance of denying the premise (man' al-muqaddima) to show that the conclusion does not follow.

The second example deals with blood from a wound. The objector repels by noting that if the blood were impure, one would be required to wash the spot, but since no such requirement exists, the cause is absent. This illustrates the 'takhalluf li-mani'' method: the ruling is absent because the cause is absent, not because it is present but hindered.

The third example is more nuanced: a flowing wound that does not nullify ablution immediately, but does so after the prayer time ends. The response is that the ruling (being an event) does exist, but its effect is delayed due to a hindrance (the need to allow the believer to pray). This is also 'takhalluf li-mani'' but with a different twist: the ruling exists but is temporarily blocked.

The fourth example is a metaphysical one concerning the reality (haqiqa) of man. The objector argues that if realities exist, then either the existence of each reality is itself existent or not, leading to an infinite regress or a self-contradiction. The defender repels by denying that such entailment is necessary: the reality of existence is not a separate existence, and if it were, it would be identical to its existence. This is an instance of 'man' istilzamih li-l-muhal' (denying that a certain consequence is impossible).

The fifth example is a kalam dispute: the Mu'tazili objects to the Ash'ari claim that all actions are created by Allah by citing adultery, which is evil, and arguing that Allah cannot create evil. The defender repels by distinguishing between creating an evil act (which is not evil) and performing it (which is evil). This again uses 'man' al-istihala' by denying that creating adultery is impossible.

Throughout, the passage demonstrates the dialectical method of responding to objections without immediately accepting the opponent's framework.

Key Terms
دفع
منع
تخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of daf', what method is used to repel the objection that urine is a nullifier of ablution?
A) Denying the premise by claiming the urine is not impure exiting but merely apparent moisture.
B) Showing that the ruling does not follow due to a hindrance.
C) Denying that the evidence entails an impossibility.
D) Accepting the premise but denying the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The two fiqh examples show that daf' can be executed by denying a premise or by pointing out that the ruling is absent due to the absence of the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which method of daf' is used in the following objection: A mu'allil claims that alcohol is impure because it is an intoxicant, and intoxicants are impure. The objector says: 'We do not accept that all intoxicants are impure; only khamr is impure by nass, so the evidence does not apply.'
?
Construct a daf' argument against the claim that 'every liquid that is intoxicating is impure' by using the second method (takhalluf li-mani') showing that the ruling does not follow.

Dialectic -- Jadal
advanced
10 min
Advanced Daf': Hindrance, Impossibility, and Kalam
Continues with three more examples of daf', including delayed ruling, metaphysical infinite regress, and kalam dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze complex examples of daf' involving a delayed ruling, infinite regress, and the Mu'tazili objection on divine creation, and identify the method used.

Core Idea
Daf' can involve time-dependent hindrances and metaphysical paradoxes.

The previous lesson covered simple daf' examples. Here we encounter more sophisticated scenarios: a hindrance that delays a ruling, a metaphysical objection about the existence of realities, and a theological dispute with the Mu'tazila. These demonstrate the depth and versatility of daf' in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the third example: hindrance delaying the ruling
The objector points to a wound that does not nullify ablution immediately, so it seems the ruling does not follow. The defender repels by saying the ruling exists but is delayed due to a hindrance (the need to allow prayer). After the time exits, the ruling applies. This is takhalluf li-mani': the conclusion follows but is blocked temporarily.
Note that the hindrance is a valid cause for delay, not a denial of the evidence.
2
Analyze the fourth example: denying entailment of impossibility
The claimant argues that the reality of man exists. The objector says this leads to an infinite regress or contradiction. The defender repels by denying that such entailment is necessary: the reality of existence is not separate; it is identical to existence. This is man' istilzam al-muhal.
This is a sophisticated metaphysical reply.
3
Analyze the fifth example: denying impossibility in kalam
The Mu'tazili objects that if Allah created all actions, He would create adultery, which is evil, so He would be evil. The defender repels by denying that creating evil is evil; what is evil is performing it, not creating it. This is man' al-istihala: denying that creating adultery is impossible for Allah.
This distinguishes between creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb).
4
Identify the common pattern
All three examples show the defender using one of the three methods without accepting the objector's framing. The defender offers a specific reason why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّخَلُّفُ لِمَانِعٍ، أَوْ بِمَنْعِ اسْتِلْزَامِهِ لِلْمُحَالِ، أَوْ بِمَنْعِ الاسْتِحَالَةِ. وَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ: مَا إِذَا قَالَ الْمُعَلِّلُ: البَوْلُ نَجَسٌ خَارِجٌ مِنْ بَدَنِ الإِنْسَانِ، فَيَكُونُ حَدَثًا، فَيَدْفَعُهُ السَّائِلُ بِمَنْعِ جَرَيَانِ الدَّلِيلِ بِأَنْ يَقُولَ: لا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّهُ نَجَسٌ خَارِجٌ، بَلْ هُوَ بَادٍ، لِأَنَّ تَحْتَ كُلِّ جِلْدٍ رُطُوبَةً، فَإِذَا فَارَقَهَا الجِلْدُ بَدَتْ. وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي: كَمَا إِذَا انْدَفَعَ ذَلِكَ التَّخَلُّفُ بِأَنْ نَقُولَ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ الدَّمَ لَيْسَ بِنَجَسٍ، لِأَنَّهُ لا يَلْزَمُ غَسْلُ ذَلِكَ المَوْضِعِ، فَانْعِدَامُ الحُكْمِ لانْعِدَامِ العِلَّةِ، لا مَعَ وُجُودِهَا. وَمِثَالُ الثَّالِثِ: أَنَّهُ إِذَا أُورِدَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ التَّعْلِيلِ أَنَّ مَا يَخْرُجُ مِنْ جُرْحِ صَاحِبِ الجُرْحِ السَّائِلِ نَجَسٌ خَارِجٌ مِنْ بَدَنِ الإِنْسَانِ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَدَثٍ، حَيْثُ لَمْ تَنْتَقِضْ بِهِ الطَّهَارَةُ مَادَامَ الوَقْتُ بَاقِيًا، لِدَفْعِهِ بِأَنْ نَقُولَ: لَيْسَ الحُكْمُ المَطْلُوبُ مُتَخَلِّفًا عَنْ الدَّلِيلِ بَلْ هُوَ مَوْجُودٌ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يَظْهَرْ فِي الحَالِ لِوُجُودِ مَانِعٍ، وَإِلا لَمْ يَتَمَكَّنِ المُكَلَّفُ مِنَ الأَدَاءِ، وَلِهَذَا يَلْزَمُ الطَّهَارَةُ بَعْدَ خُرُوجِ الوَقْتِ بِذَلِكَ الحَدَثِ، لا بِخُرُوجِ الوَقْتِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَدَثٍ بِالإِجْمَاعِ، وَالحُكْمُ هُوَ كَوْنُهُ حَدَثًا مُوجِبًا لِلْوُضُوءِ مُطْلَقًا، لا كَوْنُهُ مُوجِبًا لَهُ مَعَ وُجُودِ المَانِعِ. وَمِثَالُ الرَّابِعِ: بِأَنْ يَقُولَ المُدَّعِي: حَقِيقَةُ الإِنْسَانِ مَوْجُودَةٌ، لِأَنَّهُ شَيْءٌ، وَحَقَائِقُ الأَشْيَاءِ مَوْجُودَةٌ، فَيُورَدُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ حَقِيقَةٍ مِنَ الحَقَائِقِ يَلْزَمُ مُحَالٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَتْ مَوْجُودَةً فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ وُجُودُهَا مَوْجُودًا أَوْ لا، فَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي فَكَيْفَ يُوجَدُ بِدُونِ الوُجُودِ؟ وَإِنْ كَانَ الأَوَّلُ يَتَكَلَّمُ فِي وُجُودِ ذَلِكَ الوُجُودِ، وَهَكَذَا، فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ إِلَى وُجُودٍ لا وُجُودَ لَهُ أَوْ يَتَسَلْسَلَ، وَكِلاهُمَا مُحَالاَنِ، وَنَدْفَعُهُ بِأَنْ لا نُسَلِّمُ لُزُومَ المُحَالِ، وَإِنَّمَا يَلْزَمُ لَوْ كَانَ حَقِيقَةُ الوُجُودِ وُجُودِيَّةً، وَلا نُسَلِّمُ ذَلِكَ، وَلَوْ سُلِّمَ فَوُجُودُهُ عَيْنُهُ. وَمِثَالُ الخَامِسِ: أَنَا نَقُولُ: إِنَّ فِعْلَ زَيْدٍ وَعَمْرٍو بِخَلْقِ اللهِ تَعَالَى، لِأَنَّهُ فِعْلُ عَبْدٍ، وَأَفْعَالُ العَبْدِ بِخَلْقِهِ تَعَالَى، فَيُورَدُ عَلَيْهِ النَّقْضُ مِنْ قِبَلِ المُعْتَزِلِيِّ بِالزِّنَا، بِأَنْ يَقُولَ: الزِّنَا فِعْلٌ مِنْ أَفْعَالِ العِبَادِ، وَلَيْسَ بِخَلْقِ اللهِ تَعَالَى، لِأَنَّهُ قَبِيحٌ، وَخَلْقُ القَبِيحِ قَبِيحٌ، وَاتِّصَافُهُ بِهِ تَعَالَى مُحَالٌ، وَنَدْفَعُهُ بِمَنْعِ كَوْنِ خَلْقِ الزِّنَا قَبِيحًا وَمُحَالاً، وَإِنَّمَا القَبِيحُ وَالمُحَالُ فِعْلُهُ لا خَلْقُهُ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا بَوْنٌ لا يُخْفَى.
The [objection's] conclusion not following due to a hindrance, or by denying its entailment of the impossible, or by denying impossibility. Example of the first: When the arguer says: Urine is impure exiting from the human body, so it is an event that invalidates ablution, then the questioner repels it by denying the application of the proof by saying: We do not accept that it is impure exiting; rather it is apparent, because under every skin is moisture, and when the skin leaves it, it appears. Example of the second: As when that non-following is repelled by saying: That blood is not impure, because washing that spot is not necessary, so the absence of the ruling is due to absence of the cause, not with its presence. Example of the third: When it is objected to that reasoning that what comes out of the wound of one with a flowing wound is impure exiting from the human body, while it is not an invalidating event, since purification is not nullified by it as long as the time remains, to repel it we say: The required ruling is not delayed from the evidence, but exists, but did not appear at the moment due to a hindrance; otherwise the legally responsible person could not perform [the prayer], and for this reason purification becomes necessary after the time exits due to that event, not due to time exit, for it is not an event by consensus, and the ruling is its being an event necessitating ablution absolutely, not its necessitating it in the presence of a hindrance. Example of the fourth: When the claimant says: The reality of man exists, because it is a thing, and the realities of things exist, then it is objected that upon the assumption of a reality among realities, an impossibility follows, namely that if it existed, then either its existence exists or not; if the second, how can it exist without existence? If the first, then one speaks about the existence of that existence, and so on, leading either to an existence that has no existence or to infinite regress, both impossible. And we repel it by not accepting the entailment of impossibility; it would only entail if the reality of existence were existential, and we do not accept that; and if accepted, its existence is identical to it. Example of the fifth: We say: The action of Zayd and Amr is by the creation of Allah, because it is the action of a servant, and the actions of the servant are by His creation. Then an objection is raised by the Mu'tazili regarding adultery, saying: Adultery is an action of the servants, and it is not by the creation of Allah, because it is evil, and creating evil is evil, and attributing it to Allah is impossible. And we repel it by denying that creating adultery is evil and impossible; rather the evil and impossible is its doing, not its creation, and between them is an obvious difference.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines three methods of repelling objections (daf') in dialectical argumentation and provides five examples. The three methods are: (1) showing that the conclusion (ruling) does not follow from the evidence due to the presence of a hindrance (takhalluf li-mani'), (2) denying that the evidence entails an impossibility (man' istilzamih li-l-muhal), and (3) denying the impossibility alleged by the objector (man' al-istihala). The author then illustrates each with concrete examples from fiqh and kalam.

The first example involves a claim that urine is an impure substance exiting the body, thus invalidating ablution. The objector repels this by denying that urine is impure; he argues it is merely moisture that appears under the skin, so it does not have the property of being impure. This is an instance of denying the premise (man' al-muqaddima) to show that the conclusion does not follow.

The second example deals with blood from a wound. The objector repels by noting that if the blood were impure, one would be required to wash the spot, but since no such requirement exists, the cause is absent. This illustrates the 'takhalluf li-mani'' method: the ruling is absent because the cause is absent, not because it is present but hindered.

The third example is more nuanced: a flowing wound that does not nullify ablution immediately, but does so after the prayer time ends. The response is that the ruling (being an event) does exist, but its effect is delayed due to a hindrance (the need to allow the believer to pray). This is also 'takhalluf li-mani'' but with a different twist: the ruling exists but is temporarily blocked.

The fourth example is a metaphysical one concerning the reality (haqiqa) of man. The objector argues that if realities exist, then either the existence of each reality is itself existent or not, leading to an infinite regress or a self-contradiction. The defender repels by denying that such entailment is necessary: the reality of existence is not a separate existence, and if it were, it would be identical to its existence. This is an instance of 'man' istilzamih li-l-muhal' (denying that a certain consequence is impossible).

The fifth example is a kalam dispute: the Mu'tazili objects to the Ash'ari claim that all actions are created by Allah by citing adultery, which is evil, and arguing that Allah cannot create evil. The defender repels by distinguishing between creating an evil act (which is not evil) and performing it (which is evil). This again uses 'man' al-istihala' by denying that creating adultery is impossible.

Throughout, the passage demonstrates the dialectical method of responding to objections without immediately accepting the opponent's framework.

منع
تخلف

C) By agreeing that Allah cannot create evil.
D) By claiming that adultery is not an action.

Key Takeaway
These advanced examples show that daf' can involve time-dependent hindrances, metaphysical distinctions, and theological nuances, all within the same three-method framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify the method of daf' used in the following exchange: A claimant says 'The world is eternal because it cannot be created from nothing.' The objector says 'This leads to an infinite regress of causes.' The defender replies 'We do not accept that an infinite regress is impossible here.'

Construct a daf' argument against a Mu'tazili who says 'If Allah decrees evil, He is unjust.' Use the method of man' al-istihala (deny that the impossibility follows) and support with a distinction between decree and commission.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
5 min
The Three Types of Denying the Signification (Nafi al-Madlul)
Classification of denial of signification into three types with their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three forms of denial of signification in a jadal exchange and determine which are admissible.

Core Idea
In dialectics, denying the opponent's claim is only legitimate after their proof and with your own evidence.

In a structured dialectical debate (munazara), the questioner has specific tools to challenge the claimant. One of these is 'denial of the signification' (nafi al-madlul) — directly asserting that the opponent's conclusion is false. But not all denials are equal. The logicians classify them into three types based on whether evidence is provided and when it is presented. This passage from the Rāshidiyya commentary explains these types and their rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types in the matn
Find and list the three Arabic terms used in the matn: مكابرة (mukabara), غصب (ghasb), and معارضة (mu'arada). Note that each corresponds to a different way of denying the opponent's claim (madlul).
The matn states: 'Denial without evidence is mukabara; with evidence before the opponent's proof is ghasb; after the opponent's proof is mu'arada.'
2
Understand the basis of classification
The classification depends on two factors: (1) Does the denier provide evidence? (2) If yes, is the evidence presented before or after the opponent has established his own proof? Mukabara lacks evidence; ghasb has evidence but too early; mu'arada has evidence at the correct time.
Timing is crucial: evidence before the proof is deemed usurpation.
3
Examine the ruling for each type
According to the text: mukabara is 'not heard' (la tusma'u); ghasb is also not accepted by the verifiers (muhaqqiqin); mu'arada is the legitimate form of counter-argument. The commentary explains that ghasb is metaphorical because the opponent's claim is called 'signification' before proof, based on its eventual status.
Mu'arada is the only type that is accepted in formal debate.
4
Connect to the broader jadal framework
Place this classification alongside other tools: man' (request for evidence on a premise) and naqd (refutation of the proof). Nafi al-madlul targets the conclusion directly, whereas man' and naqd attack the structure of the proof. Note that mu'arada is equivalent to establishing evidence to deny the conclusion.
The commentary clarifies that mu'arada here is defined as denial, but its earlier definition was 'establishing evidence' – a nuance due to the close connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَفْيُ الْمَدْلُولِ مِنْ غَيْرِ الدَّلِيلِ مُكَابَرَةٌ لا تُسْمَعُ، وَمَعَ الدَّلِيلِ قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ غَصْبٌ، وَبَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَيْهِ مُعَارَضَةٌ. (نفي المدلول) أَنْ يَكُونَ قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ الْمُدَّعِي الدَّلِيلَ أَوْ بَعْدَهَا وَمِنْ غَيْرِ الدَّلِيلِ) عَلَيْهِ بِأَنْ يَقُولَ السَّائِلُ: هَذَا الْمَدْلُولُ لَيْسَ بِصَحِيحٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يُقِيمَ عَلَى عَدَمِ صِحَّتِهِ دَلِيلاً (مُكَابَرَةٌ) لا تُسْمَعُ (و) نَفْيُهُ (مَعَ) إِقَامَةِ السَّائِلِ (الدَّلِيلَ) عَلَيْهِ) قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ) الْمُدَّعِي (الدَّلِيلَ) عَلَيْهِ (غَصْبٌ) سُمِّيَ - قَدَّسَ سِرَّهُ - الْمُدَّعِي قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ الْمُدَّعِي الدَّلِيلَ مَدْلُولاً مَجَازًا بِاعْتِبَارِ مَا يُؤَوَّلُ إِلَيْهِ، أَوْ لأَنَّهُ مِنْ شَأْنِهِ أَنْ يُقِيمَ الدَّلِيلَ عَلَيْهِ، أَوْ لِمُنَاسَبَةِ قَوْلِهِ: وَبَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ. ثُمَّ الْغَصْبُ لَيْسَ بِمَسْمُوعٍ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ (١) وَبَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ) أَيِ الْمُعَلِّلِ (الدَّلِيلَ عَلَيْهِ) أَيْ عَلَى الْمَدْلُولِ (مُعَارَضَةٌ) وَلا يَذْهَبُ عَلَيْكَ أَنَّهُ يُفْهَمُ مِنْ هَذَا الْكَلامِ أَنَّ الْمُعَارَضَةَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ النَّفْيِ، وَتَعْرِيفُهَا السَّابِقُ دَلِيلٌ وَاضِحٌ عَلَى أَنَّهَا إِقَامَةُ الدَّلِيلِ، فَلَعَلَّ لِمَكَانِ الْمُلازَمَةِ بَيْنَ الْمَعْنَيَيْنِ أُطْلِقَ عَلَيْهِمَا. (١) تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ الْمَنْعَ هُوَ طَلَبُ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ مُعَيَّنَةٍ، وَأَنَّ النَّقْضَ إِبْطَالُ دَلِيلِ الْمُعَلِّلِ بَعْدَ تَمَامِهِ، وَهُنَا بَيَّنَ الْمُصَنِّفُ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ - حُكْمَ مَا إِذَا سَلَكَ السَّائِلُ غَيْرَ طَرِيقِهِ بَأنْ نَفَى الْمَدْلُولَ دُونَ الدَّلِيلِ، وَكَانَ نَفْيُهُ لَهُ بِدُونِ دَلِيلٍ سَوَاءً كَانَ قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ الْمُعَلِّلِ الدَّلِيلَ أَوْ بَعْدَهَا. بَيَّنَ الْمُصَنِّفُ أَنَّ مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ الْعَمَلِ مِنَ السَّائِلِ مُكَابَرَةٌ أَيْ لَيْسَتْ مُنَاظَرَةً وَإِذَنْ لا تُسْمَعُ كَمَا أَنَّ السَّائِلَ إِذَا أَقَامَ الدَّلِيلَ عَلَى نَفْيِ الْمَدْلُولِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يُقِيمَ الْمُعَلِّلُ الدَّلِيلَ عَلَيْهِ كَانَ ذَلِكَ مِنْهُ غَصْبًا.
Denial of the signification without evidence is obstinacy [that] is not heard. And with evidence before the establishment of the evidence[ by the opponent] is usurpation. And after the establishment of the evidence upon it is opposition. (Denial of the signification) is that it be before the claimant's establishment of the evidence or after it, and from without evidence upon it, as when the questioner says: This signification is not correct, without establishing a proof of its incorrectness (is) obstinacy not heard. And his denial with the questioner's establishment of the evidence upon it before the claimant's establishment of the evidence upon it is usurpation. He called - may his secret be sanctified - the claimant before the claimant's establishment of the evidence a signification metaphorically, considering what it leads to, or because it is his function to establish the evidence upon it, or for appropriateness with his saying: 'and after establishment of the evidence.' Then usurpation is not heard according to the verifiers. (1) And after the establisher's establishment of the evidence upon it, i.e., upon the signification, it is opposition. And do not be unaware that from this speech it is understood that opposition is an expression for denial, and its previous definition is clear evidence that it is the establishment of evidence; perhaps because of the connection between the two meanings, both are used. (1) It was said earlier that al-man' (prevention) is asking for evidence on a specific premise, and that al-naqd (refutation) is invalidating the evidence of the complainant after its completion. Here the author - may Allah have mercy on him - explained the ruling when the questioner follows a different path by denying the signification without [touching] the evidence, and his denial of it is without evidence, whether it be before or after the complainant's establishment of the evidence. The author explained that such action by the questioner is obstinacy, i.e., not a debate, and therefore it is not heard. Likewise, if the questioner establishes evidence for denying the signification before the complainant establishes evidence for it, that is from him usurpation.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the act of denying the signification (nafi al-madlul) in a dialectical context. The main text (matn) states three types: (1) Denial without any evidence (mukabara) - this is mere obstinacy and is not admitted in debate. (2) Denial with evidence, but before the opponent has presented his proof (ghasb) - this is considered usurpation because it jumps ahead; it is also not accepted by the verifiers. (3) Denial with evidence after the opponent has presented his proof (mu'arada) - this is legitimate counter-argument.

The commentary expands on each. For mukabara, it explains that the questioner simply asserts the falsity of the claim without providing any evidence, whether before or after the opponent's proof. Such an act is dismissed as not being a proper debate. For ghasb, the commentary notes that the questioner provides evidence to deny the opponent's claim before the opponent has even established his own evidence. The term 'ghasb' (usurpation) is used metaphorically because the opponent's claim is referred to as 'signification' before its proof is given, either because it will eventually be proven or because it is in the process of being proven. Ghasb, like mukabara, is not accepted. For mu'arada, it is denial backed by evidence after the opponent's proof is complete; this is the proper form of counter-argument.

The commentary also clarifies a potential confusion: the earlier definition of mu'arada was 'establishing evidence', but here it is used to mean 'denial with evidence'. The commentator notes that this is justified because there is a necessary connection between the two meanings: establishing evidence to deny is a type of establishing evidence.

The footnote adds context: man' is requesting evidence for a specific premise, and naqd is invalidating the opponent's argument after its completion. This passage deals with a different scenario where the questioner bypasses the opponent's argument entirely and directly denies the conclusion. The ruling is that without evidence it is obstinacy; with evidence but prematurely it is usurpation; and with evidence after the opponent's argument it is legitimate opposition.

Key Terms
نفي المدلول
مكابرة
غصب
معارضة

Checkpoint
In the classification of nafi al-madlul, what is the status of a questioner who denies the opponent's conclusion but provides a proof before the opponent has offered any proof?
A) It is called mukabara and is not heard.
B) It is called ghasb and is not heard.
C) It is called mu'arada and is accepted.
D) It is valid because evidence is provided.

Key Takeaway
The legitimacy of denying an opponent's conclusion in jadal depends on two factors: having evidence and presenting it after the opponent's proof; otherwise it is either obstinacy or usurpation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following scenario: The claimant argues that wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant. The questioner replies 'That's false' without any evidence. Identify which type of denial this is and whether it is accepted.
?

Types of Denial in Debate and the Requirement of Accepting the Opponent's Evidence
Author defines three types of denial and debates requirement of accepting opponent's evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three types of denial of the indicated in jadal and identify the positions on whether acceptance of the opponent's evidence is required for objection.

Core Idea
Denial without evidence is stubborn; denial out of order is usurpation; only timely counter-evidence is legitimate objection.

In dialectical debate, the questioner's denial of the opponent's claim must follow strict rules. This passage from the Rashidiyya defines three types of denial based on timing and evidential support, then enters a key debate: must the questioner first accept the opponent's evidence before objecting? Understanding this classification and debate is essential for mastering the procedures of jadal and munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of denial
Locate the three categories the author lists: denial without evidence (mukabara), denial with evidence before the opponent's evidence (ghasb), and denial with evidence after the opponent's evidence (mu'arada). Understand their definitions and procedural contexts.
The author explicitly names and defines each type in the first sentence.
2
Distinguish legitimate from illegitimate
Which types are permitted and which are prohibited? Mukabara and ghasb are not permitted; mu'arada is legitimate. Reflect on why timing and evidence matter in debate.
The passage states that mukabara is 'not to be heard' and ghasb is also prohibited.
3
Locate the debate on taslim
Identify the two positions: (1) acceptance of the opponent's evidence is required for objection (the more famous view), and (2) it is not required (the author's more apparent view). Understand the reasoning behind each position, especially the objection that requiring acceptance leads to affirming contradictories.
The debate is introduced with 'هل يشترط فيها تسليم دليل الخصم أم لا'.
4
Understand the consequence and the proposed solution
The second opinion limits the questioner's function to man' and naqd. To avoid this, some scholars propose that every mu'arada should be framed as a refutation (tariq al-naqd). Understand how this works by reformulating the objection as: 'If your argument were sound, then what contradicts its conclusion would not be true; but I have evidence showing its truth.'
The passage ends by explaining this method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي المدلول من غير الدليل مكابرة لا تسمع، ومع الدليل قبل إقامة الدليل غصب، وبعد إقامة الدليل عليه معارضة، وهل يشترط فيها تسليم دليل الخصم ولو من حيث الظاهر أم لا؟ الأول أشهر، والثاني أظهر، لكن يلزم حصر وظيفة السائل في المنع والنقض، ومن هنا التزم بعضهم تقريرها مطلقا بطريق النقض. ثم اختلف في اشتراط التسليم وإليه إشارة بقوله: (وهل يشترط فيها تسليم دليل الخصم ولو من حيث الظاهر أم لا) الأول أشهر والثاني أظهر لأن تسليم دليل المعلل يستلزم تصديق مدلوله بحسب الظاهر، فيلزم تصديق المتنافيين، ولك أن تقول: إن مرادهم بالتسليم تسليم دلالته على مدعي الخصم، ولا يلزم من ذلك تسليم مدعاه، حتى يلزم تصديق المتنافين ( لكن يلزم ) على الثاني ) حصر وظيفة السائل في المنع والنقض ولا يخفى عليك أنه إنما يلزم الحصر أن لو اشترط عدم التسليم ، أما إذا لم يشترط عدم التسليم فلا ، لأنه حينئذ يوجد معارضة مع التسليم ، غايته أن المعارضة الغير مقرونة مع التسليم يجوز أن تندرج في النقض . و من ههنا ( أي من أجل عدم اشتراط التسليم ) التزم بعضهم تقريرها مطلقا ( أعم من أن يكون معارضة فيها مناقضة، ومن أن يكون معارضة خالصة ) و بطريق النقض بأن يقال لو كان دليلكم بجميع مقدماته صحيحا لما يصدق ما ينافي مدلوله، لكن عندي دليل يدل على صدقه.
Denial of the indicated without evidence is stubborn refusal not to be heard, and with evidence before the establishment of the evidence is usurpation, and after the establishment of the evidence upon it is objection. And is acceptance of the opponent's evidence required in it, even apparently, or not? The first is more famous, the second is more apparent, but it necessitates limiting the function of the questioner to prevention and refutation, and from here some committed to presenting it absolutely via the method of refutation. Then they differed regarding the requirement of acceptance, and he referred to it by his saying: 'And is acceptance of the opponent's evidence required in it, even apparently, or not?' The first is more famous, the second is more apparent because acceptance of the opponent's evidence necessitates affirming its indicated according to appearance, so affirming two contradictories follows. But you can say: Their intent by acceptance is acceptance of its indication upon the opponent's claim, and that does not necessitate acceptance of his claim, such that affirming contradictories follows. But it necessitates, on the second, limiting the function of the questioner to prevention and refutation. And it is not hidden from you that the limitation only follows if non-acceptance is required; but if non-acceptance is not required, then not, because then there exists objection with acceptance, at most that the objection not accompanied by acceptance can be subsumed under refutation. And from here (i.e., due to non-requirement of acceptance) some committed to presenting it absolutely (whether it be an objection containing contradiction, or a pure objection) by the method of refutation, by saying: If your argument with all its premises were sound, then what contradicts its indicated would not be true, but I have evidence indicating its truth.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the rules for the questioner's denial of the opponent's claim (نفي المدلول) in dialectical debate. The author classifies denial into three types based on whether evidence is provided and its timing relative to the opponent's presentation of evidence. First, denial without any evidence is called مكابرة (stubborn refusal) and is not permitted. Second, denial with evidence before the opponent has presented his evidence is called غصب (usurpation) and is also not permitted. Third, denial with evidence after the opponent's evidence is called معارضة (objection), which is a legitimate dialectical move. The author then raises the question whether in a valid معارضة the questioner must first accept (تسليم) the opponent's evidence, at least apparently. Two opinions are given: the first (must accept) is more famous among scholars, but the second (not required) is more apparent because requiring acceptance would force the questioner to affirm two contradictory statements—the opponent's claim and his own counter-evidence. However, the author clarifies that 'acceptance of the evidence' means accepting that the evidence indicates the opponent's claim, not that the claim itself is true, so no contradiction arises. The author notes that the second opinion leads to a limitation: the questioner's role would be restricted to only منع (prevention, i.e., objecting to a premise) and نقض (refutation, i.e., showing a contradiction in the opponent's argument), because if the questioner can object without accepting the opponent's evidence, then every معارضة could be framed as a refutation. To avoid this, some scholars propose that معارضة should always be presented through the method of refutation (بطريق النقض), i.e., saying: 'If your argument were sound, then what contradicts its conclusion would not be true; but I have evidence that shows its truth.' This effectively turns the objection into a refutation, preserving the questioner's full range of responses.

Key Terms
نفي المدلول
مكابرة
غصب
معارضة
تسليم دليل الخصم
منع
نقض
طريق النقض

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of denial of the indicated is considered legitimate and permitted in debate?
A) مكابرة (stubborn refusal)
B) غصب (usurpation)
C) معارضة (objection)
D) مكابرة and غصب

Key Takeaway
In jadal, only timely counter-evidence (mu'arada) is permissible; the debate over taslim reflects a tension between procedural rigor and dialectical flexibility, resolved by some through the method of refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following scenario: In a debate, the questioner says: 'Your argument is invalid because the premise is false,' without providing any reason. What type of denial is this? Why is it not permitted?
?
Given two positions on taslim, explain the implication for the questioner's role: if taslim is required, what happens to the questioner's ability to counter with new evidence? How does framing mu'arada as refutation resolve the limitation?

The Condition of Taslim in Mu'arada: Acceptance of the Opponent's Proof in Dialectical Counter-Argument
Author discusses whether acceptance of opponent's proof is required in counter-argument and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the debate over whether accepting the opponent's proof (taslim) is required for a valid counter-argument (mu'arada) and identify the consequences for the questioner's role.

Core Idea
Acceptance of the opponent's proof can lead to self-contradiction; the debate turns on whether that risk is acceptable.

In formal Islamic dialectic (munazara), the questioner's counter-argument (mu'arada) is a key move. A critical issue is whether the questioner must first accept (taslim) the opponent's proof before presenting a counter-argument. This passage from al-Rashidiyya presents the two positions: the more famous view requiring taslim, and the more apparent view that does not. The author explains the logical consequences of each position, especially the risk of affirming contradictory statements. Understanding this nuance is essential for respecting the rules of productive theological and legal debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage asks: 'Is acceptance of the opponent's proof required in a counter-argument, even in its apparent form, or not?' Locate the two positions explicitly mentioned.
The first position requires taslim; the second does not.
2
Understand the risk of requiring taslim
If the questioner accepts the opponent's proof, he must affirm its apparent implication. If he then offers a counter-argument that also claims truth, he may be committed to affirming two contradictory statements. Why is this problematic?
The contradiction is between the implication of the opponent's proof and the implication of the counter-argument.
3
Examine the nuance: acceptance of implication vs. acceptance of claim
The author suggests that taslim might only mean accepting that the proof implies the opponent's claim, not the claim itself. Evaluate whether this avoids contradiction.
This nuance is presented as a possible interpretation to mitigate the contradiction.
4
Analyze the consequence of not requiring taslim
If taslim is not required, the questioner's role is limited to man' (objection to a premise) and naqḍ (refutation). The author clarifies that this limitation applies only if non-acceptance is explicitly conditioned; otherwise, counter-argument can coexist with acceptance.
The limitation is not absolute; it depends on the condition of non-acceptance.
5
Note the 'naqḍ' method as a form of counter-argument
The passage describes a method: 'If your proof with all its premises were sound, it would not be true what contradicts its implication, but I have a proof that indicates its truth.' Understand how this serves as a pure counter-argument without requiring taslim.
This method is used by some scholars to present the counter-argument absolutely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح الرشيدية
مِنْ حَيثُ الظاهِرُ أَمْ لا ؟ الأوَّلُ أَشْهَرُ ، والثاني أَظْهَرُ ، لَكِنْ يَلْزَمُ حَصْرُ وظيفَةِ السَّائلِ في المَنْعِ والنَّقْضِ ، ومِنْ هَهُنا التَزَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ تَقْرِيرَها مُطلقًا بِطَريقِ النَّفْضِ .
ثم اختلف في اشتراط التسليم وإليه إشارة بقوله : ( وهل يشترط فيها تسليم دليل الخصم ولو من حيث الظاهر» بأن لم يتعرض لدليله أصلا لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات أم لا يشترط الأول ) وهو الاشتراط أشهر والثاني وهو عدم الاشتراط ( أظهر لأن تسليم دليل المعلم يستلزم تصديق مدلوله بحسب الظاهر، فيلزم تصديق المتنافيين ، ولك أن تقول : إن مرادهم بالتسليم تسليم دلالته على مدعي الخصم ، ولا يلزم من ذلك تسليم مدعاه ، حتى يلزم تصديق المتنافين ( لكن يلزم ) على الثاني ) حصر وظيفة السائل في المنع والنقض ولا يخفى عليك أنه إنما يلزم الحصر أن لو اشترط عدم التسليم ، أما إذا لم يشترط عدم التسليم فلا ، لأنه حينئذ يوجد معارضة مع التسليم ، غايته أن المعارضة الغير مقرونة مع التسليم يجوز أن تندرج في النقض .
و من ههنا (( أي من أجل عدم اشتراط التسليم التزم بعضهم تقريرها مطلقا » أعم من أن يكون معارضة فيها مناقضة، ومن أن يكون معارضة خالصة و بطريق النقض بأن يقال لو كان دليلكم بجميع مقدماته صحيحا لما يصدق ما ينافي مدلوله، لكن عندي دليل يدل على صدقه .
From the standpoint of the apparent [meaning] or not? The first is more famous, the second is more apparent, but it necessitates limiting the function of the questioner to prevention and refutation. And from here some committed to presenting it absolutely by way of refutation. Then there is disagreement regarding the condition of acceptance (taslim), and to it is an indication in his saying: 'Is acceptance of the opponent's proof required, even in its apparent form, such that he does not address his proof at all, neither by negation nor affirmation, or is it not required?' The first (the condition) is more famous, and the second (non-condition) is more apparent, because acceptance of the teacher's proof necessitates affirming its implication according to the apparent, so it necessitates affirming two contradictory statements. But you may say: Their intention by acceptance is acceptance of its implication for the opponent's claim, and that does not necessitate acceptance of his claim such that affirming two contradictories is necessary. But it necessitates, according to the second, limiting the function of the questioner to prevention and refutation. And it is not hidden from you that the limitation is necessitated only if non-acceptance is conditioned. But if non-acceptance is not conditioned, then not, because then there is counter-argument with acceptance; at most, counter-argument not conjoined with acceptance may be included in refutation. And from here, i.e., due to not conditioning acceptance, some committed to presenting it absolutely, whether it is a counter-argument containing invalidation or a pure counter-argument, and by way of refutation, by saying: If your proof with all its premises were sound, it would not be true what contradicts its implication, but I have a proof that indicates its truth.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage discusses a key principle in the dialectic of munazara (debate): the question (sā'il) of whether, when presenting a counter-argument (mu'āraḍa), the questioner must first accept (taslīm) the opponent's proof. The author presents two views: the first, requiring acceptance, is more famous (ashhar); the second, not requiring it, is more apparent (azhar) logically. The reasoning is that accepting the opponent's proof forces one to affirm its apparent meaning, which could lead to affirming two contradictory statements if the counter-argument also claims truth. However, a nuance is offered: acceptance might only mean accepting that the proof implies the opponent's claim, not the claim itself, thus avoiding contradiction. The consequence of not requiring acceptance is that the questioner's role is limited to two moves: man' (objecting to a premise) and naqḍ (refuting the proof). The author clarifies that this limitation applies only if non-acceptance is explicitly conditioned; otherwise, counter-argument can coexist with acceptance. Some scholars therefore present the counter-argument absolutely (muṭlaqan), either as a pure counter-argument or one that contains a refutation (naqḍ), using the method of naqḍ by arguing: 'If your proof with all its premises were sound, it would not be true what contradicts its implication, but I have a proof that indicates its truth.'

Key Terms
المعارضة
التسليم
المنع
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the potential problem with requiring taslim (acceptance of the opponent's proof) before presenting a counter-argument?
A) It forces the questioner to accept the opponent's conclusion, ending the debate.
B) It may force the questioner to affirm two contradictory statements if a counter-argument is also presented.
C) It is incompatible with the use of man' (objection) and naqḍ (refutation).
D) It is rejected by all scholars as being logically unsound.

Key Takeaway
The condition of taslim in mu'arada is a debated issue: requiring it risks contradiction, while not requiring it limits the questioner's tools to man' and naqḍ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
