Classification of Mu'arada and its Relation to Qalb
Author classifies mu'arada and relates it to qalb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mu'arada in certain matters (qati'iyyat) and probable matters (zanniyyat), and explain the relationship between mu'arada fiha al-naqd and mu'arada bi'l-qalb.

Core Idea
Counter-argument in certain matters implicitly refutes; in probable matters it does not, and it is brother to inversion.

In dialectical disputes, not all counter-arguments are equal. This passage from the Rashidiyya explains that when the subject matter is certain (qaṭ'ī), a counter-argument automatically implies refutation (naqḍ), because two certain contradictory statements cannot both be true. In contrast, in probable matters like juristic analogy, a counter-argument does not necessarily refute the opponent. The passage then reveals a surprising kinship: counter-argument containing refutation and counter-argument by inversion (qalb) share the same essence, differing only in perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of mu'arada based on certainty
Locate where the author distinguishes between qati'iyyat and zanniyyat. What is the key difference in how mu'arada is treated in each?
2
Analyze the naming rationale
Why is it called 'mu'arada fiha al-naqd' and not 'naqd fiha mu'arada'? What does this tell us about the explicit and implicit elements?
3
Examine the brotherhood claim
Find the phrase 'huwa wa al-mu'arada bi'l-qalb akhawan'. What does 'akhawan' mean here? How do they differ by consideration (i'tibar)?
4
Connect to the broader framework
How does the concept of not requiring taslim (acceptance of the proof) relate to the possibility of mu'arada fiha munaqada? What new type emerges?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيلَ المُعَارَضَةُ فِي القَطْعِيَّاتِ رَاجَعَةٌ إِلَى النَّقْضِ ، ويُسَمَّى مُعارَضَةٌ فيها النَّقْضُ ، دُون النَّقْليَّاتِ ، وَقِيلَ هُوَ والمُعَارَضَةٌ بِالقَلْبِ أَخَوَانَ ، والتَّغاير بالاعتبار.
الدلائل العقلية والنقلية اليقينية راجعة إلى النقض لامتناع اجتماع القطعيين المتنافيين بحسب نفس الأمر ويسمى المذكور معارضة فيها النقض، وإنما سميت معارضة فيها النقض، ولم تسمى نقضا فيها معارضة، لأن المعارضة صريحة، والنقض ضمني والضمنيات لا تعتبر دون النقليات الظنية كالقياس الفقهي ، فإنه يجوز أن يكون أحد القاسين خطأ في نفس الأمر ، ويعارض القياس الصواب ، فلا حاجة إلى القول برجوعه إلى النقض وقيل هو أي معارضة فيها النقض، والمعارضة بالقلب أخوان أي متشاركان في الماهية والحقيقة والتغاير بينهما بالاعتبار فباعتبار أنها تقلب دليل المستدل شاهدا عليه بعد أن كان شاهدا له قلبا يسمى وباعتبار تضمنها معنى النقض معارضة فيها النقض. أي هذه تتمة البحث السابع الأول يترتب عليه الجمع بين المتنافيين . وأما الثاني فلا وهنا بين أنه بناء على عدم اشتراط تسليم الدليل يجوز أن تقرر بطريق النقض وحينئذ تسمى معارضة فيها مناقضة. أم كونها معارضة فلأنه أقام الدليل على خلاف ما أقام الدليل عليه الخصم . وأما أنها مناقضة . فلأن فيها إهمال دليل المعلل وفي الإهمال إيماء إلى بطلانه ضمنا وهذا هو النقض، فتكون المعارضة فيه صريحة والنقض ضمنا ولهذا قال المصنف . معارضة فيها مناقضة ولم يقل مناقضة فيها معارضة كما أنها يجوز أن تقرر بطريق المعارضة الصريحة كما قررت بطريق المعارضة التي فيها مناقضة . قلنا إن المعارضة تكون في إقامة دليل على نفي المدلول بعد إقامة المعلل الدليل عليه . وأنه إذا أقام دليله من غير تسليم دليل المعلل كانت معارضة فيها نقض . وهنا بين المصنف أن بعضهم قال : إن المعارضة الموجهة على القطعيات هي التي تسمى معارضة فيها نقض بخلاف النقليات والظنيات . لأن هذه يتأتى فيها أن يقام دليل آخر ينتج مدلولا آخر لأنها ظنية . بخلاف القطعيات
And it was said: Counter-argument in certain matters returns to refutation, and it is called 'counter-argument containing refutation', unlike transmitted matters. And it was said: It and counter-argument by inversion are brothers, and the difference is by consideration. The certain rational and transmitted proofs return to refutation because of the impossibility of two certain contradictory statements coexisting in reality, and the aforementioned is called 'counter-argument containing refutation'. It was named 'counter-argument containing refutation' and not 'refutation containing counter-argument' because the counter-argument is explicit and the refutation is implicit, and implicit things are not considered. Unlike probable transmitted matters, such as juristic analogy, for it is possible that one of the two analogies is erroneous in reality and contradicts the correct analogy, so there is no need to say that it returns to refutation. And it was said: It, i.e., counter-argument containing refutation, and counter-argument by inversion are brothers, i.e., sharing in essence and reality, and the difference between them is by consideration. For by considering that it overturns the prover's proof to be evidence against him after it was evidence for him, it is called [by inversion]; and by considering its inclusion of the meaning of refutation, it is called 'counter-argument containing refutation'. This is the completion of the seventh investigation: the first [type] entails bringing together two contradictories, but the second does not. And here it is explained that based on not requiring acceptance of the proof, it is permissible to present it by way of refutation, and then it is called 'counter-argument containing invalidation'. As for it being a counter-argument, it is because he presents a proof contrary to what the opponent presented. As for it being an invalidation, it is because it involves neglecting the proof of the explainer, and in neglect there is an indication of its falsity implicitly, and this is refutation. Thus the counter-argument is explicit and the refutation is implicit. For this reason the author said 'counter-argument containing invalidation' and did not say 'invalidation containing counter-argument'. Similarly, it is permissible to present it by way of explicit counter-argument just as it was presented by way of counter-argument containing invalidation. We said: Counter-argument consists in presenting a proof to negate the implication after the explainer has presented the proof for it. And that if he presents his proof without accepting the proof of the explainer, it is a counter-argument containing refutation. And here the author explained that some said: The counter-argument directed at certain matters is what is called 'counter-argument containing refutation', unlike transmitted and probable matters, because in these it is possible to present another proof that produces another implication, since they are probable, unlike certain matters.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage classifies counter-argument (mu'āraḍa) into types based on the certainty of the subject matter. It begins by stating a view: counter-argument in matters that are certain (qaṭ'iyyāt) reduces to refutation (naqḍ) and is called 'counter-argument containing refutation' (mu'āraḍa fīhā al-naqḍ). This applies to both rational and transmitted certain proofs, because two certain contradictory statements cannot both be true in reality, so one must be invalid. The reason it is named with counter-argument as the explicit element and refutation as implicit is that the counter-argument is the main overt move, while the refutation is understood implicitly. This is contrasted with probable matters (ẓanniyyāt), like juristic analogy (qiyās fiqhī), where it is possible that one analogy is erroneous and the other correct, so there is no need to claim that the counter-argument reduces to refutation. Next, another view is presented: counter-argument containing refutation and counter-argument by inversion (mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb) are 'brothers', meaning they share the same essence and reality, differing only in consideration (i'tibār). Inversion is where the proof is turned against the prover, while the other form contains refutation implicitly. The passage then explains that based on the earlier discussion of not requiring acceptance (taslīm), a counter-argument can be presented by way of refutation, becoming 'counter-argument containing invalidation' (mu'āraḍa fīhā munāqaḍa). The author emphasizes that the explicit aspect is the counter-argument and the implicit is the refutation, hence the naming. Finally, it reiterates that counter-argument is defined as presenting a proof that negates the implication after the opponent has presented his proof, and if done without accepting the opponent's proof, it includes refutation. The distinction between certain and probable matters is key: in certain matters, the counter-argument inherently implies refutation because both cannot be true; in probable matters, multiple conflicting proofs can coexist without immediate refutation.

Key Terms
القطعية (al-qaṭ'iyyāt): Matters that are certain, based on rational or transmitted proofs that are decisive; counter-arguments in such matters reduce to refutation. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, qati' often refers to definitive proof; here it is used in jadal context.]
الظنية (al-ẓanniyyāt): Probable matters, such as juristic analogies, where counter-arguments do not necessarily reduce to refutation. [Note: Stable across disciplines.]
المعارضة بالقلب (al-mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb): A counter-argument that overturns the opponent's proof, turning it against him; considered a 'brother' to mu'āraḍa fīhā al-naqḍ. [Note: Distinct from logical conversion ('aks); here it is a specific dialectical move.]
المناقضة (al-munāqaḍa): Invalidation; in this context, a counter-argument that implicitly refutes the opponent's proof by neglecting or undermining it. [Note: Sometimes synonymous with naqḍ; here it is a specific type of mu'āraḍa.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a debate about a certain theological matter (like the existence of the Creator), both sides present proofs that are considered certain (qaṭ'ī). According to the passage, how should the counter-argument be classified?
A) As a pure mu'āraḍa without refutation.
B) As a mu'āraḍa that reduces to refutation (naqḍ).
C) As a mu'āraḍa by inversion (qalb).
D) As a mu'āraḍa that is independent of naqḍ.

Key Takeaway
Counter-argument in certain matters inherently contains refutation; in probable matters it does not, and this form shares its essence with counter-argument by inversion.

Another scholar counters with a different qiyas. According to the passage, does this counter-argument reduce to refutation? Explain.

Reduction of Mu'arada in Certainties to Naqd
Author discusses whether mu'arada in certainties reduces to naqd and distinguishes it from opinion-based matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why mu'arada in certainties reduces to naqd, and contrast this with mu'arada in transmitted opinion-based matters like fiqh analogy.

Core Idea
In certainties, a counter-argument is implicitly a refutation; in opinions, it is not.

When two certainties appear to conflict, one must be false. That is why mu'arada in certainties is reducible to naqd, as the author explains. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish certainties from opinion-based matters
Identify what counts as a certainty (qat'iyyat) vs. a transmitted opinion-based matter (naqliyyat). Certainties are logically necessary truths, while transmitted matters are probabilistically known through scripture or analogy.
2
Recognize the impossibility of two contradictory certainties
Understand that if two certain propositions contradict, they cannot both be true in reality. Therefore, one must be false, and the opposing argument serves to expose this falsehood.
This is the core logical reason for the reduction.
3
Understand the explicit-implicit distinction
Note that the author says mu'arada is explicit and naqd is implicit. In certainties, the counter-argument is presented openly, but its refutational force is built-in.
Compare with naqliyyat where both arguments might be plausible.
4
Compare with fiqh analogy
Consider the example: In fiqh, two mujtahids may offer conflicting analogies. Both could be wrong or one right, so mu'arada does not automatically reduce to naqd.
This illustrates the boundary of the reduction.
5
Summarize the reduction
Conclude that in certainties, mu'arada reduces to naqd due to the impossibility of true contradiction; in opinion-based matters, reduction is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل المعارضة في القطعيات راجعة إلى النقض، ويسمى معارضة فيها النقض، دون النقليات، وقيل هو والمعارضة بالقلب أخوان، والتغاير بالاعتبار. الدلائل العقلية والنقلية اليقينية راجعة إلى النقض لامتناع اجتماع القطعيين المتنافيين بحسب نفس الأمر ويسمى المذكور معارضة فيها النقض وإنما سميت معارضة فيها النقض، ولم تسمى نقضا فيها معارضة، لأن المعارضة صريحة، والنقض ضمني والضمنيات لا تعتبر دون النقليات الظنية كالقياس الفقهي، فإنه يجوز أن يكون أحد القاسين خطأ في نفس الأمر، ويعارض القياس الصواب، فلا حاجة إلى القول برجوعه إلى النقض. وقيل هو أي معارضة فيها النقض، وتذكير الضمير لأن المعارضة مصدر، أو بتأويل المذكور والمعارضة بالقلب أخوان أي متشاركان في الماهية والحقيقة والتغاير بينهما لاعتبار. فباعتبار أنها تقلب دليل المستدل شاهدا عليه بعد أن كان شاهدا له قلبا يسمى.
And it was said: Mu'arada in certainties is reducible to naqd, and it is called mu'arada containing naqd, not in transmitted (opinion-based) matters. And it was said: it and mu'arada by conversion are two brothers, and the difference is by consideration. The certain rational and transmitted proofs are reducible to naqd due to the impossibility of two contradictory certainties coexisting in themselves. And the mentioned is called mu'arada containing naqd. It was called mu'arada containing naqd and not called naqd containing mu'arada because the mu'arada is explicit and the naqd is implicit, and implicit things are not considered. Not so in transmitted opinion-based matters like fiqh analogy, for it is possible that one of the two analogizers is mistaken in reality, and he opposes the correct analogy, so there is no need to say it reduces to naqd. And it was said: it, i.e., mu'arada containing naqd (the pronoun is masculine because mu'arada is a verbal noun or by interpretation of the mentioned), and mu'arada by conversion are two brothers, i.e., sharing in essence and reality, and the difference between them is by consideration. Because by consideration that it turns the proof of the proponent into a witness against him after it was a witness for him, it is called conversion.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of mu'arada (counter-argument) in the context of certain knowledge (qat'iyyat). The author begins by stating a view: in certain matters, mu'arada is reducible to naqd (refutation). This means that when two contradictory certainties are presented, one must be false, so the counter-argument effectively refutes the opponent's claim. It is called 'mu'arada containing naqd' because the mu'arada is explicit while the naqd is implicit. In contrast, in opinion-based transmitted matters (like fiqh analogies), mu'arada does not necessarily reduce to naqd because it is possible that both arguments are valid in different respects, or one is mistaken. The author then compares this type of mu'arada with mu'arada by conversion (qalb), stating they are essentially the same in essence but differ in consideration: if the counter-argument turns the opponent's own evidence against him, it is called qalb; if it implicitly refutes, it is mu'arada containing naqd. The explanation uses an Islamic example: fiqh analogy (qiyas fiqhi) where analogies can conflict without necessarily entailing a logical impossibility.

Key Terms
المعارضة
النقض
القلب
القطعيات
النقليات

D) Because transmitted matters require naqd.

Key Takeaway
The reduction of mu'arada to naqd applies only to certainties, not to probable transmitted matters like fiqh analogies.

Mu'arada Containing Naqd and Qalb: Two Sides of the Same Coin
Author compares mu'arada containing naqd with mu'arada by conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mu'arada containing naqd from mu'arada by conversion (qalb) based on perspective.

Core Idea
Mu'arada and qalb share the same essence but differ in how they relate to the opponent's evidence.

The author presents a second opinion that mu'arada containing naqd and mu'arada by conversion are like two brothers, identical in substance but differing from one viewpoint. This lesson unpacks what that means and how to apply the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of counter-argument
Recognize that 'mu'arada containing naqd' is a counter-argument that implicitly refutes the opponent's claim, while 'mu'arada by qalb' turns the opponent's own evidence against him.
2
Understand the common essence
Note that both types share the same essential nature (mahiyya) according to the author. They both aim to invalidate the opponent's position.
The author calls them 'brothers'.
3
Recognize the difference in consideration
The difference is 'by consideration' (i'tibar). From one perspective, if the counter-argument uses the opponent's own proof, it is qalb; if it uses a separate proof that implies refutation, it is mu'arada containing naqd.
This is a conceptual distinction, not a categorical one.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a concrete objection, determine whether it is mu'arada containing naqd or qalb. If the objection is: 'Your premise leads to a contradiction, so your argument fails,' it is qalb if it uses the opponent's premise; if it provides a new argument, it is mu'arada containing naqd.
5
Summarize the relationship
Conclude that the two types are essentially the same kind of opposition, distinguished only by whether the refutation is implicit (mu'arada) or explicit through reversal (qalb).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following: Proponent argues 'The world is eternal because it has no beginning.' Opponent counters 'If the world is eternal, then it has no cause, but everything requires a cause, so your premise leads to contradiction.' Identify whether this is mu'arada containing naqd or qalb, and explain why.

The Four Types of Mu'arada in Jadal
Author enumerates four types of mu'arada involving badaha and dalil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of mu'arada and identify which type is being used in a given dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Mu'arada is a structured counter-argument that uses either self-evidence or proof.

In dialectical debate (jadal), counter-arguments vary. This passage classifies mu'arada into four types based on whether the counter-argument appeals to self-evidence (badaha) or explicit proof (dalil), and whether the opponent's claim is self-evident or supported by proof. Understanding these types sharpens your ability to parse and construct rigorous theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of classification
The passage uses two criteria: the basis of the counter-argument (self-evidence or proof) and the nature of the opponent's claim (self-evident or clarified by proof). This yields four combinations.
The terms used: badaha (self-evidence), dalil (proof), badihi (self-evident), mubayyan bil-dalil (clarified by proof).
2
Match each type to its description
Type 1: mu'arada bi'l-badaha 'ala al-badihi. Type 2: mu'arada bi'l-badaha 'ala al-badihi al-mubayyan bi'd-dalil. Type 3: mu'arada bi'd-dalil 'ala al-badihi. Type 4: mu'arada bi'd-dalil 'ala al-badihi al-mubayyan bi'd-dalil.
Focus on the Arabic phrasing in the passage.
3
Construct an example for each type
For Type 1: Claimant says 'The world is created (badihi)', objector says 'The opposite is also badihi'. For Type 2: Claimant says 'The world is created (badihi) because of change (sensory)', objector says 'The opposite is badihi'. For Type 3: Claimant says 'The world is created (badihi)', objector brings a proof of eternity. For Type 4: Claimant says 'The world is created (badihi) because of change', objector brings a proof of eternity.
These examples are based on the commentary; you can adapt to other theological topics.
4
Recognise the debate structure
In Types 1 and 2, the objector claims self-evidence without proof; in Types 3 and 4, the objector brings a proof. Note the passage mentions some scholars hesitated about the permissibility of certain types.
This awareness helps you evaluate the strength of a mu'arada in actual debate.

تتمة : تردد بعضهم في جواز المعارضة على المعارضة ، والمعارضة بالبداهة ، والدليل على البديهي ، والمبين بالدليل . هذه أربعة أقسام للمعارضة : الأول : المعارضة بالبداهة على البديهي أي على الحكم الذي يدعي المدعي بداهته بأن يقول المعارض ما ادعيتم بداهته يقتضي خلاف بداهته بديهة العقل، فهذه تسمى معارضة باعتبار أن المدعي وإن لم يتعرض لدليل المدعي ، لكن دعوى بداهته بمنزلة إقامة الدليل ، كأنه قال : هذا الحكم ثابت ، لأنه بديهي فيجوز للسائل أن يقول : نقيض هذا الحكم ثابت ، لأنه بديهي. والثاني : المعارضة بالبديهة على البديهي المبين بداهته بالدليل، مثل أن يقول المدعي : هذا الحكم بديهي لأنه من المحسوسات ، فيقول السائل : خلاف هذا الحكم ثابت بالبديهة ، فدعوى الخصم البديهة بمنزلة إقامة الدليل . والثالث : المعارضة بالدليل على الحكم الذي يدعي المدعي بديهته كما إذا قال المدعي : هذا الحكم بديهي ، يقول السائل : لنا دليل يدل على خلافه، ويبين الدليل والرابع : المعارضة بالدليل على الحكم الذي بين المدعي بداهته بالدليل، كما إذا قال المدعي : هذا الحكم من المشاهدات ، يقول السائل : لنا دليل يدل على خلاف هذا الحكم.
Complement: Some of them hesitated regarding the permissibility of mu'arada upon mu'arada, and mu'arada by badaha, and the proof upon the self-evident, and the clarified by proof. These are four divisions of mu'arada: First: Mu'arada by badaha upon the self-evident, i.e., upon the ruling that the claimant claims its self-evidence, by the objector saying: What you claimed its self-evidence necessitates contrary to its self-evidence by the self-evidence of the intellect. So this is called mu'arada because the claimant, although he does not address the proof of the claimant, his claim of self-evidence is like presenting a proof, as if he said: This ruling is established because it is self-evident. Then it is permissible for the questioner to say: The opposite of this ruling is established because it is self-evident. Second: Mu'arada by badaha upon the self-evident that has been clarified as self-evident by proof, like the claimant saying: This ruling is self-evident because it is from sensory perceptions. Then the questioner says: The contrary of this ruling is established by badaha. So the opponent's claim of badaha is like presenting a proof. Third: Mu'arada by proof upon the ruling that the claimant claims is self-evident, as when the claimant says: This ruling is self-evident. The questioner says: We have a proof indicating its contrary, and he explains the proof. Fourth: Mu'arada by proof upon the ruling that the claimant has clarified its self-evidence by proof, as when the claimant says: This ruling is from observed matters. The questioner says: We have a proof indicating the contrary of this ruling.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of four types of mu'arada (counter-argument) in dialectical debate, based on whether the counter-argument uses self-evident (badahi) reasoning or explicit proof (dalil), and whether the opponent's claim is itself self-evident or established by proof. The first type is 'mu'arada by badaha against a self-evident claim': the objector asserts that the opponent's supposed self-evident claim contradicts another self-evident truth. For example, if a claimant says 'This ruling is self-evident,' the objector can respond 'The opposite is also self-evident.' The second type is similar but the opponent's self-evident claim was originally supported by proof (e.g., 'it is self-evident because it is from sensory perception'). The objector still counters with a self-evident opposite. The third type involves the objector bringing a proof against a self-evident claim, and the fourth involves bringing a proof against a claim that the opponent had already justified by proof. The passage highlights that the legitimacy of such mu'arada was debated (some scholars hesitated). An Islamic example: if a claimant says 'It is self-evident that the world is created,' the objector might say 'Self-evidence shows it is eternal' (first type), or if the claimant says 'It is self-evident from observation that the sun rises,' the objector might say 'The opposite is also self-evident' (second type). These distinctions refine the art of jadal.

البديهي
المبين بالدليل

Key Takeaway
The four types of mu'arada refine dialectical debate by specifying whether the counter-argument uses self-evidence or proof, and whether the opponent's claim is self-evident or proven.

Five Types of Mu'aarada and Their Permissibility
Author enumerates five types of counter-argument and discusses the dispute over their permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five types of mu'aarada and evaluate the arguments for and against their permissibility in dialectical debate.

Core Idea
The five types of mu'aarada clarify the levels at which counter-arguments can be launched, and the debate over their legitimacy reveals key principles of dialectical fairness.

In this passage, the author presents a full taxonomy of mu'aarada, from opposing self-evidence with self-evidence to opposing an evidenced judgment with counter-evidence, and even countering a counter-argument. After listing them, he reports that some scholars rejected all five as fruitless or improper, but he affirms their permissibility. This lesson will guide you through each type and the reasoning behind the dispute, enhancing your ability to use and evaluate counter-arguments in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types
Locate the four initial types and the fifth type added later. For each, note what is being countered (a self-evident claim or an evidenced judgment) and what instrument is used (self-evidence or evidence).
2
Understand the rationale for each type
For each type, consider the opponent's action: is he denying a claim of self-evidence? Offering a different proof? The classification helps clarify the dialectical moves.
3
Examine the objections
The passage lists objections to each type. For each objection, identify the reason why the type is considered impermissible: e.g., first type is 'not beneficial' because evidence collapses; second type has no evidence on either side.
4
Assess the author's response
The author says 'the correct view is its permissibility.' The commentary hints that the first type is permissible because the second counter-argument does not cause the first evidence to fail. Reflect on why each objection might fail.
The full defense is in the commentary continuation on page 71.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Take a real jadal scenario from usul al-fiqh or kalam and see which type of mu'aarada would be used. For instance, if a claimant says 'The world is temporal because it changes' and the opponent says 'The world is eternal because it is perfect,' that might be a mu'aarada with evidence against an evidenced claim.

ثم يضاف إلى الأربعة المتقدمة نوع خامس وهو المعارضة على المعارضة.

أما الأول : فلأنه غير نافع، لأنه إذا استدل المدعي على المطلوب بأدلة كثيرة ، والخصم استدل على نقيضه بدليل واحد وسقطت تلك الدلائل بهذا الدليل ولا يثبت شيء من الطرفين. وأما الثاني : فلأنه لا دليل في شيء من الجانبين. وكذا الثالث : لأن الدليل الذي أقامه المدعي إنما ينتهض على دعوى البداهة لا على الحكم . وأما الرابع فلأنه لا دليل في جانب المدعي ، وكذا الخامس : لما ذكرنا انتهاض الدليل على دعوى البداهة لا على الحكم .

A supplement: Some of them hesitated regarding the permissibility of counter-argument against counter-argument, and counter-argument with self-evidence, and evidence against the self-evident, and that which is clarified by evidence. These are four types of counter-argument: First: counter-argument with self-evidence against the self-evident, i.e., against the judgment that the claimant claims to be self-evident, by the opponent saying: 'What you claimed to be self-evident requires contrary to its self-evidence by the self-evidence of the intellect.' This is called counter-argument because the claimant, even if he does not produce evidence for his claim, his claim of self-evidence is in the position of adducing evidence, as if he said: 'This judgment is established because it is self-evident.' Then it is permissible for the questioner to say: 'The opposite of this judgment is established because it is self-evident.' Second: counter-argument with self-evidence against the self-evident that has been clarified by evidence, such as the claimant saying: 'This judgment is self-evident because it is from sense-perceptions,' then the questioner says: 'The opposite of this judgment is established by self-evidence.' So the opponent's claim of self-evidence is in the position of adducing evidence. Third: counter-argument with evidence against the judgment whose self-evidence the claimant claims, as when the claimant says: 'This judgment is self-evident,' the questioner says: 'We have evidence indicating its opposite,' and he presents the evidence. Fourth: counter-argument with evidence against the judgment whose self-evidence the claimant has clarified by evidence, as when the claimant says: 'This judgment is from direct observations,' the questioner says: 'We have evidence indicating the opposite of this judgment.' Then to the preceding four is added a fifth type, which is counter-argument against counter-argument. So these five types of counter-argument some hesitated regarding their permissibility and said: they are not permissible. As for the first: because it is not beneficial, for if the claimant adduces many evidences for what is sought, and the opponent adduces a single evidence for its opposite, those evidences fall due to this one evidence and nothing is established from either side. As for the second: because there is no evidence on either side. Similarly, the third: because the evidence that the claimant brought only applies to the claim of self-evidence, not to the judgment. As for the fourth: because there is no evidence on the claimant's side. Similarly, the fifth: for what we mentioned, that the evidence applies to the claim of self-evidence, not to the judgment. But the correct view is its permissibility.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Rashidiyya discusses the classification of mu'aarada (counter-argument) in dialectical debate (jadal). The author begins by noting that some scholars hesitated about the permissibility of certain types of counter-argument. He then enumerates four initial types, later adding a fifth. 

The first type is counter-argument with self-evidence (badaha) against a claim that is said to be self-evident. Here, the opponent counters by asserting that the opposite of the claimed self-evident judgment is itself self-evident. The second type is similar, but the claimant had supported his claim of self-evidence with evidence (e.g., 'it is from sense-perceptions'), and the opponent still counters with a claim of self-evidence for the opposite. The third type involves the opponent producing evidence (not just self-evidence) against a judgment that the claimant merely claimed to be self-evident, without any supporting evidence. The fourth type is when the claimant had already provided evidence clarifying the self-evidence (e.g., 'it is from direct observation'), and the opponent then produces counter-evidence. The fifth type is counter-argument against counter-argument itself – i.e., a chain of mu'aarada.

The text then outlines the reasons why some scholars declared these impermissible. For the first type, they argued it is fruitless: if the claimant brings many proofs and the opponent brings one counter-proof, all proofs collapse and nothing is settled. For the second, there is no actual proof on either side, only competing claims of self-evidence. For the third, the claimant's 'evidence' only addressed the claim of self-evidence, not the underlying judgment. For the fourth, the claimant has no real evidence. For the fifth, again the counter-argument only attacks the claim of self-evidence, not the judgment itself. 

Despite these objections, the author affirms that the correct view is permissibility. The commentary (which continues on page 71) explains that the first type is permissible because the opponent's second counter-argument leaves the claimant's first evidence untouched – thus not all is lost. The author encourages careful reflection to see the permissibility of all five types. This passage illustrates the subtlety of dialectical strategy in classical Islamic logic, where counter-arguments are carefully categorized and evaluated.

Key Terms
المعارضة
البداهة
البديهي

B) Counter-argument with self-evidence against the self-evident clarified by evidence
C) Counter-argument with evidence against the judgment claimed to be self-evident
D) Counter-argument with evidence against the judgment clarified by evidence

Key Takeaway
The five types of mu'aarada show that counter-arguments can target either the self-evident claim or the evidence, and despite objections, the correct view permits them, enhancing dialectical depth.

The Five Types of Mu'arada and Their Permissibility
Author enumerates five types of mu'arada and affirms their permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of mu'arada (counterargument) in munazara and evaluate the arguments for and against their permissibility.

Core Idea
Mu'arada is permissible in all five forms because each involves valid logical opposition.

In the discipline of munazara (dialectical debate), a key technique is mu'arada, where the opponent presents a counterargument against the claimant's proof. However, some scholars questioned whether certain forms of mu'arada are permissible. This passage from a classical jadal text enumerates five types and resolves the doubts, ultimately affirming their permissibility. We will analyze the objections and the responses to understand the logical structure of dialectical opposition.

Guided Path
1
List the five types of mu'arada
From the passage, identify each type as described by the author and the commentator. Note the differences between types based on whether proofs are multiple or single, and whether the claim is self-evident or not.
2
Extract the objections against each type
For each type, find the specific reason given by scholars who considered mu'arada impermissible. For example, the first type is objected to because it leaves both sides without established proof.
The objections are about apparent lack of benefit or proof.
3
Analyze the resolution for each type
The commentator and author show why each objection fails. For the first type, the opponent's proof only counters one of the claimant's proofs, leaving the others intact. For the second, the opponent's claim of self-evidence acts as a proof itself.
The resolution relies on recognizing that each type still involves a valid argumentative move.
4
Understand the added fifth type
The passage adds a fifth type: mu'arada against mu'arada. Consider why this also faced hesitation and how the example of rational vs. transmitted proof illustrates the principle.
This type shows the recursive nature of dialectic.
5
Connect to broader jadal principles
Mu'arada is a tool for fairness in debate. Understanding its permissibility ensures that both sides have equal opportunities to counter arguments. Reflect on how this principle applies in theological and legal debates.
This reinforces the adab al-bahth tradition.

These are the five divisions of mu'arada; some scholars hesitated regarding its permissibility and said: they are not permissible. As for the first: because it is not beneficial, for if the claimant argues for the desired conclusion with many proofs, and the opponent argues for its negation with one proof, and those proofs are defeated by this one proof, nothing from either side is established. As for the second: because there is no proof on either side. Similarly the third: because the proof that the claimant establishes only applies to the claim of self-evidence, not to the ruling. As for the fourth: because there is no proof on the claimant's side, and similarly the fifth: because of what we mentioned that the proof applies to the claim of self-evidence, not to the ruling. And if you reflect on what we mentioned of the detailing of the five divisions, the aspect of their permissibility becomes apparent: the aspect of the permissibility of the first: it is that when the second proof of the reasoner is countered by a proof from the objector, his first proof remains safe from objection; so reflect well so that the truth becomes clear to you. And the truth is its permissibility, i.e., permissibility of what some hesitated about. We said previously that some hesitated regarding the permissibility of mu'arada with the five types mentioned. And here the author said: the truth is its permissibility, i.e., permissibility of mu'arada with the five types mentioned. And the commentator explained the aspect of those who said it is not permissible in each type of the five, then mentioned that it is possible to show their permissibility. Then he mentioned only the aspect of permissibility of the first and left the rest, but I say: as for the second, which is mu'arada of self-evidence with self-evidence, it is because the claim of self-evidence from the objector is like a proof for him. As for the third, which is mu'arada of the sought conclusion whose self-evidence is explained by a proof with self-evidence, it is also like a proof from the objector. As for the fourth, it is because the objector establishes a proof for the self-evidence of the ruling that contradicts the ruling of the reasoner who claims the self-evidence of his ruling. As for the fifth, which is mu'arada with a proof in a self-evident matter where the opponent claims its contrary and it is also self-evident, by a proof. So you see that mu'arada is actualized in the five mentioned types. Then to the four preceding types is added a fifth type: mu'arada against mu'arada. Some also hesitated about its permissibility like the preceding four. And from this they claimed that when self-evident is countered by a demonstrative proof, that is more deserving of consideration, like transmitted proof with rational proof, unless the transmitted proof yields certainty.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the five types of mu'arada (counterargument) in munazara and the debate over their permissibility. The author begins by stating that some scholars hesitated about the permissibility of these five types and considered them not permissible. He then lists the objections to each type: the first type (countering multiple proofs with one) is said to be useless because both sides are defeated; the second (both sides lacking proof) leaves no argument; the third (countering a proof for a self-evident ruling with another self-evident claim) fails because the proof addresses the claim of self-evidence, not the ruling itself; the fourth (countering a self-evident ruling with a proof for its opposite) leaves the claimant with no proof; and the fifth (countering a self-evident claim with a proof for its opposite) similarly fails. However, the author then invites reflection to see the aspect of permissibility. He explains the permissibility of the first: when the objector's proof counters the reasoner's second proof, the first proof remains untouched. After presenting the commentator's explanation, the author adds his own reasoning for the remaining types: in the second, the objector's claim of self-evidence is itself a proof; in the third, the objector's use of self-evidence against a proof is also a proof; in the fourth, the objector establishes a proof for the self-evidence of a contradictory ruling; in the fifth, both sides use proofs for self-evident claims, so mu'arada is actualized. Then a fifth type is added: countering the counterargument itself, which also faced hesitation. Finally, the author likens countering a self-evident claim with a demonstrative proof to countering a transmitted proof with a rational one, except when the transmitted proof is certain. This passage clarifies that despite initial objections, all forms of mu'arada are permissible in dialectical debate, as each involves a valid logical opposition.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best illustrates the first type of mu'arada (countering multiple proofs with one)?
A) A Hanafi argues that wiping a part of the head is sufficient, and the Shafi'i counters with multiple proofs for complete wiping.
B) A claimant brings three proofs for the obligation of fasting, and the opponent brings one proof for its non-obligation, defeating all three.
C) Both sides bring proofs for the same claim and then object to each other's proofs.
D) One side claims 'this is self-evident' and the other brings a demonstrative proof against it.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is permissible in all five forms because each type involves a valid logical opposition that prevents either side from being left without argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify which type of mu'arada is involved in the following exchange: A claimant argues that the world is created (huduth) using multiple proofs, and the opponent counters with a single proof that the world is eternal (qidam). How does the passage analyze this scenario?
?
2. Construct a brief munazara exchange involving the fifth type (mu'arada against mu'arada) on a theological or legal issue, ensuring that both sides use legitimate forms of counterargument.
